A wood thick grown with trees which stood vnfelled to that day
Beginning from a plain, had thence a large prospect throughout
The falling grounds that every way did muster round about.
As Soon as that the men came there, some pitched up the toils,
Some took the couples from the Dogs, and some pursude the foyles
In places where the Swine had tract: desiring for to spy
Their own destruction. Now there was a hollow bottom by,
To which the watershots of rain from all the high grounds drew.
Within the compass of this pond great store of Oysyers grew:
And Sallowes lithe, and flackring Flags, and moorish Rushes eke,
And lazy Reeds on little shankes, and other baggage like.
From hence the Bore was rowzed out, and fiersly forth he flies
Among the thickest of his foes like thunder from the Skies,
When Clouds in meeting force the fire to burst by violence out.
He bears the trees before him down, and all the wood about
Does sound of crashing. All the youth with hideous noise and shout
Against him bend their Boarspeare points with hand and courage stout.
He rushes forth among the Dogs that held him at a bay,
And now on this side now on that, as any come in way,
He rippes their skins and splitteth them, and chaseth the^ away,
Echion first of all the rout a Dart at him did throw,
Which mist and in a Maple tree did give a little blow
The next (if he that threw the same had used lesser might,)
The back at which he aimed it was likely for to smight.
It ouerflew him. Jason was the man that cast the Dart.
With that the son of Ampycus said: Mopsus.
Phoebus (if with heart
I have and still do worship thee) now grant me for to hit
The thing that I do level at. Apollo graunts him it
As much as lay in him to grant. He hit the Swine in deed.
But neither entered he his hide nor caused him to bleed.
For why Diana (as the Dart was flying) took away
The head of it: and so the Dart could headless bear no sway.
But yet the moodie beast thereby was set the more on fire
And cha^ing like the lightning swift he vttreth forth his ire.
The fire did sparkle from his eyes: and from his boiling breast
He breathed flaming flakes of fire conceived in his chest.
And look with what a violent brunt a mighty Bullet goes
From engines bent against a wall, or bulwarks full of foes:
With even such violence rusht the Swine among the Hunts a main,
And overthrew Eupalamon and Pelagon both twain
That in the right wing placed were. Their follows stepping to
And drawing them away, did save their lives with much a do.
But as for poor Enesimus Hippocoons son had not
The luck to escape the deadly dint. He would away have got,
And trembling turnde his back for fear. The Swine him ouertooke,
And cut his hamstrings, so that straight his going him forsook.
And Naestor to have lost his life was like by fortune ere
The siege of Troie, but that he took his rist upon his spear:
And leaping quickly up upon a tree that stood hard by,
Did safely from the place behold his foe whom he did fly.
The Boar then whetting sharp his tuskes against the Oken wood
To mischief did prepare himself with fierce and cruel mood.
And trusting to his weapons which he sharpened had a new,
In great Orithyas thigh a wound with hooked groyne he drew.
The valiant brothers those same twins of Tyndarus (not yet
Celestial signs) did both of them on coursers sit
As white as snow: Castor and Pollux.
and each of them had shaking in his fist
A lightsome Dart with head of steel to throw it where he list.
And for to wound the bristled Bore they surely had not mist
But that he still recovered so the couerts of the wood,
That neither horse could follow him, nor Dart do any good.
Still after followed Telamon, whom taking to his feet
No heed at all for egernesse, a Maple root did meet,
Which tripped up his heels, and flat against the ground him laid
And while his brother Peleus relieved him, the Maid
Of Tegea took an arrow swift, and shot it from her bow.
The arrow lighting underneath the hauers ear bylow,
And somewhat rasing of the skin, did make the blood to show.
The Maid hirselfe not gladder was to see that luckie blow,
Than was the Prince Meleager. He was the first that saw,
And first that showed to his Mates the blud that she did draw:
And said for this thy valiant act due honor shall thou have.
The men did blush, and chearing up each other courage gave.
With shouting, and disorderly their Darts by beaps they threw.
The number of them hindered them, not suffering to ensew
That any lighted on the mark at which they all did ame.
Behold, enragde against his end the hardy Knight that came
From Arcadie, rusht rashly with a Pollar in his fist
And said you yonglings learn of me what difference is betwist
A wenches weapons and a man's: and all of you give place
To my redoubted force. For though Diana in this chase
Should with her own shield him defend, yet should this hand of mine
Even ma^gre Dame Dianaas heart confound this orped Swine.
Such boasting words as these through pride presumptuously he crakes:
And streyning out himself upon his ^iptoes straight he takes
His Pollar up with both his hands. But as this bragger meant
To fetch his blow, the cruel beast his malice did prevent:
And in his coddes (the sp^^ding place of death) his tusshes puts,
And rippeth up his paunche. Down falls Ancaeus and his guts
Come tumbling out besmearde with blood, and foiled all the plot.
Pirithous lxions son at that abashed not:
But shaking in his valiant hand his hunting staff did go
Still stoutly forward face to face t'encounter with his foe
To whom Duke Theseus cride a far. O dearer unto me
Than is my self, my soul I say, stay: lawful we it see
For valiant men to keep aloof. The over hardy heart
In rash aduentring of him self has made Ancaeus smart.
This sed, he threw a weighty Dart of Cornell with a head
Of brass: which being leueld well was likely to have sped,
But that a bough of Chestnut tree thick leaued by the way
Did latch it, and by means thereof the dint of it did stay.
Another Dart that Jason threw, by fortune mist the Bore,
And light between a Mastifes chaps, and through his guts did gore,
And naild him to the earth. The hand of Prince Meleager
Played hittymissie. Of two Darts his first did fly to far,
And lighted in the ground: the next amid his back stickt fast.
And while the Bore did play the fiend and turned round aghast,
And grunting flang his foam about together mixed m^^h blood
The giver of the wound (the more to stir his enmies mood,)
Stepped in, and underneath the shield did thrust his Boarspeare through.
Then all the Hunters shouting out demeaned joy enough.
And glad was he that first might come to take him by the hand.
About the ugly beast they all with gladness gazing stand
And wondering what a field of ground his carcass did possess,
There durst not any be so bold to touch him. Nerethelesse,
They every of them with his blood their hunting staves made red.
Then stepped forth Meleager, and treading on his head
Said thus: O Lady Atalant, receive thou here my fee,
And of my glory vouch thou safe partaker for to be.
Immediately the ugly head with both the tusshes brave
And eke the skin with bristles stur right griesly, he her gave.
The Lady for the giuers sake, was in her heart as glad
As for the gift. The rest repinde that she such honor had.
Through all the rout was murmuring. Of whom with roaring rear
And arms displayed that all the field might easily see and hear,
The Thesties cried Dame come of and lay us down this gear.
And thou a woman offer not us men so great a shame,
As we to toil and thou to take the honor of our game.
Ne let that fair smooth face of thine beguile thee, least that he
That being doted in thy love did give thee this our fee,
Be over far to rescow thee. And with that word they took
The gift from her, and right of gift from him. He could not brook
This wrong: but gnashing with his teeth for anger that did boil
Within, said fiersly: learn ye you that other folks dispoyle
Of honor given, what diffrence is between your threats, and deeds,
And therewithal Plexippus breast (who no such matter dreedes)
With wicked weapon he did pierce. As Toxey doubting stood
What way to take, desiring both t'aduenge his brothers blood,
And fearing to be murdered as his brother was before,
Meleager (to dispatch all doubts of musing any more)
Did heat his sword for company in blood of him again,
Before Plexippus blud was cold that did thereon remain.
Althaea going toward Church with presents for to yild
Due thanks and worship to the Gods that for her son had killed
The Boar, beheld her brothers brought home dead: and by and by
She beat her breast, and filde the town with shrieking piteously,
And shifting all her rich array, did put on mourning weed
But when she understood what man was doer of the deed,
She left all mourning, and from tears to vengeance did proceed.
There was a certain firebrand which when Oenies wife did lie
In childebed of Meleager, she chanced to epsy
The Destnies putting in the fire: and in the putting in,
She heard them speak these words, as they his fatal threede did spin
O lately borne, like time we give to thee and to this brand.
And when they so had spoken, they depar^ed out of hand.
Immediately the mother caught the blazing bough away,
And quenched it. This bough she kept full charely many a day:
And in the keeping of the same she kept her son alive.
But now intending of his life him clearly to deprive,
She brought it forth, and causing all the coals and shiuers to
Be laid by, she like a foe did kindle fire thereto.
Four times she was about to cast the firebrand in the flame:
Four times she pulled back her hand from doing of the same.
As mother and as sister both she strove what way to go:
The diverse names drew diversely her stomach to and fro.
Her face waxt often pale for fear of mischief to ensue:
And often red about the eyes through heat of ire she grew.
One while her look resembled one that threatened cruelnesse:
Another while ye would have thought she minded pitiousnesse.
And though the cruel burning of her heart did dry her tears,
Yet burst out some. And as a Boat which tide contrary bears
Against the wind, feels double force, and is compeld to yield
To both. So Thesties daughter now unable for to weelde
Her doubtful passions, diversely is carried of and on,
And chaungeably she waxes calm, and storms again anon.
But better sister ginneth she than mother for to be:
And to the intent her brothers ghostes with blood to honor, she
In meaning to be one way kind, does work another way
Against kind. When the plagie fire waxt strong she thus did say
Let this same fire my bowels burn. And as in cursed hands
The fatal wood she holding at the Hellish Altar stands:
She said ye triple Goddesses of wreake, ye H^lhounds three
Behold ye all this furious fact and sacrifice of me.
I wreake, and do against all right: with death must death be paid:
On mischief mischief must be heapt: on corse must corse be laid.
Confounded let this wicked house with heaped sorrows be
Shall Oenie joy his happy son in honor for to see
And Thestie mourn bereft of his? Nay: better yet it were,
That each with other company in mourning you should bear.
Ye brothers Ghostes and souls new dead I wish no more, but you
To feel the solemn obsequies which I prepare as now:
And that mine offering you accept, which dearly I have bought
The issue of my wretched womb. Alas, alas what thought
I for to do? O brothers I beseech you bear with me.
I am his mother: so to do my hands unable be
His trespass I confess deserves the stopping of his breath:
But yet I do not like that I be Author of his death.
And shall he then with life and limme, and honor to, escape free?
And vaunting in his good success the King of Calidon be?
And you dear souls (...) raked up but in a little dust?
I will not surely suffer it. But let the villain trust
That he shall die, and draw with him to ruin and decay
His Kingdom, Country and his Sire that does upon him stay.
Why where is now the mothers heart and pity that should reign
In Parents? and the ten Months pains that once I did sustain?
O would to God thou burned had a babie in this brand,
And that I had not taken it out and quenched it with my hand.
That all this while thou lived have, my goodness is the cause.
And now most justly unto death thine own desert thee draws.
Receive the guer^on of thy deed: and render thou again
Thy twice given life, by bearing first, and secondarly when
I caught this firebrand from the flame: or else come deal with me
As with my brothers, and with them let me entumbed be.
I would, and cannot. What then shall I stand to in this case?
One while my brothers corses seem to prease before my face
With lively Image of their deaths. Another while my mind
Does yield to pity, and the name of mother does me blind
No^ woe is me. To let you have the upper hand is sin:
But ne^ethelesse the upper hand O brothers do you win*
Cond^cio^ly that when that I to comfort you withal.
Have wrought this feate, my self to you resort in person shall.
This sed, she turnde away her face, and with a trembling hand
Did cast the deathfull brand amid the burning fire. The brand
Did either sigh, or seem to sigh in burning in the flame,
Which sorry and unwilling was to fasten on the same.
Meleager being absent and not knowing ought at all
Was burned with this flame: and felt his bowels to appall
With secret fire. He bare out long the pain with courage stout.
But yet it grieved him to die so cowardly without
The shedding of his blood. He thought Anceus for to be
A happy man that did of wound. With sighing called he
Upon his aged father, and his sisters, and his brother,
And lastly on his wife to, and by chance upon his mother.
His pain increased with the fire, and fell therewith again:
And at the self same instant quight extinguished were both twain.
And as the ashes soft and hoar by leisure ouergrew
The glowing coals: so leysurely his spirit from him drew.
The drouped stately Calydon. Both young and old did mourn
The Lords and Commons did lament. and married wives with torn
And tattred hair did cry alas. His father did beray
His horie head and face with dust, and on the earth flat lay,
Lamenting that he lived had to see that woeful day
For now his mothers guilty hand had for that cursed crime
Done execution on her self by sword before her time.
If God to me a hundred mouths with sounding tongues should send,
And reason able to conceive, and thereunto should lend
Me all the grace of eloquence that ere the Muses had,
I could not show the woe wherewith his sisters were bestad.
Unmindfull of their high estate, their naked breasts they smit,
Until they made them black and blew. And while his body yet
Remained, they did cherish it, and cherish it again.
They kissed his body: yea they kissed the chist that did contain
His corse. And after that the corse was burnt to ashes, they
Did press his ashes with their breasts: and down along they lay
Upon his tumb, and there embraste his name upon the stone,
And fillde the letters of the same with tears that from them gone.
At length Diana satisfide with slaughter brought upon
The house of Oenie, lifts them up with f^thers euerichone,
(Save Gorgee and the daughtriulaw of noble Al^mene) and
Makes wings to stretch along their sides, and horned nebs to stand.
Upon their mouths. And finally she altring quight their fair
And native shape, in shape of Birds does send them through the Air.
The noble Theseus in this while with others having done
His part in killing of the Boar, too Athens ward begone
Too take his way. But Acheloy then being swollen with rain
Did stay him of his journey, and from passage him restrain.
Of Athens valiant knight (quoth he) come underneath my roof,
And for to pass my raging stream as yet attempt no proof.
This brook is wont whole trees too bear and euelong stones too carry
With hideous roaring down his stream. I often have seen him harry
Whole Shepcotes standing nere his banks, with flocks of sheep therein.
Naught booted bulls their strength: naught steedes by swiftnes there could win,
Yea many lusty men this brook has swallowed, when y^ snow
From mountains molten, caused him his banks too ouerflow.
The best is for you for too rest until the River fall
Within his bounds: and run ageine within his channel small.
Content (quoth Theseus): Acheloy, I will not sure refuse
Thy counsel nor thy house. And so he both of them did use.
Of Pommy hollowed diversely and ragged Pebble stone
The walls were made. The floor with Moss was soft to tread upon.
The roof thereof was checkerwise with shelles of Purple wrought
And Perle. The Sun then full two parts of day to end had brought,
And Theseus down to table sat with such as late before
Had friendly borne him company at killing of the Bore.
A tone side sat Ixions son, and on the other sat
The Prince of Troyzen Lelex with a thin heard horie pate.
And then such other as the brook of Acarnania did
Uouchsafe the honor to his board and table for to bid,
Who was right glad of such a guest. Immediately there came
Barefooted Nymphs who brought in meat. And when that of the same
The Lords had taken their repast, the meat away they took,
And set down wine in precious stones. Then Theseus who did look
Upon the Sea that underneath did lie within their sight,
Said: tell us what is yonsame place, (and with his fingar right
He poynted thereunto) I pray, and what that Island hight,
Although it seems mo than one. The River answered thus,
It is not one main land alone that kenned is of us.
There are upon a five of them. The distaunce of the place,
Does hinder too discern between each I Will the perfect space.
And that the less ye wonder may at Phoebees act a late,
To such as had neglected her upon contempt or hate,
These Isles were sumtyme Waternimphes: who having killed Neat,
Twyce five, and called too their feast the Country Gods too eat,
Forgetting me kept frolic cheer. At that began I too swell,
And ran more large than ever erst, and being over fell
I^stomacke and in stream, I rent the wood from wood, and feeld
From feeld, and with the ground the Nymphs as then with stomachs meeld
Remembering me, I tumbled to the Sea. The waves of me
And of the sea the ground that erst all whole was wont too be
Did rend a sunder into all the Isles you yonder see,
And made a way for waters now too pass between them free.
They now of Vrchins have their name. But of these Islands, one
A great way of (behold ye) stands a great way of alone,
As you may see. The Mariners do call it Perimell.
With her (she was as then a Nymph) so far in love I fell,
That of her maydenhod I her spoyld: which thing displeasd so sore
Her father Sir Hippodamas, that from the craggy shore
He threw her headlong down to drown her in the sea. But I
Did latch her straight, and bearing her a flote did loud thus cry.
O Neptune with thy threetynde Mace who have by lot the charge
Of all the waters wild that bound upon the earth at large,
To whom we holy streams do run, in whom we take our end,
Draw near, and gently to my boon effectually attend.
This Lady whom I bear a flote myself has hurt. Be meek
And upright. If Hippodamas perchance were fatherleeke,
Or if that he extremity through outrage did not seek,
He oughted too have pitied her and for too bear with me.
Now help us Neptune I thee pray, and condescend that she
Whom from the land her fathers wrath and cruelnesse does chase
Who through her fathers cruelnesse is drowned: may find the grace
To have a place: or rather let hirselfe become a place.
And I will still embrace the same. The King of Seas did move
His head, and as a token that he did my suit approve,
He made his surges all too shake, The Nymph was sore afraid.
Howbeet she swam, and as she swam, my hand I softly laid
Upon her breast which quiuered still. And while I touched the same,
I sensibly did feel how all her body hard became:
And how the earth did ouergrow her bulk. And as I spoke,
New earth enclosde her swimming limbs, which by and by did take
Another shape, and grew intoo a mighty I Will. With that
The River ceast and all men there did wonder much thereat.
Pirithous being over hault of mind and such a one
As did despise both God and man, did laugh them euerychone
Too scorn for giving credit, and said thus. The words thou spaakst
Are feyned fancies Acheloy: and ouerstrong thou maakst
The Gods: to say that they can give and take way shapes. This scoff
Did make the heeres all amazde, for none did like thereof.
And Lelex of them all the man most ripe in years and wit,
Said thus. Unmeasurable is the power of heaven, and it
Can have none end. And look what God does mind too bring about,
Must take effect. And in this case too put ye out of dout,
Upon the hills of Phrygie near of Teyle there stands a tree
Of Oak enclosed with a wall. Myself the place did see.
For Pithey unto Pelops feelds did send me where his father
Did sumtyme reign. not far fro thence there is a poole which rather
Had been dry ground inhabited. But now it is a meare
And Moorecocks, Cootes, and Cormorants do breed and nestle there.
The mighty Jove and Mercury his son in shape of men
Resorted thither on a time. A thousand houses when
For room too lodge in they had sought, a thousand houses bard
Their doors against them. Nerethelesse one Cotage afterward
Received them, and that was but a pelting one in deed.
The roof thereof was thatched all with straw and fennish reed.
Howbeet two honest ancient folk, (of whom she Baucis hight
And he Philemon) in that Cote their faith in youth had plight:
And in that Cote had spent their age. And for they patiently
Did bear their simple poverty, they made it light thereby,
And showed it no thing to be repyned at atall.
It skilles not whether there for Hyndes or Master you do call.
For all the household were but two: and both of them obeyde,
And both commanded. When the Gods at this same Cotage staid,
And ducking down their heads, within the low made Wicket came,
Philemon bringing each a stool, bade rest upon the same
Their limbs: and busy Baucis brought them quishons homely geere.
Which done, the embers on the harth she began abroad to steer,
And laid the coals together that were raak^ up over night,
And with the brands and dried leaves did make them gather might,
And with the blowing of her mouth did make them kindle bright.
Then from an inner house she fetched sear sticks and clifted brands,
And put them broken underneath a Skillet with her hands.
Her Husband from their Gardenplot fetched Coleworts. Of the which
She shreaded small the leaves, and with a Forke took down a flitch
Of restie Bacon from the Balke made black with smoke, and cut
A piece thereof, and in the pan to boiling did it put.
And while this meat a seething was, the time in talk they spent,
By means whereof away without much tedousnesse it went.
There hung a Boawle of Beeche upon a spirget by a ring.
The same with warmed water filld the two old folk did bring
Too bathe their guests foul feet therein. Amid the house there stood
A Couch whose bottom sides and feet were all of Sallow wood,
And on the same a Mat of Sedge. They cast upon this bed
A covering which was never wont upon it too be spread
Except it were at solemn feasts: and yet the same was old
And of the coursest, with a bed of sallow meet too hold.
The Gods sat down. The aged wife right chare and busy as
A Be, s^t out a table. of the which the third foot was
A little shorter than the rest. A tylesh^rd made it even
And took away the shormgnesse: and when they had it driven
To stand up level, with green Mintes they by and by it wipte.
Then set they on it Olyss.
Pallas fruit with double color stripte.
And Cornels kept in pickle moist, and Endive, and a root
Of Radish, and a jolly lump of Butter fresh and ^oote,
And Eggs rear roasted. All these Eats in earthen dishes came.
Then set they down a graven cup made also of the same
Self kind of Plate, and Mazers made of Beech whose inner side
Was rubd with yellow wax. And when they pawsed had a tide,
Whote meat came pyping from the fire. And shortly thereupon
A cup of green hedge wine was brought. This taken away, anon
Came in the latter course, which was of Nuts, Dates, dried figs
Sweet smelling Apples in a Mawnd made flat of Oysyer twigges,
And Prunes and Plums and Purple grapes cut newly from the tree,
And in the mids a honnycomb new taken from the Be.
Besides all this there did ensew good countnance ouermore,
With will not poor nor nigardly. Now all the while before,
As ofen as Philemon and Dame Baucis did perceive
The empty Cup to fill alone, and wine too still receive,
Amazed at the strangeness of the thing, they began straight way
With fearful hearts and hands hilld up too frame themselves too pray.
Desyring for their slend^r cheer and fare too pardoned be.
They had but one (...) Goose which kept their little Tennantree,
And this too offer too the Gods their guestes they did intend.
The Gander wight of wing did make the slow old folk too spend
Their pains in vain, and mokt them long. At length he seemd too fly
For succor too the Gods themselves, who bade he should not dye.
For we be Gods (quoth they) and all this wicked towneship shall
Abye their gylt. On you alone this mischeef shall not fall.
No more but give you up your house, and follow up this hill
Toogither, and upon the top thereof abide our will.
They both obeyed. And as the Gods did lead the way before,
They lagged slowly after with their staves, and labored sore
Against the rising of the hill. They were not mickle more
Than full a flyghtshot from the top, when looking back they saw
How all the town was drowned save their little shed of straw.
And as they woondred at the thing and did bewail the case
Of those that had their neighbors been, the old poor Cote so base
Whereof they had been owners erst, became a Church. The proppes
Were turned into pillars howge. The straw upon the tops
Was yellow, so that all the roof did seem of burnisht gold:
The floor with Marble paued was. The doors on either fold
Were graven. At the sight hereof Philemon and his make
Began too pray in fear. Then Jove thus gently them bespake.
Declare thou ryghtuowse man, and thou O woman meet too have
A ryghtuowse howsband what ye would most chiefly wish or crave.
Philemon taking conference a little with his wife,
Declared both their meenings thus. We covet during life,
Your Chapleynes for too be too keep your Temple. And because
Our years in concord we have spent, I pray when death near draws,
Let both of us toogither leave our lives: that neither I
Behold my wives deceace, nor she see mine when I do dye.
Their wish had sequel to their will. As long as life did last,
They kept the Church. And being spent with age of years forepast,
By chance as standing on a time without the Temple door
They told the fortune of the place, Philemon old and poor
Saw Baucis floorish green with leaves, and Baucis saw likewise
Philemon braunching out in boughs and twigs before her eyes.
And as the Bark did ouergrow the heads of both, each spoke
Too other while they might. At last they each of them did take
Their leave of other both at once, and therewithal the bark
Did hyde their faces both at once. The Phrygians in that park
Do at this present day still show the trees that shaped were
Of their two bodies, growing yet together jointly there.
These things did ancient men report of credit very good.
For why there was no cause why they should lie. As I there stood
I saw the garlands hanging on the bo^ghes, and adding new
I said let them whom God does love be Gods. and honor dew
Be given to such as honor him with fear and r^uerence true.
He ^^lld his peace, and both the thing and he that did it tell
Did move them all, but Theseus most. Whom being minded well
To here of woondrous things, the brook of Calydon thus bespake.
There are O valiant knight sum folk that had the power too take
Strange shape for once, and all their lives continewed in the same.
And othersum too sundry shapes have power themselves to frame.
As thou O Protevv dwelling in the sea that cleepes the land.
For now a yoonker, now a boar, anon a Lion, and
Straight way thou did become a Snake, and by and by a Bull
That people were afraid of thee too see thy horned skull.
And oftentimes thou seemde a stone, and now and then a tree,
And counterfetting water sheere thou seemedst ott to be
A River: and another while contrary therevntoo
Thou wart a fire. No less power than also thus too do
Had Erisicthons daughter whom Avvtolychus took too wife.
Her father was a person that despised all his life
The power of Gods, and never did vouchsauf them sacrifyse.
He also is reported too have heawen in wicked wise
The grove of Ceres, and to fell her holy woods which ay
Had vndiminisht and vnhackt continewed to that day
There stood in it a warrie Oak which was a wood alone.
Upon it round hung fillets, crowns, and tables, many one,
The vows of such as had obteynd their hearts desire. Full often
The Woodnymphes underneath this tree did fetch their frisks aloft
And oftentimes with hand in hand they daunced in a round
About the Trunk, whose bigness was of timber good and sound
Full fifteen fadom. All the trees within the wood beside,
Were unto this, as weeds to them: so far it did them hyde.
Yet could not this move Triops son his axe therefro too hold,
But bade his servants cut it down. And when he did behold
Them stunting at his hest, he snatched an axe with furious mood
From one of them, and wickedly said thus. Although this wood
Not only were the derling of the Goddess, but also
The Goddess even herself: yet would I make it ere I go
Too kiss the clowers with her top that pranks with branches so.
This spoken, as he sweakt his axe aside to fetch his blow,
The manast Oak did quake and sygh, the Acornes that did grow
Thereon toogither with the leaves too wex full pale began,
And shrinking in for fear the boughs and branches looked wan.
As Soon as that his cursed hand had wounded once the tree,
The blood came spinning from the carf, as freshly as ye see
It issue from a Bullocks neck whose throat is newly cut
Before the Altar, when his flesh to sacrifyse is put.
They were amazed euery^hone. And one among them all
Too let the wicked act, durst from the tree his hatchet call.
The lewd Thessalian facing him said: Take thou here too thee
The guerdon of thy godliness. and turning from the tree,
He chopped of the fellows head. Which done, he went again
And heawed on the Oak. straight from amid the tree as then
There issued such a sound as this. Within this tree dwell I
A Nymph too Ceres very dear, who now before I dye
In comfort of my death do give thee warning thou shall bye
Thy doing dear within a while. he goes wilfully
Still thorough with his wickedness, until at length the Oak
Pulld partly by the force of ropes, and cut with axis stroke,
Did fall, and with his weight bare down of under wood great store.
The Woodnymphes with the losses of the woods and theyrs right sore
Amazed, gathered on a knot, and all in mourning weed
Went sad too Ceres, praying her too wreake that wicked deed
Of Erisicthons. Ceres was content it should be so.
And with the moving of her head in nodding too and fro,
She shook the feeldes which laden were with fruitful Harvest tho.
And therewithal a punishment most piteous she proceeds
Too put in practyse: were it not that his most heinous deeds,
No pity did deserve too have at any bodies hand.
With helpelesse hungar him too pyne, in purpose she did stand*
And forasmuch as she herself and famine might not meet.
(For fate forbids famine too abide within the leete
Where plenty is) she thus bespake a fayrie of the hill.
There lies in the utmost bounds of Tartarie the chill
A Dreerie place, a wretched soil, a barreine plot: no grain,
No fruit, no tree, is growing there: but there does ay remain
Unweeldsome cold, with trembling fear, and palenesse white as clowt,
And foodlesse famine. Will thou her immediately withowt
Delay too shed herself intoo the stomach of the wretch,
And let no plenty staunch her force. but let her working stretch
Above the power of me. And least the longnesse of the way
May make thee weary, take thou here my charyot: take I say
My draggons for to bear thee through the air. In saying so
She gave her them. The Nymph mounts up, and flying thence as the
Alyghts in Scythy land, and up the cragged top of hye
Mount Caucasus did cause her Snakes with much a do too stye.
Where seeking long for famine, she the gaptoothd elfe did spy
Amid a barreine stony feeld a ramping up the grass
With ougly nails, and chanking it. Her face pale colored was.
Her hear was harsh and shirle, her eyes were sunken in her head.
Her lyppes were hoar with filth, her t^^th were fu^d and rusty read.
Her skin was starched, and so sheere a man might well espy
The very bowels in her bulk how every one did lie.
And eke above her courbed loins her withered hippes were seen.
In stead of belly was a space where belly should have been.
Her breast did hang so sagging down as that a man would ween
That scarcely to her ridgebone had her ribs been fastened well.
Her leanness made her joints bolne big, and kneepannes for too swell.
And with exceeding mighty knubs her heels behynd boynd out.
Now when the Nymph behild this elfe a far, (she was in dout
Too come too near her:) she declarde her Ladies message. And
In that same little while although the Nymph aloof did stand,
And though she were but newly come, yet seemed she too feel
The force of famine. Whervppon she turning back her wheel
Did reyne her dragons up aloft: who straight with courage free
Conveyed her into Thessaly. Although that famine be
Ay contrary too Ceres woork, yet did she then agree
Too do her will. and glyding through the Air supported by
The wynd, she found thappoynted house: and entering by and by
The caytifs chamber where he slept (it was in time of night)
She hugged him between her arms there snort^ng bolt vpryght.
And breathing her into him, blew upon his face and breast,
That hungar in his empty veins might work as he did rest.
And when she had accomplished her charge, she then forsook
The fruitful Clymates of the world, and home ageine betook
Herself unto her frutelesse feeldes and former dwelling place.
The gentle sleep did all this while with feathers soft embrace
The wretched Erisicthons corse. Who dreaming straight of meat
Did stir his hungry jaws in vain as though he had too eat
And chanking tooth on tooth a pace he gryndes them in his head,
And occupies his empty throat with swallowing, and in stead
Of food deuoures the lither air. But when that ^leepe with night
Was shaken of, immediately a furious appetite
Of feeding began too rage in him, which in his greedy gummes
And in his meatlesse maw does reign vnstauncht. Anon there cummes
Before him whatsoever lives on sea, in air or land:
And yet he cries still for more. And though the platters stand
Before his face full furnished, yet does he still complain
Of hungar, craving meat at meal. The food that would sustain
Whole householdes, Towneships, Shyres and Realms suffyce not him alone
The more his pampred pau^ch consumes, ye more it makes moan
And as the sea receyues the brooks of all the worldly Realms,
And yet is never satisfyde for all the foreign streams.
And as the fell and ravening fire refuses never wood,
But burns faggots numberless, and with a furious mood
The more it has, the more it still desires evermore,
Encreacing in devouring through encreasement of the store:
So wicked Erisicthons mouth in swallowing of his meat
Was ever hungry more and more, and longed ay to eat.
Meat tolld in meat: and as he ate the place was empty still.
The hungar of his brinklesse Maw the gulf that nowght might fill
Had brought his fathers good too nowght. But yet continewed ay:
His cursed hungar vnappeasd: and nothing could alay
The flaming of his starued throat. At length when all was spent,
And intoo his vnfilled Maw both goods and lands were sent,
An only daughter did remain unworthy too have had
So lewd a father. Her he sold, so hard he was bestad.
But she of gentle courage could no bondage well abide.
And therefore stretching out her hands too seaward there beside,
Now save me quoth she from the yoke of bondage I thee pray,
O thou that my virginity enioyest as a pray.
Neptunus had it. Who too this her prayer did consent.
And though her master looking back (for after him she went)
Had newly seen her: yet he turned her shape and made her man,
And gave her look of fisherman. Her master looking than
Upon her, said. Good fellow thou that on the shore do stand
With angling rod and bayted hook and hanging line in hand,
I pray thee as thou do desire the Sea ay calm too thee,
And fishes for to byght thy bayt, and stricken still too be,
Tell where the frizzle topped wench in course and sluttish geere
That stood right now upon this shore (for well I wot that here
I saw her standing) is become. For further than this place
No footestep is appeering. She perceiving by the case
That Neptunes gift made well with her, and being glad too see
Herself enquyrd for of herself, said thus: who ere you be
I pray you for too pardon me. I turned not mine eye
A tonesyde ne a toother from this place, but did apply
My labor hard. And that you may the lesser stand in dowt,
So Neptune further still the Art and craft I go about,
As now a while no living Wight upon this level sand
(Myself excepted) neither man nor woman here did stand.
Her master did believe her words: and turning backward went
His way beguyld: and straight too her her native shape was sent.
But when her father did perceive his daughter for too have
A body so transformable, he oftentimes her gave
For monny. but the damzell still escaped now a Mare
And now a Cow, and now a Bird, a Heart, a Hynd, or Hare,
And ever fed her hungry Sire with undeserved fare.
But after that the malady had wasted all the meats
As well of store as that which she had purchased by her feats:
Most cused keytife as he was with bighting he did rend
His flesh, and by diminishing his body did intend
Too seed his body, till that death did speed his fatal end.
But what meene I too busye me in foreign matters thus?
Too alter shapes within precinct is lawful even too us
My Lords. For sumtime I am such as you do now me see
Sumtyme I wynd me in a Snake: and often I seem too be
A Captain of the herd with horns. For taking horns on me
I lost a tyne which heeretoofore did arm me as the print
Does plainly show. with that same word he syghed and did stin^.
Finis octaui Libri.
THE NINTH BOOK of Ouids Metamorphosis.
WHat ayleth thee (quoth Theseus) too sygh so sore? and how
Befell it thee too get this mayme that is upon thy brow?
The noble stream of Calydon made answer, who did wear
A Garland made of reeds and flags upon his sedgie hear.
A grievous penance you enjoin. for who would gladly show
The combats in the which himself did take the overthrow?
Yet will I make a just report in order of the same.
For why? too have the woorser hand was not so great a shame,
As was the honor such a match too undertake. And much
It comforts me that he who did me overcome, was such
A valiant champion. If perchance you erst have heard the name
Of Deyanyre the fairest Maid that ever God did frame
She was in mine opinion. And the hope too win her love
Did mickle envy and debate among her wooers move.
With whom I entering too the house of him that should have be
My fathrilaw, Parthaons son (I said) accept thou me
Thy Sonnylaw. And Hercules in self same sort did woo.
And all the other suitors straight gave place unto us two.
He vaunted of his father Jove, and of his famous deeds,
And how against his stepdames spyght his prowess still proceeds.
And I ageine a toother side said thus It is a shame
That God should yield too man. (this strife was long ere he became
A God). Thou seeist me a Lord of waters in thy Realm
Where I in wide and wynding banks do bear my flowing stream.
No stranger shall thou have of me sent far from foreign land:
But one of household, or at least a neighbor here at hand.
Alonly let it be too me no hindrance that the wife
Of Jove abhorres me not, ne that upon the pain of life
She sets me not too task. For where thou bostest thee too be
Al^menas son, Jove either is not father unto thee:
Or if he be it is by sin. In making Jove thy father,
Thou maakst thy mother but a whore. now choose thee whither rather
Thou had too grant this tale of Jove surmised for too be,
Or else thy self begot in shame and borne in bastardee.
At that he grimly bendes his brows, and much a do he has
Too hold his hands. so sore his heart inflamed is with wrath.
He said no more but thus. My hand does serve me better than
My tongue. Content I am (so I in feighting vanquish can)
That thou shall overcome in words. And therewithal he began
Me feercely to assail. Me thought it was a shame for me
That had even now so stoutly talked, in doings faint too be.
I casting of my greenish cloak thrust stiffly out at length
Mine arms and streynd my pawing arms too hold him out by strength,
And framed every limme too cope. With both his hollow hands
He caught up dust and sprincked me: and I likewise with sands.
Made him all yelow too. One while he at my neck does snatch
Another while my clear crisp legs he strives for too catch,
Or trippes at me: and euerywhere the vauntage he does watch.
My weightinesse defended me, and clearly did disfeate
His stout assaults as when a wave with hideous noise does beat
Against a Rock, the Rock does still both sauf and sound abide
By reason of his massinesse. We drew a while a side.
And then incountring fresh ageine, we kept our places stowt
Full minded not too yield an inch, but for too hold it out.
Now were we stonding foot too foot. And I with all my breast
Was leaning forward, and with head against his head did rest,
And with my gryping fingars I against his fingars thrust.
So have I seen two myghtie Bulls together feercely just
In seeking as their pryse to have the fairest Cow in all
The feeld too be their make, and all the herd both great and small
Stand gazing on them fearfully not knowing unto which
The conquest of so great a gain shall fall. Three times a twich
Gave Hercules and could not wrinch my leaning breast him fro
But at the fourth he shook me of and made me too let go
My hold: and with a push (I will tell truth) he had a knacke
Too turn me of, and heavily he hung upon my back.
And if I may believed be (as sure I meene not I
To vaunt my self vayngloriusly by telling of a lie,)
Me thought a mountain whelmed me. But yet with much a do
I wrested in my sweating arms, and hardly did undo
His griping hands. He following still his vauntage, suffered not
Me once too breath or gather strength. but by and by he got
Me by the neck. Then was I feign too sink with knee too ground,
And kiss the dust. Now when in strength too weak myself I found,
I took me too my slights, and slipped in shape of Snake away
Of woondrous length. And when that I of purpose him too fray
Did bend myself in swelling rolls, and made a hideous noise
Of hissing with my forked tongue, he smyling at my toys,
And laughing them too scorn said thus. It is my Cradle game
To vanquish Snakes O Acheloy. Admit thou overcame
All other Snakes, yet what art thou compared too the Snake
Of Lerna, who by cutting of did still encreasement take?
For of a hundred heads not one so soon was paarde away,
But that upon the stump thereof there budded other tway
This sprouting Snake whose braunching heads by slaughter did revive
And grow by cropping, I subdewd, and made it could not thryue.
And think thou (who being none would seem a Snake) too escape?
Who do with foorged weapons feyght and under borrowed shape?
This said, his fingars of my neck he fastened in the nape
Me thought he graand my throat as though he did with pinsons nip
I struggled from his churlish thumbes my pinched chappes too slip
But do the best and worst I could he overcame me so.
Then thirdly did remain the shape of Bull. and quickly tho
I turning too the shape of Bull rebelld against my ^o.
He stepping too my left side cloce, did fold his arms about
My wattled neck, and following me then running maynely out
Did brag me back, and made me pitch my horns against the ground,
And in the deepest of the sand he overthrew me round.
And yet not so content, such hold his cruel hand did take
Upon my welked horn, that he a sunder quight it brake,
And pulld it from my maimed brew. The waterfayries came
And filling it with fruit and flowers did consecrate the same,
And so my horn the Tresory of plenteousnesse became.
As Soon as Acheloy had told this tale a waiting Maid
With flaring hear that lay on both her shoulders and arayd
Like one of Dame Dianas Nymphs with solemn grace forth came
And brought that rich and precious horn, and heaped in the same
All kind of fruits that Harvest sendes, and specially such fruit
As serves for latter course at meals of every sort and suit.
As Soon as daylight came ageine, and that the Sunny rays
Did shine upon the tops of things, the Princes went their ways.
They would not tarry till the flood were altogether fallen
And that the River in his banks ran low ageine and calm.
Then Acheloy amid his waves his Crabtree face did hyde
And head disarmed of a horn. And though he did abide
In all parts else both sauf and sound, yet this deformitye
Did cut his comb: and for too hyde this blemish from the eye.
He hydes his hurt with Sallow leaves, or else with sedge and reed.
But of the selfsame Maid the love killd thee feerce Nesse in deed,
When percing swiftly through thy back an arrow made thee bleed.
For as Jove's issue with his wife was onward on his way
In going too his countryward, enforst he was too stay
At swift Euenus bank, because the stream was risen sore
Above his bounds through rage of rain that fell but late before
Agein so full of whoorlpooles and of gulles the channel was,
That scarce a man could any where find place of passage. As
Not caring for himself but for his wife he there did stand,
This Nessus came unto him (who was strong of body and
Knew well the foordes,) and said use thou thy strength O Hercules
In swimming. I will find the means this Lady shall with ease
Be set upon the further bank. So Hercules betook
His wife too Nessus. She for fear of him and of the brook
Lookte pale. Her husband as he had his quiver by his side
Of arrows full, and on his back his heavy Lions hyde,
(For too the further bank he erst his club and bow had cast)
Said. since I have begone, this brook both must and shall be past,
He never casts further doubts, nor seeks the calmest place,
But through the roughest of the stream he cuts his way a pace.
Now as he on the furthersyde was taking up his bow,
He heard his wedlock shreeking out, and did her calling know:
And cryde to Nesse (who went about to deal unfaithfully
In running with his charge away) Hoawe whither do thou fly
Thou Royster thou, upon a vain hope by swiftness too escape
My hands? I say give ear thou Nesse for all thy double shape,
And meddle not with that that's mine. Though no regard of me
Might move thee too refrain from rape, thy father yet might be
A warning, who for offering shame too Juno now does feel
Continual torment in his limbs by turning on a wheel.
For all that thou have horses feet which do so bold thee make,
Yet shall thou not escape my hands. I will thee overtake
With wound and not with feet. He did according as he spoke.
For with an arrow as he fled he struck him through the back,
And out before his brist ageine the hooked iron stacke.
And when the same was pulled out, the blood a main ensewd
At both the holes with poison foul of Lerna Snake embrewd:
This blood did Nessus take, and said within himself: well: fith
I needs must dye, yet will I not dye vnreuendgd. And with
The same he staynd a shirt, and gave it unto Dyanyre,
Assuring her it had the power too kindle Cupids fire.
A great while after when the deeds of worthy Hercules
Were such as filled all the world, and also did appease
The hatred of his stepmother, As he upon a day
With conquest from Oechalia came, and was about to pay
His vows to Jove upon the Mount of Cenye, tatling fame
(Who in reporting things of truth delyghts too sauce the same
With tales, and of a thing of nowght does ever greater grow
Through false and newly forged lies that she hirself does sow)
Told Dyanyre that Hercules did cast a liking too
A Lady called Iolee* And Dyanyra (whoo
Was jealous over Hercules,) gave credit too the same.
And when that of a Leman first the tidings too her came,
She being stricken too the heart, did fall too tears alone,
And in a lamentable wise did make most woeful moan.
Anon she said: what meene these tears thus gushing from mine eyes?
My husbands Leman will rejoice at these same tears of mine.
Nay, since she is too come, the best it were too shonne delay,
And for too woork sum new deuyce and practyse while I may,
Before that in my bed her limbs the filthy strumpet lay.
And shall I then complain? or shall I hold my tongue with skill?
Shall I return too Calydon? or shall I tarry still?
Or shall I get me out of doors, and let them have their will?
What if that I ( Meleager) remembering me too be
Thy suster, too attempt sum act notorious did agree?
And in a harlots death did show (that all the world might see)
What greef can cause the womankynd too enterprise among?
And specially when therevntoo they forced are by wrong.
With wavering thoughts right violently her mind was tossed long.
At last she did prefer before all others, for too send
The shirt bestayned with the blood of Nessus too the end
Too quicken up the quayling love. And so not knowing what
She gave, she gave her own remorse and greef too ^ychas that
Did know as little as herself: and wretched woman, she
Desyrd him gently too her Lord presented it too see.
The noble Prince receiving it without mistrust therein,
Did wear the poison of the Snake of Lerna next his skin.
Too offer incense and too pray too Jove he did begin,
And on the Marble Altar he full boawles of wine did shed,
When as the poison with the heat resolving, largely spread
Through all the limbs of Hercules. As long as ere he could,
The stoutnesse of his ha^t was such, that sygh no whit he would.
But when the mischeef grew so great all patience too surmount,
He thrust the altar from him straight, and filled all the mount
Of Oeta with his roaring out. He went about too tear
The deathfull garment from his back, but where he pulled, there
He pulld away the skin: and (which is lothsum too report)
It either cleaued too his limbs and members in such sort
As that he could not pull it o^, or else it tare away
The flesh, that bare his mighty bones and grisly sinews lay.
The scalding venim boiling in his blood, did make it hiss,
As when a gad of steel red whot in water quenched is.
There was no measure of his pain. The frying venim hent
His inwards, and a purple swet from all his body went.
His findged sinews shrinking crakt, and with a secret strength
The poison even within his bones the Maree melts at length.
And holding up his hands too heaven he said with hideous reere.
O Saturnes daughter feed thy self on my distresses here.
Yea feed. and cruel wight this plage behold thou from above
And glut thy savage heart therewith. Or if thy fo may move
Thee unto pity, (for too thee I am an utter so)
Bereeue me of my hateful soul distressed with helpless woe,
And borne too endless toil. For death shall unto me be sweet,
And for a cruel stepmother is death a gift most meet.
And is it I that did destroy Busiris who did foil
His temple floores with strangers blood? Is It I that did dispoyle
Antaeus of his moothers help? Is It I that could not be
Abashed at the Spanyard who in one had bodies three?
Nor at the trypleheaded shape O Cerberus of thee?
Are you the hands that by the horns the Bull of Candie drew?
Did you king Augies stable clenze whom afterward ye slew?
Are you the same by whom the fowls were s^aard from Stymphaly?
Caught you the Stag in Maydenwood which did not run but fly?
Are you the hands whose puissance received for your pay
The golden belt of Thermodon? Did you convey away
The Apples from the Dragon fell that waked night and day?
Against the force of me, defense the Centaures could not make,
Nor yet the Boar of Arcadie: nor yet the ougly Snake
Of Lerna, who by loss did grow and dooble force still take.
What? is it I that did behold the pampred Iades of Thrace
With Maungers full of flesh of men on which they fed a pace?
Is It I that down at sight thereof their greazy Maungers threw,
And both the fatted Iades themselves and ^ke their master slew?
The Nemean Lion by these arms lies dead upon the ground.
These arms the monstr^ous Giant Cake by Tyber did confound.
Upon these shoulders have I borne the weight of all the sky.
Jove's cruel wife is weerye of commanding me. Yet I
Unweerie am of doing still. But now on me is light,
An uncouth plage, which neither force of hand, nor virtues might,
Nor Art is able too resist. Like wasting fire it spreedes
Among mine inwards, and through out on all my body feeds.
But all this while Eurysthye lives in health. And sum men may
Beeleue there be sum Gods in deed. Thus much did Hercule say.
And wounded over Oeta high, he stalking began too stray,
As when a Bull in maimed bulk, a deadly Dart does bear,
And that* the dooer of the deed is shrunk aside for fear.
Often syghing might you him have seen, often trembling, often about
Too tear the (...) with his hands from top too to througho^t.
And throwing down the mighty trees, and chaufing with the hills,
Or casting up his hands too heaven where Jove his father dwelles.
Behold as Lychas trembling in a hollow rock did lurk,
He spyed him. And as his greef did all in fury woork,
He said. Art thou sir Lychas he that broughtest unto me
This plagye present? of my death must thou the woorker be?
He quaakt and shaakt, and looked pale, and fearfully began make
Excuse. But as with humbled hands he kneeling too him spoke,
The furio^s Hercule caught him up, and swindging him about
His head a half a doozen times or more, he floong him out
Into the Euboyan sea with force surmounting any sling.
He hardened intoo peble stone as in the air he hing.
And even as rain conieald by wynd is said too turn too snow,
And of the snow round rolled up a thicker mass too grow,
Which falls down in hayle: so men in ancient time report,
That Lychas being swindgd about by violence in that sort,
(His blood then being drayned out, and having left at all
No moisture,) intoo peble stone was turned in his fall.
Now also in the Euboyan sea appeeres a high short rock
In shape of man against the which the shipmen shun too knock,
As though it could them faeele* and they do call it by the name
Of Lychas still. But thou Jove's imp of great renown and fame,
Did fell the trees of Oeta high, and making of the same
A pyle, did give too Philoctet^.
Poeans son thy quiver and thy bow,
And arrows which should help agein Troy town too overthrow.
He put too fire, and as the same was kindling in the pyle,
Thy self did spread thy Lions skin upon the wood the while,
And leaning with thy head against thy Club, thou laydst thee down
As cheerfully, as if with flowers and garlonds on thy crown
Thou had been set a banqueting among full cups of wine.
Anon on every side about those careless limbs of thine
The fire began too gather strength, and crackling noise did make,
Assayling him whose noble heart ^or daliance did it take.
The Gods for this defender of the earth were sore afraid
Too whom with cheerful countnance Jove perceiving it thus said.
This fear of yours is my delyght, and gladly even with all
My heart I do rejoice O Gods that mortal folk me call
Their king and father, thinking me ay myndfull of their weal,
And that mine offspring should do well your selves do show such zeal.
For though that you do attribute your favor too desert,
Considring his most woondrous acts: yet I too for my part
Am bound unto you. Nerethelesse, for that I would not have
Your faithful hearts without just cause in fearful passions wave,
I would not have you of the flames in Oeta make account.
For as he has all other things, so shall he them surmount.
Save only on that part that he has taken of his mother,
The fire shall have no power at all. Eternal is the the other,
The which he takes of me, and cannot dye, ne yield too fire.
When this is rid from earthly dross, then will I lift it higher,
And take it intoo heaven: and I believe this deed of mine
Will gladsome be too all the Gods. If any do repyne,
If any do repyne I say that Hercule should become
A God, repyne he still for me, and look he sour and glum.
But let him know that Hercules deserves this reward,
And that he shall against his will allow it afterward.
The Gods assented euery^hone. And Juno seemd too make
No evil countnance too the rest, until her husband spoke
The last. for then her look was such as well they might perceive,
She did her husbands noting her in evil part conceive.
While Jove was talking with the Gods, as much as fire could waste
So much had fire consumde. And now O Hercules thou haste
No carkesse for too know thee by. That part is quyght bereft
Which of thy mother thou did take. Alonly now is left
The likeness that thou tookst of Jove. And as the Serpent slye
In casting of his withered slough, renewes his years thereby,
And wexeth lustyer than before, and looks crisp and bright
With scoured scales: so Hercules as soon as that his spryght
Had left his mortal limbs, began in his better part too thryue,
And for too seem a greater thing than when he was alive,
And with a stately majesty right reverend too appear.
His mighty father took him up above the cloudy spheere,
And in a charyot placed him among the streaming stars.
Howge Atlas felt the weight thereof. But nothing this disbarres
Eurysthyes malice. Cruelly he prosecutes the hate
Upon the offspring, which he bare against the father late.
But yet too make her moan unto and wayle her miscrie
And tell her sons great works, which all the world could testify,
Old Alcmen had Dame Iolee. By Hercules last will
In wedlock and in hearty love she joined was too Hill,
By whom she then was big with child: when thus Alcmena said*
The Gods at least be merciful and send thee then their aid,
And short thy labor, when the fruit the which thou ghost withal
Now being ripe en^orceth thee with fearful voice too call
Upon Ilithya president of chyldbirthes, whom the ire
Of Juno at my traveling made deaf too my desire.
For when the Sun through twyce five signs his course had fully run,
And that the paynfull day of birth approached of my son,
My burden strayned out my womb, and that that I did bear
Became so great, that of so howge a mass ye well might swear
That Jove was father. Neither was I able too endure
The travel any longer time. Even now I you assure
In telling it a shuddring cold through all my limbs does strike,
And partly it renewes my peynes too think upon the like.
I being in most cruel throws nights seven and days eke seven,
And tired with continual pangs, did lift my hands too heaven,
And crying out aloud did call Lucina too mine aid,
Too loose the burden from my womb. She came as I had prayed:
But so corrupted long before by Juno my most fo,
That for too martyr me too death with pain she purposde tho.
For when she heard my piteous plaints and gronings, down she sat
On you same altar which you see there standing at my gate.
Upon her left knee she had pitched her right ham, and beside
She stayed the birth with fingars one within another tide
In lattiswyse. And secretly she whisperde witching spells
Which hindered my deliverance more than all her doings else.
I labord still: and forced by pain and torments of my fitts,
I rayld on Jove (although in vain) as one beside her wits.
And ay I wished for too dye. The words that I did speak,
Were such as even the hardest stones of very flint might break.
The wives of Thebee being there, for sauf deliverance prayed
And giving cheerful words, did bid I should not be dismayed.
Among the other women there that too my labor came,
There was an honest yeomans wife, Galantis was her name.
Her hear was yellow as the gold, she was a jolly Dame,
And stoutly served me, and I did love her for the same.
This wife (I know not how) did smell some packing gone about
On Iunos part. And as she often was passing in and out,
She spyde Lucina set upon the altar holding fast
Her arms toogither on her knees, and with her fingars cast
Within each other on a knot, and said unto her thus.
I pray you who so ere you be, rejoice you now with us,
My Lady Alcmen has her wish, and sauf is brought a bed.
Lucina leaped up amazde at that that she had sed.
And let her ^ands a sunder slip. And I immediately
With loosening of the knot, had sauf deliverance by and by.
They say that in deceyuing Dame Lucina Galant laughed.
And therefore by the yellow locks the Goddess wroth her caught,
And dragged her. And as she would have risen from the ground,
She kept her down, and into legs her arms she did confound.
Her former stoutnesse still remaynes: her back does keep the hew
That ^rst was in her hear: her shape is only altered new.
And for with lying mouth she helped a woman laboring, she
Does kindle also at her mouth. And now she haunteth free
Our hou^es as she did before, a Weas^e as we see.
With that she syghes too think upon her servants hap, and then
Her daughtrinlaw immediately replied thus again.
But mother, she whose altered shape does move your heart so sore,
Was neither kith^nor kin too you. What will you say therefore,
If of mine own dear suster I the woondrous fortune show?
Although my sorrow and the tears that from mine eyes do flow,
Do hinder me, and stop my speech. Her mother (you must know
My father by another wife had me) bare never mo
But this same Lady Dryopee, the fairest Lady tho
In all the land of Oechalye Whom being then no maid
(For why the (...).
God of Delos and of Delphos had her frayd)
Andraemon takes too his wife, and thinks him well apayd.
There is a certain leaning Lake whose bowing banks do show
A likeness of the salt sea shore. Upon the brim do grow
All round about it Mirtletrees. My suster thither goes
Unwares what was her destiny, and (which you may suppose
Was more too be disd^yned at) the cause of coming there
Was too the fayries of the Lake fresh garlonds for too bear.
And in her arms a babye her sweet burden she did hold.
Who sucking on her breast was yet not full a tweluemoonth old.
Not far from this same pond did grow a Lote tree florisht gay
With purple flowers and beries sweet, and leaves as green as Bay.
Of these same flowers too please her boy my suster gathered sum,
And I had thought too do so too, for I was thither cum.
I saw how from the sliuered flowers red drops of blood did fall,
And how that shuddring horribly the branches quaakt withal,
You must perceive that (as too late the Countryfolk declare)
A Nymph called Lotos flying from foul Pryaps filthy ware,
Was turned intoo this same tree reserving still her name.
My suster did not know so much. who when she backward came
Afraid at that that she had seen, and having sadly prayed
The Nymphs of pardon, too have gone her way again assayd:
Her feet were fastened down with roots. She stryued all she might
Too pluck them up. but they so sure within the earth were pyght,
That nothing save her upper parts she could that present move.
A tender bark grows from beneath up leysurly above,
And softly ouerspreddes her loins. which when she saw, she went
About too tear her hear, and full of leaves her hand she hent.
Her head was ouergrowen with leaves. And little Amphise (so
Had Eurytus his Graundsyre naamd her son not long ago)
Did feel his mothers dugges wex hard. And as he still them drew
In sucking, not a whit of milk nor moisture did ^ns^w.
I standing by thee did behold thy cruel chance: but naught
I could relieve thee suster mine. yet too my power I wrought
Too stay the growing of thy trunk and of thy branches by
Embracing thee. Yea I protest I would right willingly
Have in the self same bark with thee been closed up. Behold,
Her husband good Andraemon and her wretched father old
Sir Eurytus came thither and enquyrd for Dryopee.
And as they asked for Dryopee, I showed them Lote the tree.
They kissed the wood which yet was warm, and falling down bylow,
Did hug the roots of that their tree. My suster now could show
No part which was not wood except her face. A deawe of tears
Did stand upon the wretched leaves late formed of her hears.
And while she might, and while her mouth did give her way too speak,
With such complaynt as this, her mind she last of all did break.
If credit may be given too such as are in wretchedness,
I swear by God I never yet deserved this distress.
I suffer pain without desert. My life has guiltless been.
And if I lie, I would these boughs of mine which now are green,
Might withered be, and I heawen down and burned in the fire.
This infant from his mothers womb remove you I desire:
And put him forth too nurce, and cause him underneath my tree
Often times too suck, and oftentimes too play. And when that he
Is able for too speak I pray you let him greet me here,
And sadly say in this same trunk is hid my mother dear.
But learn him for too shun all ponds and pulling flowers from trees,
And let him in his heart believe that all the shrubs he sees,
Are bodies of the Goddesses. Adieu dear husband now,
Adieu dear father, and adieu dear suster. And in you
If any love of me remain, defend my boughs I pray
From wound of cutting hook and ax, and bit of beast for ay.
And for I cannot stoop too you, raise you yourselues too me,
And come and kiss me while I may yet touched and kissed be.
And lift me up my little boy. I can no longer talk,
For now about my lillye neck as if it were a stalk
The tender rynd begins too creep, and ouergrowes my top.
Remove your fingars from my face* the spreading bark does stop
My dying eyes without your help. She had no sooner left
Her talking, but her life therewith toogither was bereft.
But yet a good while after that her native shape did fade,
Her newmade boughs continewed warm. Now while that Iole made
Report of this same woondrous tale, and while Alcmena (who
Did weep) was drying up the tears of Iole weeping too,
By putting too her thomb: there hapt a sodeine thing so strange.
That unto mirth from heaviness their hearts it straight did change.
For at the door in manner even a very boy as then
With short soft Down about his chin, revoked back again
Too youthful years, stood Iolay with countnance smooth and trim.
Dame Hebee Iunos daughter had bestowde this gift on him,
Entreated at his earnest suit. Whom mynding fully there
The giving of like gift ageine too any too forswear,
Dame Themis would not suffer. For (quoth she) this present hour
Is cruel war in Thebee town, and none but Jove has power
Too vanquish stately Canapey. The brothers shall a like
Wound either other. And alive a Prophet shall go seek
His own quick ghost among the dead, the earth him swallowing in.
The son by taking vengeance for his fathers death shall win
The name of kind and wicked man, in one and self same case.
And flayght with mischeefes, from his wits and from his native place
The furies and his mothers ghost shall restlessely him chase,
Until his wy^e demand of him the fatal gold for meede,
And that his cousin Phegies sword do make his sides too bleed.
Then shall the fair Callirrhoee Achelous daughter pray
The mighty Jove in humble wise too grant her children may
Retire ageine too youthful years, and that he will not see
The death of him that did revenge vnvenged for too be.
Jove moved at her suit shall cause his daughtrinlaw too give
Like gift, and back from age too youth Callirrhoes children drive.
When Themis through foresyght had spoke these words of prophecy,
The Gods began among themselves vain talk too multiply,
They mooyld why others might not give like gift as well as she.
First Pallants daughter grudged that her husband old should be.
The gentle Ceres murmurde that her Iasions hear was hoar.
And Vulcane would have called ageine the years long spent before
By Ericthonius. And the nyce Dame Venus having care
Of time too come, the making young of old Anchises swear.
So every God had ^ne too whom he special favor bare.
And through this partial love of theyrs seditiously increast
A hurlyburly, till the time that Jove among them preast,
And said. So smally do you stand in awe of me this hour,
As thus too rage? Thinks any of you himself too have such power,
As for too alter destinye? I tell you Iolay
Recovered has by destinye his years erst past away.
Callirrhoes children must return too youth by destiny,
And not by force of arms, or suit susteynd ambitiously.
And too th'entent with meelder minds ye may this matter bear,
Even I myself by destinyes am ruled. which if I were
Of power too alter, think you that our Aeacus should stoop
By reason of his feeble age? or Radamanth should droope?
Or Minos, who by reason of his age is now disdeynd,
And lives not in so sure a state as heretoofore he reygnd?
The words of Jove so movd the Gods that none of them complaynd,
Since Radamanth and Aeacus were both with age constreynd:
And Minos also: who (as long as lusty youth did last,)
Did even with terror of his name make mighty Realms aghast.
But then was Minos weakened sore, and greatly stood in fear
Of Milet one of Deyons race: who proudly did him bear
Upon his father Phoebus and the stoutnesse of his youth.
And though he feared he would rebel: yet durst he not his mouth
Once open for too banish him his Realm: until at last
Departing of his own accord, Miletus swiftly past
The Gotes^a and did build a town upon the Asian ground,
Which still reteynes the name of him that first the same did found.
And there the daughter of the brook Maeander which does go
So often backward, Cyane a Nymph of body so
Exceeding comely as the like was seldom heard of, as
She by her fathers wynding banks for pleasure walking was,
Was known by Milet: unto whom a pair of twins she brought,
And of the twins the names were Caune and Byblis* Byblis ought
Too be a mirror unto Maids in lawful wise too love.
This Byblis cast a mind too Caune. but not as did behoue
A suster too her brotherward. When first of all the fire
Did kindle, she perceyvd it not. She thought in her desire
Of kissing him so oftentimes no sin, ne yet no harm
In cleeping him about the neck so often with her arm.
The glittering gloss of godliness beguyld her long. Her love
Began from evil unto worse by little too remove.
She commes too see her brother decked in brave and trim attire,
And for too seem exceeding fair it was her whole desire.
And if that any fayrer were in all the ^locke than she,
It spyghts her. In what case she was as yet she did not see.
Her heat exceeded not so far as for too vow: and yet
She suffered in her troubled brist full many a burning fit.
Now calls she him master, now she utter hates all
The names of kin. She rather had he should her Byblis call
Than suster. yet no filthy hope she durst permit too creep
Within her mind awake. But as she lay in quiet sleep,
She often behild her love: and often she thought her brother came
And lay with her, and (though a sleep) she blushed at the same.
When sleep was gone, she long lay dumb still musing on the sight,
And said with wavering mind. Now woe is me most wretched wight.
What meenes the image of this dream that I have seen this night?
I would not wish it should be true, Why dreamed I then so?
Sure he is fair although he should be judged by his fo.
He likes me well* and were he not my brother, I might set
My love on him, and he were me right worthy for too get,
But unto this same match the name of kindred is a let.
Well. So that I wake do still me undefiled keep,
Let come as often as they will such dreamings in my sleep.
In sleep there is no witness by* In sleep yet may I take
As great a pleasure (in a sort) as if I were awake.
Oh Venus and thy tender son Sir Cupid, what delyght,
How present feeling of your sport has touched me this night?
How lay I as it were resolvd both maree, flesh, and bone?
How gladdes it me too think thereon? Alas too soon was gone
That pleasure, and too hastye and despyghtfull was the night

Between us two removed me, how well it would agree
O Caune that of thy father I the daughtrinlaw should be?
How fitly might my father have a sonneinlaw of thee?
Would God that all save auncesters were common too us twain
I would thou were of nobler stock than I* I cannot said
O perle of beauty what she is whom thou shall make a mother.
Alas how ill befalles it me that I could have none other
Than those same parents which are (...)? So only s^ill my brother
And not my husband may thou be. The thing that hurts us both
Is one, and that between us ay inseparably gothe.
what meene my dreams the? what effect have dreams? and may there be
Effect in dreams? The Gods are far in better case than we.
For why? the Gods have matched with their susters as we see.
So Saturn did alie with Ops the nearest of his blood.

Admit the matter likes me. He will for sin it take.

Ah whither am I headlong driven? auaunt foul filthy fire:

Shall I therefore who would not have rejected him if he

And can thou utter forth thy mind? and tell him of thy need?
My love will make me speak. I can. Or if that shame do stay

This likes her best. upon this point now restes her doubtful mind

And leaning on her elbow said. Let him advise him what

Alas too what ungraciousness intend I for too fall?
What fury raging in my heart my senses does appall?
In thinking so, with trembling hand she framed her too wryght
The matter that her troubled mind in musing did indyght.
Her right hand holds the pen, her left does hold the empty wax.
She ginnes. She doutes, she wryghtes: she in the tables finds lacks.
She notes, she blurres, dislikes, and likes: and changes this for that.
She lays away the book, and takes it up. She wotes not what
She would herself. What ever thing she myndeth for too do
Misliketh her. A shamefastness with boldness mixed theretoo
Was in her countnance. She had once writ Suster: Out again
The name of Suster for too raze she thought it best. And then

The health which if thou give her not she is not like too have
Thy lover wishes unto thee. I dare not ah for shame
I dare not tell thee who I am, nor let thee hear my name.
And if thou do demand of me what thing I do desire,

And long I stryued (wretched wench) too escape the violent Dart

Let aged folks have skill in law: too age it does belong

W^e know not what is lawful (...). And therefore we may* (...)

Why did not open tokens warn too bridle my desire,

And moysted with a stream of tears the grass upon the feeld.
The waternymphes (so folk report) put under her a spring,
Which never might be dryde: and could they give a greater thing?
Immediately even like as when ye wound a pitchtree rynd,
The gum does issue out in drops: or as the western wynd
With gentle blast toogither with the warmth of Sun, vnbynd

Was turned too a fountain, which in those same vallyes bears
The title of the founder still, and gusheth freshly out
From underneath a Sugarchest as if it were a spowt.
The fame of this same wondrous thing perhaps had filled all
The hundred Towns of Candye had a greater not befall
More nearer home by Iphys means transformed late before.
For in the shyre of Phestos hard by Gnossus dwelt of yore
A yeoman of the meaner sort that Lyctus had too name.
His stock was simple, and his wealth according too the same.
Howbeet his life so vpryght was, as noman could it blame.
He came unto his wife then big and ready down too lie,
And said: two things I wish thee. Tone, that when thou out shall cry,
Thou may dispatch with little pain: the other that thou have
A Boay. For Gyrles too bring them up a greater cost do crave.
And I have no ability. And therefore if thou bring
A wench (it goes against my heart too think upon the thing)
Although against my will, I charge it straight destroyed be.
The bond of nature needs must bear in this behalf with me.
This sed, both wept exceedingly, as well the husband who
Did give commandment, as the wife that was commanded too.
Yet Telethusa earnestly at Lyct her husband lay,
(Although in vain) too have good hope, and of himself more stay.
But he was full determined. Within a while, the day

My servant Telethusa, cease this care, and break the charge

Bring up the same what ere it be I am a Goddess who

When Isis had this comfort given, she went her way again.

And so the lie through godly guile forth vnperceyued gothe.
The garments of it were a boayes. The face of it was such
As either in a boay or gyrle of beawtie uttered much.
When Iphys was of thirteen years, her father did insure

Both longed for the wedding day toogither for too cope.
For whom Ianthee thinks too be a man, she hopes too see

Herself a Maiden with a Maid (right strange) in love became.

Or nature were acquainted with. A Cow is never fond

The Stag the Hynde, the Cock the Hen. But never man could show,
That female yet was ^ane in love with female kind. O would

Not bring forth all that monstrous were, the daughter of the Son

My love is furio^ser than hers, if truth confessed be.
For she was fond of such a lust as might be compast. She
Was served by a Bull beguyld by Art in Cow of tree.
And one there was for her with whom aduowtrie to commit.
If all the conning in the world and slyghts of subtle wit
Were here, or if that Daedalus himself with vncowth wing

Could he with all his conning crafts now make a boay of me?
Or could he O Ianthee change the native shape of thee?
Nay rather Iphys settle thou thy mind and call thy wits

(Unless thou wilt deceive thy self.) So far forth wisely wade,
As right and reason may support, and love as women ought

This hope thy sex denies thee. Not watching does restreyne
Thee from embracing of the thing whereof thou art so feign.
Nor yet the Husbands iealowsie, nor rowghnesse of her Sire,
Nor yet the coyness of the Wench does hinder thy desire.
And yet thou can not her enjoy. No though that God and man
Should labor too their uttermost and do the best they can
