I cannot wish the thing but that I have it. Frank and free
The Gods have given me what they could. As I will, so will he
That must become my fathrinlaw. so wills my father too.
But nature stronger than them all consents not theretoo.
This hinders me, and nothing else. Behold the blisfull ^yme,
The day of Marriage is at hand. Ianthee shall be mine,
And yet I shall not her enjoy. Amid the water we
Shall thirst. O Juno president of marriage, why with thee
Comes Hymen too this wedding where no brydegroome you shall see,
But both are Brydes that must that day toogither coupled be?
This spoken, she did hold her peace. And now the toother maid
Did burn as whote in love as she. And earnestly she prayed
The brydale day might come with speed. The thing for which she longd
Dame Telethusa fearing sore, from day too day prolongd
The time, often feyning sickness, often pretending she had seen
Ill tokens of success. at length all shifts consumed been.
The wedding day so often delayed was now at hand. The day
Before it, taking from her head the kercheef quyght away,
And from her daughters head likewise, with scattred hear she laid
Her hands upon the Altar, and with humble voice thus prayed.
O Isis who do haunt the town of Paretonie, and
The feeldes by Maraeotis lake, and Pharos which does stand
By Alexandria, and the Nyle divided intoo seven
Great channels, comfort thou my fear, and send me help from heaven,
Thyself O Goddess, even thyself, and these thy relikes I
Did once behold and knew them all: as well thy company
As eke thy sounding rattles, and thy ^ressets burning by,
And myndfully I marked what commandment thou did give.
That I escape unpunished, that this same wench does live,
Thy counsel and thy hest it is. Have mercy now on twain,
And help us. With that word the tears ran down her cheeks amayne.
The Goddess seemed for too move her Altar: and in deed
She moved it. The temple doors did tremble like a reed.
And horns in likeness too the Moon about the Church did shine.
And Rattles made a raughtish noise. At this same luckie sign,
Although not wholly careless, yet right glad she went away.
And Iphys followed after her with larger pace than ay
She was accustomd. And her face continued not so whyght.
Her strength increased, and her look more sharper was too sight.
Her hear grew shorter, and she had a much more lively spryght,
Than when she was a wench. For thou O Iphys who right now
A modther were, art now a boay. With offerings both of you
Too Church retire, and there rejoice with faith vnfearfull. They
With offerings went too Church ageine, and there their vows did pay.
They also set a table up, which this breef meeter had.
The vows that Iphys vovvd a vvench he has performd a Lad.
Next morrow over all the world did shine with lightsome flame,
When Juno, and Dame Venus, and Sir Hymen jointly came
Too Iphys marriage, who as then transformed too a boay
Did take Ianthee too his wife, and so her love enjoy

FRom thence in saffron colored robe flew Hymen through ye air,
And into Thracia being called by Orphy did repair.
He came in d^^de at Orphyes call: bu^ neither did he sing
The words of that solemnity, nor merry countnance bring,
Nor any hand^ell of good luck. His torch with drizling smoke
Was dim: the same too burn out clear, no stirrr^ng could provoke.
The end was woorser than the sign. For as the ^ryde did rome
Abroad accompanyde with a train of Nymphs too bring her home,
A serpent lurking in the grass did (...) her in the ancle:
Whereof she did incontinent, so swift the (...) did ^ancle.
Whom when the Thracian Poet had bewayl^ sufficiently
On earth, the Ghostes departed hence he minding for too try,
Down at the gate of Taenarus did go too Limbo lake.
And thence by ghastly folk and souls late buried he did take
His journey too Persephon^e and too the king of Ghosts
That like a Lordly tyran reigns in those v^^^easant coasts.
And playing on his tuned harp he thus began too sound.
O you the Souereines of the world set underneath the ground,
Too whom we all (what ever thing is made of mortal kind)
Repair, if ^y your leave I now may freely speak my mind,
I come not hither as a spy (...)
Nor yet the foul three headed Eurre whose hears all Adders be
Too tie in cheynes. The cause of this my vyage is my wife
Whose foot a Uiper stinging did abridge her youthful life.
I would have bor^e it pa^iently: and so too do I straue.
But Love surmoun^ed power. This God is known great force too have
Above on earth. And whether he reign here or no I dow^.
But I believe he reigns here too. If fa^^e that flies about
Of former rape report not wrong, Love coupled also you.
By these same places full of fear: by this howge Chaos now,
And by the st^lnesse of this waste and emptye Kingdom, I
Beseech ye of Eurydicee v^^eele the destinye
That was so swiftly reeled up All things too you belong.
And though we lingering for a while our pageants do prolong,
Yet soon or late we all too one abiding place do rome:
We haste us hither all: this place becomes our latest home:
And you do over human kind reign longest time. Now when
This woman shall have lived full her time, she shall again
Become your own. The use of her but for a while I crave.
And if the Destnyes for my wife deny me for too have
Releace, I fully am resolvd for ever here too dwell.
Rejoice you in the death of both. As he this tale did tell,
And played on his instrument, the bloodlesse ghostes shed tears:
Too tire on Titius growing heart the greedy Grype forbeares:
The shunning water Tantalus endeuereth not too drink:
And Danaus daughters ceast too fill their tubbes that have no brink:
Ixions wheel stood still: and down sat Sisyphus upon
His rolling stone. Then first of all (so fame for truth has gone)
The Furies being stricken there with pity at his song
Did weep. And neither Pluto nor his Lady were so strong
And hard of stomach too withhold his just petition long.
They called forth Eurydicee who was as yet among
The newcome Ghosts. and limped of her wound. Her husband took
Her with condition that he should not back upon her look,
Until the time that he were past the bounds of Limbo quyght:
Or else too lose his gift. They took a path that steep vpryght
Rose dark and full of foggye mist. And now they were within
A kenning of the upper earth, when Orphye did begin
Too dowt him least she followed not, and through an eager love
Desirous for too see her he his eyes did backward move.
Immediately she slipped back. He re^ching out his hands.
Desirous too be caught and for too ketch her grasping stands.
But nothing save the slippry air (unhappy man) he caught.
She dy^ng now the second time compla^nd of Orphye naught.
For why what had she too complain, unless it were of love?
Which made her husband back again his eyes upon her move?
Her last farewell she spoke so often, that scarce he heard the sound,
And then revolted too the place in which he had her found.
This double dying of his wife set Orphye in a stound,
No less than him who at the sight of Plutos dreadful Hound
That on the middle neck of three does bear an iron cheyne,
Was stricken in a sodein fear and could it not restreyne,
Until the time his former shape and nature being gone,
His body quyght was ouergrowne, and turned intoo stone.
Or than the foolish Olenus, who on himself did take
Another fault, and giltlesse needs himself would guilty make,
Toogither with his wretched wife Lethaea, for whose pride
They both becoming stones, do stand even yet on watery I Would.
He would have gone too Hell ageine, and ^arnest suit did make:
But Charon would not suffer him too pass the Stygian lake.
Seven days he sat forlorn upon the bank and never ^ate
A bit of bread. Care, tears, and thought, and sorrow were his meat
And crying out upon the Gods of Hell as cruel, he
Withdrew too lofty Rhodopee and Heme which beaten be
With Northern wynds. Three times the Sun had passed through the sheere
And watery sign of Pisces and had finished full the year.
And Orphye (were it that his ill success he still did rue,
Or that he vowed so too do) did utterly eschew
The womankynd. Yet many a one desirous were too match
With him, but he them with repulse did all alike dispatch.
He also taught the Thracian folk a st^wes of Males too make
And of the flowring pryme of boayes the pleasure for too take.
There was a hill, and on the hill a very level plot.
Fair green with grass. But as for shade or covert was there not.
As Soon as that this Po^t borne of Gods, in that same place
Sat down and touched his tuned strings, a shadow came a pace.
There wanted neither Chaons tree, nor yet the trees too which
Fresh Phaetons susters turned were, nor Beeche, nor Holme, nor Which
Nor gentle Asp, nor wyuelesse Bay, nor lofty Chestnuttree.
Nor Hazle spalt, nor Ash whereof the shafts of spears made be.
Nor knotlesse Fur, nor cheerful Plane, nor Maple ^lecked grain.
Nor Lote, nor Sallow which delights by waters too remain.
Nor slender twigged Tamarisk* nor Bor ay green of hew.
Nor Figtrees laden with their fruit of colors brown and blew.
Nor double colored Myrtletrees. Moreover thither came
The wrything Iuye, and the Uyne that runs upon a frame.
Elmes clad with Uynes, and Ashes wyld, and Pitchtrees black as cole,
And full of trees with goodly fruit red stryped, Ortyards whole.
And Palmetrees lythe which in reward of conquest men do bear,
And Pynapple with tufted top and harsh and prickling hear,
The tree too Cybele mother of the Gods most dear. For why?
Her minion Atys putting of the shape of man, did dye,
And hardened intoo this same tree. Among this companee
Was present with a pyked top the Cypress, now a tree,
Sumtime a boay beloved of the God that with a string
Does arm his bow, and with a string in tune his Uiall bring.
For, hallowed too the Nymphs that in the feeldes of Carthye were
There was a goodly mighty Stag whose horns such breadth did bear,
As that they shadowed all his head. His horns of gold did shine,
And down his breast hung from his neck, a chyne with jewels fine.
Amid his fru^t with pretty strings a tablet being tide,
Did waver as he went: and from his cares on either side
Hung perles of all one growth about his hollow temples bright.
This goodly Spitter being void of dread, as having quyght
Forgot his native fearfulness, did haunt men's houses, and
Would suffer folk (yea though unknown) too coy him with their hand.
But more than unto all folk else he deerer was too thee
O Cyparisse the fairest Wight that ever man did see
In Coea. Thou too pastures, thou too water springs him led,
Thou wreathedst sundry flowers between his horns upon his head.
Sumtyme a horsman thou his back for pleasure did bestryde,
And haltring him with silken bit from place too place did ride.
In summer time about high noone when Titan with his heat
Did make the hollow crabbed cleas of Cancer for too sweat,
Unweeting Cyparissus with a Dart did strike this Heart
Quyght through. And when that of the wound he saw he must depart,
He purposd for too die himself. What words of comfort spoke
Not Phoebus too him? willing him the matter light too take
And not more sorrow for it than was requisite too make.
But still the Lad did sygh and sob, and as his last request
Desired God he might thenceforth from moorning never rest.
Anon through weeping overmuch his blood was drayned quyght:
His limbs were green: his hear which hung upon his forehead whyght
Began too be a bristled bush: and taking by and by
A stiffnesse, with sharpened top did face the starrie sky
The God did sigh, and sadly said. Myself shall moorne for thee,
And thou for others: and ay one in moorning thou shall be.
Such wood as this had Orphye drawn about him as among
The herdes of beasts, and flocks of Birds he sat amyds the throng.
And when his thumb sufficiently had tried every string,
And found that though they severally in sundry sounds did ring,
Yet made they all one Harmony. He thus began too sing.
O Muse my mother frame my song of Jove. for every thing
Is subject unto royal Jove. Of Jove the heavenly King
I often have showed the glorious power. I erst in grauer verse
The Giants slain in Phlaegra feeldes with thunder, did rehearse,
But now I need a meelder style too tell of pretty boys
That were the derlings of the Gods: and of unlawful joys
That burned in the breasts of Girls, who for their wicked lust
According as they did deserve, received penance just.
The King of Gods did burn erewhyle in love of Ganymed
The Phrygian and the thing was found which Jupiter that sted
Had rather be than that he was. Yet could ^e not beteeme
The shape of any other Bird than Aegle for too seem
And so he soring in the air with borrowed wings trust up
The Troiane boay who still in heaven even yet does bear his cup,
And brings him Nectar though against Dame Iunos will it be.
And thou Amyclys son (had not they heavy destinee
Abridged thee before thy time) had also placed been
By Phoebus in the firmament. How be it (as is seen)
Thou art eternal so far forth as may be. For as often
As warrie Piscis gives place too Aries that the soft
And gentle springtyde does succeed the winter sharp and stowre:
So often thou renewest thyself, and on the fair green clowre
Do shoot out flowers. My father bare a special love too thee
Above all others. So that while the God went often too see
Eurotas and unwalled Spart, he left his noble town
Of Delphos (which a mid the world is situate in renown)
Without a sovereign* Neither Harp nor Bow regarded were.
Unmyndfull of his Godhead he refused not too bear
The nets, nor for too hold the hounds, nor as a peynfull mate
Too travel over cragged hills, through which continual gate
His flames augmented more and more. And now the sun did stand
Well near midway between the nights last past and next at hand.
They stripped themselves and noynted them with oil of Olyfe fat.
And fell to throwing of a Sledge that was right howge and flat.
First Phoebus peysing it did throw it from him with such strength,
As that the weight drove down the clouds in flying. And at length
It fell upon substantial ground, where plainly it did show
As well the cunning as the force of him that did it throw.
Immediately upon desire himself the sport too try,
The Spartane lad made haste too take up unadvisedly
The Sledge before it still did lie. But as he was in hand
Too catch it, it rebounding up against the hardened land,
Did hit him full upon the face. The God himself did look
As pale as did the lad, and up his swounding body took.
Now culles he him, now wypes he from the wound the blood away,
Anotherwhyle his fading life he stryues with herbs too stay.
Naught booted Leechcraft. Helpless was the wound. And like as one
Broosd violet stalks or Poppie stalks or Lillies growing on
Brown spindles, straight they withering droope with heavy heads and are
Not able for too hold them up, but with their tops do stare
Upon the ground. So Hyacinth in yielding of his breath
Chopped down his head. His neck bereft of strength by means of death
Was even a burden too itself, and down did loosely wrythe
On both his shoulders, now a tone and now a toother lythe.
Thou faadst away my Hyacinth defrauded of the pryme
Of youth (quoth Phoebus) and I see thy wound my heinous crime.
Thou art my sorrow and my fault: this hand of my^e has wrought
Thy death: I like a murderer have too thy grave thee brought.
But what have I offended thou? unless that too have played,
Or if that too have loved, an offense it may be said.
Would God I render might my life with and in stead of thee.
Too which sith fatal destinee denies too agree,
Both in my mind and in my mouth thou evermore shall be.
My U^all stricken with my hand, my songs shall sound of thee,
And in a newmade flower thou shall with letters represent
Our syghings* And the time shall come ere many years be spent,
That in thy flower a valiant Prince shall join himself with thee,
And lea^e his name upon the leaves for men too reed and see.
While Phoebus thus did prophecy, behold the blood of him
Which did the grass, ceast blood too be, and up there sprang a trim
And goodly flower, more orient than the Purple cloth ingrayne,
In shape a Lillye, were it not that Lillyes do remain
Of silver color, whereas these of purple hew are seen.
Although that Phoebus had the cause of this great honor been,
Yet thought he not the same enough. And therefore did he wryght
His syghes upon the leaves thereof: and so in color bright
The flower has (...) writ thereon, which letters are of greef.
So small the Spartanes thought the birth of Hyacinth repree^
Unto them, that they woorship him from that day unto this.
And as their fathers did before, so they do never miss
With solemn pomp too celebrate his feast from year too year.
But if perchance that Amathus the rich in metals, weere
Demanded if it would have bred the Propets it would swear,
Yea even as gladly as the folk whose brewes sumtyme did bear,
A pair of welked horns: whereof they Cerastes named are.
Before their door an Altar stood of Jove that takes the care.
Of alyents and of travelers, which loathsome was too see,
For lewdness wrought thereon. If one that had a stranger be
Had looked thereon, he would have thought there had on it been killd
Sum sucking calves or lambs. The blood of strangers there was spilld.
Dame Venus sore offended at this wicked sacrifyse,
Too leave her Cities and the land of Cyprus did deuyse.
But then bethinking her, she said. What has my pleasant ground
What have my Cities trespassed? what fault in them is found?
Nay rather let this wicked race by exyle punnisht been,
Or death, or by sum other thing that is a mean between
Both death and exyle. what is that? save only for too change
Their shape. In musing with herself what figure were most strange,
She cast her eye upon a horn. And therewithal she thought
The same too be a shape right meet upon them too be brought:
And so she from their mighty limbs their native figure took,
And turned them intoo boystous Bulls with grim and cruel look
Yet durst the filthy Propets stand in stiff opinion that
Dame Venus was no Goddess till she being wroth thereat,
Too make their bodies common first compelld them euerychone
And after chaungd their former kind. For when that shame was gone,
And that they wexed brazen faast, she turned them too stone,
In which between their former shape was diffrence small or none.
Whom forbycause Pygmalion saw too lead their life in sin
Offended with the vice whereof great store is packt within
The nature of the womankynd, he led a single life.
And long it was ere he could find in heart too take a wife.
Now in the while by wondrous Art an image he did grave
Of such proportion, shape, and grace as nature never gave
Nor can too any woman give. In this his work he took
A certain love. The look of it was right a Maidens look,
And such a one as that ye would believe had life, and that
Would moved be, if womanhod and reverence let not:
So artificial was the work. He woondreth at his Art
And of his counterfetted corse conceyueth love in heart.
He often touched it, feeling if the woork that he had made
Were very flesh or Iuorye still. Yet could he not persuade
Himself too think it Iuory. For he oftentimes it kissed
And thought it kissed him ageine. He hild it by the fist,
And talked too it. He believed his fingars made a dint
Upon her flesh, and feared least sum black or broosed print
Should come by touching over hard. Sumtyme with pleasant boords
And wanton toys he dalyingly does cast forth amorous words.
Sumtime (the gifts wherein young Maids are wonted too delyght)
He brought her owches, fine round stones, and Lillyes fair and whygh^*
And pretty singing birds, and flowers of thousand sorts and hew,
And peynted ball^s, and Amber from the tree distilled new.
In gorgeous garments furthermore he did her also deck,
And on her fingars put me rings, and cheynes about her neck.
Rich perles were hanging at her ears, and tablets at her breast.
All kind of things became her well. And when she was undressed,
She s^emed not less beawtifull. He laid her in a bed
The which with scarle^ did in Tire was richly ouerspred,
And terming her (...) bedfellow, he couched down her head
Upon a (...) though she could have felt the same.
The feas^ (...) Venus hallowed through the I Will of Cyprus, cam^
And Bullocks whyght with gilden horns were slain for sacrifyse,
And up too heaven of frankincence the smoky fume did rise.
When as Pygmalion having doo^e his duty that same day,
Before the altar standing, thus with fearful heart did say.
If that you Gods can all things give, then let my wife (I pray)
(He durst not say be yoonsame wench of Iuory, but) be leeke
My wench of Iuory. Venus (who was naught at all to seek
What such a wish as that did meene) then present at her feast,
For handsell of her freendly help did cause three times at least
The fire to kindle and to spyre thryse upward in the air.
As Soon as he came home, straight way Pygmalion did repair
Unto the Image of his wench, and leaning on the bed,
Did kiss her. In her body straight a warmenesse seemd too spread.
He put his mouth again to hers, and on her breast did lay
His hand. The Iuory wexed soft: and putting quyght away
All hardness, yielded underneath his fingars, as we see
A piece of wax made soft against the Sun, or drawn too be
In diverse shapes by chaufing it between ones hands, and so
To serve to uses. He amazde stood wavering too and fro
Tweene joy, and fear too be beeguyld, ageine he burnt in love,
Ageine with feeling he began his wisshed hope too prove.
He felt it verrye flesh in deed. By laying on his thumb,
He felt her pulses beating. Then he stood no longer dumb
But thanked Venus with his heart. and at the length he laid
His mouth to hers who was as then become a perfect maid.
She felt the kiss, and blushed thereat: and lifting fearfully
Her eyelidds up, her Lover and the light at once did spy.
The marriage that her self had made the Goddess blessed so,
That when the Moon with fulsum light nine times her course had go,
This Lady was delivered of a Sun that Paphus hight,
Of whom the Island takes that name. Of him was borne a knight
Called Cinyras who (had he had none issue) surely might
Of all men underneath the sun been thought the happyest wight.
Of wicked and most cursed things to speak I now commence.
Ye daughters and ye parents all go get ye far from hence.
Or if ye minded be to here my tale, believe me naught
In this beehalfe: ne think that such a thing was ever wrought.
Or if ye will beeleeue the deed, believe the vengeance too
Which lyghted on the party that the wicked act did do.
But if that it be possible that any wight so much
From nature should degenerate, as for to fall to such
A heinous crime as this is, I am glad for Thracia, I
Am glad for this same world of ours, yea glad exceedingly
I am for this my native soil, for that there is such space
Between it and the land that bred a child so void of grace.
I would the land Panchaya should of Amomie be rich,
And Cinnamom, and Costus sweet, and Incence also which
Does issue largely out of trees, and other flowers strange,
As long as that it bears Myrrh: not worth it was the change,
New trees to have of such a price. The God of love denies
His weapons too have hurted thee, O Myrrha, and he tryes
Himself unguilty by thy fault. One of the Furies three
With poysonde Snakes and hellish brands has rather blasted thee
To hate ones father is a crime as heinous as may be.
But yet more wicked is this love of thine than any hate.
The youthful Lords of all the East and Peers of cheef estate
Desire to have thee too their wife, and earnest suit do make.
Of all (excepting only one) thy choice O Myrrha take.
She feels her filthye love, and stryues against it, and within
Herself said. whither roonnes my mind? what think I to begin?
Ye Gods (I pray) and godliness, ye holy rites and awe
Of parents, from this heinous crime my vicious mind withdraw,
And disappoynt my wickedness. At leastwyse if it be
A wickedness that I intend. As far as I can see,
This love infrindgeth not the bonds of godliness a whit.
For every other living wight dame nature does permit
Too match without offense of sin. The Hecfer thinks no shame
Too bear her father on her back: The Horse beestrydes the same
Of whom he is the sire: The Got does bucke the Kid that he
Himself begat: and birds do tread the self same birds we see
Of whom they hatched were before. In happy case they are
That may do so without offense. But man's malicious care
Has made a bridle for it self, and spyghtfull laws restreyne
The things that nature sets free. yet are their Realms (men said)
In which the moother with the son, and daughter with the father
Do match, where through of godliness the bond augments the rather
With doubled love. Now woe is me it had not been my lot
In that same country too be borne. And that this lucklesse plot
Should hinder me. Why think I thus? Auaunt unlawful love.
I ought too love him I confess: but so as does behoue
His daughter: were not Cinyras my father then, Iwis
I might obtain too lie with him. But now because he is
Mine own, he cannot be mine own. The neerenesse of our kin
Does hurt me. Were I further of perchance I more might win.
And if I wist that I thereby this wickedness might shun,
I would forsake my native soil and far from Cyprus run.
This evil hea^e does hold me back, that being present still
I may but talk with Cinyras and look on him my fill,
And touch, and kiss him, if no more may further granted be.
Why wicked wench? and can thou hope for further? do not see
How by thy fault thou do confound the ryghts of name and kin?
And will thou make thy mother be a Cucqueane by thy sin?
Will thou thy fathers leman be? will thou be both the moother
And suster of thy child? shall he be both thy son and brother?
And standst thou not in fear at all of those same susters three
Whose heads with crawling snakes in stead of hear bematted be?
Which pushing with their cruel bronds folks eyes and mouths, do see
Their sinful hearts? but thou now while thy body yet is free,
Let never such a wickedness once enter in thy mind.
Defyle not mighty natures hest by lust against thy kind.
What though thy will were fully bent? yet even the very thing
Is such as will not suffer thee the same too end too bring.
For why he being well disposde and godly, myndeth ay
So much his duty that from right and truth he will not stray.
Would God like fury were in him as is in me this day.
This said, her father Cinyras (who dowted what too do
By reason of the worthy store of suitors which did woo
His daughter,) bringing all their names did will her for too show
On which of them she had herself most fancy too bestow.
At first she hild her peace a while, and looking wistly on
Her fathers face, did boil within: and scalding tears anon
Ran down her visage. Cyniras, (who thought them too proceed
Of tender hearted shamefastness) did say there was no need
Of tears, and dried her cheeks, and kissed her. Myrrha took of it
Exceeding pleasure in her self: and when that he did wit
What husband she did wish too have, she said: one like too you.
He understanding not her thought, did well her words allow.
And said: in this thy godly mind continue. At the name
Of godliness, she cast me down her look for very shame.
For why her guilty heart did know she well deserved blame.
High mydnight came, and sleep both care and carkesses oppressed.
But Myrrha lying broad awake could neither sleep nor rest.
She fryes in Cupids flames, and works continewally upon
Her furious love. One while she sinkes in deep despair. Anon
She fully minds to give attempt, but shame does hold her in.
She wishes and she wotes not what too do, nor how too gin.
And like as when a mighty tree with axes heawed rownd,
Now reedye with a strype or twain to lie upon the grownd,
Uncerteine is which way to fall and tottreth every way:
Even so her mind with dowtfull wound effeebled then did stray
Now here now there vncerteinely, and took of both encreace.
No measure of her love was found, no rest, nor yet releace,
Save only death. death likes her best. She ryseth, full in mind
To hang herself. About a post her girdle she does bynd.
And said farewell dear Cinyras, and understand the cause
Of this my death. And with that word about her neck she draws
The nooze. Her trustye nurce that in another Chamber lay
By fortune heard the whispring sound of these her words (folk say.)
The aged woman rising up vnboltes the door. And when
She saw her in that plyght of death, she shreeking out began
Too smyght her self, and scratcht her breast, and quickly too her ran
And rent the girdle from her neck. Then weeping bitterly
And holding her between her arms, she asked the question why
She went about too hang her self so unadvisedly.
The Lady hilld her peace as dumb, and looking on the ground
Unmouably, was sorye in her heart for being found
Before she had dispatched herself. Hernurce still at her lay,
And showing her her empty dugges and naked head all gray,
Besought her for the pains she took with her both night and day
In rocking and in feeding her, she would vouchsafe to say
What ere it were that grieved her. The Lady turned away
Displeasde and fetched a sygh. The nurce was fully bent in mind
Too bowlt the matter out: for which not only she did bynd
Her faith, in secret things to keep: but also said, put me
In trust too find a remedy. I am not (thou shall see)
Yet altoogither dulld by age. If furiousenesse it be,
I have both charms and chaunted herbs to help. If any wight
Bewitcheth thee, by witchcraft I will purge and set thee quyght.
Or if it be the wrath of God, we shall with sacrifyse
Appease the wrath of God right well. What may I more surmyse?
No thieves have broken in upon this house and spoyld the wealth.
Thy mother and thy father both are living and in health.
When Myrrha heard her father naamd, a grievous sygh she fet
Even from the bottom of her heart. Howbeet the nurce as yet
Misdeemd not any wickedness. But nerethelesse she gest
There was some love: and standing in one purpose made request
Too break her mind unto her, And she set her tenderly
Upon her lap. The Lady wept and sobbed bitterly.
Then culling her in feeble arms, she said I well espy
Thou art in love. My diligence in this behalf I swear
Shall seruisable too th^e be. Thou shall not need too fear
That ere thy father shall it know. At that same word she lept
From nurces lap like one that had been past her wits, and stepped
With fury to her bed. at which she leaning down her face
Said, hence I pray thee: force me not to show my shameful case.
And when the nurce did urge her still, she answered either get
The hence, or ceace too ask me why myself I thus do fret.
The thing that thou desyrste too know is wickedness. The old
Poor nurce began quake, and trembling both for age and fear did hold
Her hands to her. And kneeling down right humbly at her feet,
One while she fair entreated her with gentle words and sweet.
Another while (unless she made her privy of her sorrow)
She threatened her, and put her in a fear she would next morrow
Bewray her how she went about to hang herself. But if
She told her, she did plyght her faith and help too her releef.
She lifted up her head, and then with tears fast gusshing out
Beesloobered all her nurces breast: and going often about
Too speak, she often stayed: and with her garments hid her face
For shame, and lastly said: O happy is my moothers case
That such a husband has. with that a grievous sygh she gave,
And hilld her peace. These words of hers a trembling ch^lnesse drove
In nurcis limbs, which perst her bones: (for now she understood
The case) and all her horye hear up stiff^y staring stood
And many things she talked to put away her cursed love,
If that it had been possible the madness to remove.
The Maid herself to be full true the council does espy:
Yet if she may not have her love she fully minds to dye.
Live still (quod nurce) thou shall obtain (she durst not say thy father,
But stayed at that.) And forbycause that Myrrha should the rather
Believe her, she confirmd her words by oath. The yearly feast
Of gentle Ceres came, in which the wives both most and least
Appareld all in whyght are wo^nt the firstlings of the feeld
Fine garlonds made of ears of corn too Ceres for to yield.
And for the space of thryce three nights they counted it a sin
To have the use of any man, or once too towche his s^in.
Among these women did the Queen freequent the secret rites.
Now while that of his lawful wife his bed was void a nights,
The nurce was dooble diligent: and fynding Cinyras
Well washed with wine, she did surmyse there was a pretye less
In love with him. And hyghly she her beawty sets out.
And being asked of her years, she said she was about
The age of Myrrha. well (quod he) then bring her too my bed.
Returning home she said: be glad my nurcechilde: we have sped.
Not all so wholly in her heart was wretched Myrrha glad,
But that her fore misgiuing mind did also make her sad.
Howbeete she also did rejoice as in a certain kind,
Such discord of affections was within her combred mind.
It was the time that all things rest. And now Boots bright
The driuer of the Oxen seven about the northpole pyght.
Had sumwhat turned his wayne aside, when wicked Myrrha sped
About her buysnesse. Out of heaven the golden Phoebee fled.
With clouds more black than any pitch the stars did hyde their head.
The night beecommeth utter void of all her woonted light.
And first before all other hid their faces out of sight
Good Icar and Erigonee his daughter, who for love
Most virtuous too her fatherward, was take up above
And made a star in heaven. Three times had Myrrha warning given
By stumbling, to retire. Three times the deathfull Owl that eeuen
With doolefull noise prognosticates unhappy luck. Yet came
She forward still: the darkness of the night abated shame.
Her left hand held her nurce, her right the dark blind way did grope.
Anon she too the chamber came: anon the door was open:
Anon she entered in. with that her foltring hammes did quake:
Her color did: her blood and heart did clearly her forsake.
The nearer she approached too her wickedness, the more
She trembled: Of her enterprise it irked her full sore:
And fayn she would she might vnknowne^ have turned back. Nurce led
Her pawsing forward by the hand: and putting her too bed,
Here take this Damzell Cinyras, she is thine own she sed.
And so she laid them breast too breast. The wicked father takes
His bowelles intoo filthy bed, and there with words asslakes
The maidens fear, and cheeres her up. And least this crime of theyres
Might want the ryghtfull terms, by chance as in respect of years
He daughter did her call, and she him father. Being sped
With cursed seed in wicked womb, she left her fathers bed,
Of which soon after she became great bagged with her shame.
Next night the lewdness doubled. And no end was of the same,
Until at length that Cinyras desirous for to know
His lover that so many nights upon him did bestow,
Did fetch a light: by which he saw his own most heinous crime,
And eeke his daughter. nathelesse, his sorrow at that time
Represt his speech. Then hanging by he drew a Rapier bright.
Away ran Myrrha, and by means of darkness of the night
She was delivered from the death: and straying in the broad
Datebearing feeldes of Arabye, she through Panchaya yode,
And wandering full nine moonethes at length she rested being tyrde
In Saba land. And when the time was near at hand expyrde,
And that uneath the burden of her womb sh^e well could bear,
Not knowing what she might desire, distressed between the fear
Of death, and tediousness of life, this prayer she did make.
O Gods, if of repentant folk you any mercy take,
Sharp vengeance I confess I have deserved, and content
I am to take it patiently. How be it too thentent
That neither with my life the quick, nor with my death the dead
Anoyed be, from both of them exempt me this same sted.
And altring me, deny too me both life and death. we see
Too such as do confess their faults sum mercy showed ^oo be.
The Gods did grant her this request, the last that (...) make.
The ground did ouergrow her feet, and ancles as she spoke.
And from her bursten toes went roots, which wrything here and there
Did fasten so the trunk within the ground she could not steare.
Her bones did intoo timber turn, whereof the marie was
The pith, and into watrish sap the blood of her did pass.
Her arms were turned too greater boughs, her fingars into twig,
Her skin was hardened into bark. And now her belly big
The eatching tree had ouergrowen, and ouertane her breast,
And hasted for to win her neck, and hyde it with the rest.
She made no taryence nor delay, but met the coming tree,
And shroonk her face within the bark thereof. Although that she
Toogither with her former shape her senses all did loose,
Yet weepeth she, and from her tree warm drops do softly woose.
The which her tears are had in price and honor. And the Myrrh
That issues from her gummy bark does bear the name of her,
And shall do while the world does last. The misbegotten child
Grew still within the tree, and from his mothers womb defyld
Sought means too be delivered. Her burdened womb did swell
Amid the tree, and stretched her out. But words wherewith to tell
And utter forth her greef did want, She had no use of speech
With which Lucina in her throws she might of help beseech.
Yet like a woman labring was the tree, and bowwing down
Gave often sighs, and shed forth tears as though she there should drown.
Lucina to this woeful tree came gently down, and laid
Her hand thereon, and speaking words of ease the midwife played.
The tree did cranye, and the bark dividing made away,
And yielded out the child alive, which cryde and wayld straight way.
The waternymphes upon the soft sweet herbs the child did lay,
And bathde him with his mothers tears. His face was such as spyght
Must needs have praysd. For such he was in all conditions right,
As are the naked Cupids that in tables picturde be.
But too thentent he may with them in every point agree,
Let either him be furnisshed with wings and quiver light,
Or from the Cupids take their wings and bows and arrows quight.
Away slippes fleeting time vnspyde and mocks us too our face,
And nothing may compare with years in swiftness of their pace*
That wretche^^ (...) whom wickedly his graundfather begat,
And whom his cursed suster bare, who hidden was alate
Within the tree, and lately borne, became immediately
The beawtyfullyst babe on whom man ever set his eye.
Anon a stripling he became, and by and by a man,
And every day more beawt^full than other he becam.
That in the end Dame Venus fell in love with him: whereby
He did revenge the outrage of his mothers villanye.
For as the armed Cupid kissed Dame Venus, vnbeware
An arrow sticking out did raze her breast upon the bare.
The Goddess being wounded, ^hrust away her son. The wound
Apyeered not too be so deep as afterward was found.
It did deceive her at the first. The beawty of the lad
Inflaamd her. Too Cythera I Will no mind at all she had.
Nor unto Paphos where the sea beats round about the shore,
Nor (...) Gnyde, nor Amathus that has of metalls store.
Yea ^uen from heaven she did absteyne. She lovd Adonis more
Than heaven* To him she clinged ay, and bare him company.
And in the shadow wont she was too rest continually,
And for too set her beawtye out most seemly too the eye
By trimly decking of her self. Through ^ushy grounds and groves,
And over H^lls and Dales, and Lawn^s and stony rocks she roues,
Bare kneed with garment tucked up according too the wont
Of Phebe, and she cheerd the hounds with hallowing like a hunt,
Pursewing game of hurtlesse sort, as Hares made low before.
Or stagges with loftye heads, or bucks. But with the sturdy Boar
And ra^ening woolf, and Bearewhelpes armd with ugly paws, and eeke
The cruel Lions which delyght in blood, and slaughter seek,
She meddled not. And of these same she warned also thee
Adonis for too shoonne them, if thou wooldst have warned be.
Be bold on cowards ( Venus said) for whosoever does advance
Himself against the bold, may hap too meet with sum mischaunce.
Wherefore I pray thee my sweet boy forbear too bold too be.
For fear thy rashness hurt thy self and woork the woe of me
Encounter not the kind of beasts whom nature armed has,
For dowt thou buy thy praise too dear procuring thee sum scath.
Thy tender youth, thy beawty bright, thy countnance fair and brave
Although they had the force too win the heart of Venus, have
No power against the Lions, nor against the brist led swyne.
The eyes and hearts of savage beasts do naught too these m^lyne.
The cruel Boares bear thunder in their hooked tushes, and
Exceeding force and feercenesse is in Lions too withstand.
And sure I hate them at my heart. Too him demanding why?
A monstrous chance (quod Venus) I will tell thee by and by,
That happened for a fault. But now unwonted toil has made
Me weerye: and behold, in time this Poplar with his shade
Allureth, and the ground for cowch does serve too rest upon.
I prey thee let us rest here. They sat them down anon.
And lying upward with her head upon his lap along,
She thus began: and in her tale she bussed him among.
Perchance thou have or this time hard of one that overcame
The swiftest men in footemanshippe. no fable was that fame.
She overcame them out of dowt. And hard it is to tell
Thee whither she did in footemanshippe or beawty more excel.
Upon a season as she asked of Phoebus, what he was
That should her husband be, he said. For husband do not pass
O Atalanta, thou at all of husband have no need
Shonne husbanding. But yet thou can not shonne it I thee reed.
Alive thou shall not be thy self. She being sore afraid
Of this Apollos Oracle, did keep herself a maid,
And lived in the shady woods. When wooers to her came,
And were of her importunate, she drove away the same
With boystous words, and with sore condition of the game.
I am not too be had (quod she) unless ye able b^e
In ronning for too vanquish me. Ye must contend with me
In footemanshippe. And who so winnes the wager, I agree
Too be his wife. But if that he be found too slow, then he
Shall lose his head. This of your game the verrye law shall be.
She was in deed unmerciful. But such is beawties power,
That though the said condition were extreme and over sour,
Yet many suitors were so rash too undertake the same.
Hippomenes as a looker on of this discourteous game,
Sat by, and said. Is any man so mad to seek a wife
With such apparent peril and the hazard of his life?
And utterly he did condemn the yongmens love. But when
He saw her face and body bare, (for why the Lady then
Did strippe her too her naked skin) the which was like too mine,
Or rather (if that thou were made a woman) like too thine:
He was amazde. And holding up his hands too heaven, he says:
Forgive me you with whom I found such fault even now: In faith
I did not know the wager that ye ran for. As he prayseth
The beawty of her, in him self the fire of love he rayseth.
And through an envy fearing least she should a way be woonne,
He wished that nere a one of them so swift as she might roonne.
And wherefore (quod he) put not I myself in preace too try
The fortune of this wager? God himself continually
Does help the bold and hardye sort. now while Hippomenes
Debates these things within himself and other like to these,
The Damzell ronnes as if her feet were wings. And though that she
Did fly as swift as arrow from a Turkye bow: yet he
More woondred at her beawtye than at swiftness of her pace
Her ronning greatly did augment her beawtye and her grace.
The wynd ay whisking from her feet the labells of her socks
Upon her back as whyght as snow did toss her golden locks,
And eeke thembroydred garters that were tide beneathe her ham.
A redness mixed with whyght upon her tender body came,
As when a scarlet curtain streynd against a playstred wall
Does cast like shadow, making it seem ruddye therwithall.
Now while the stranger noted this, the race was fully run,
And Atalant (as she that had the wager clearly won)
Was crowned with a garlond brave. The vanquished sighing sore,
Did lose their lives according too agreement made before.
Howbeeit naught at all dismayed with these men's lucklesse case
He stepped forth, and looking full upon the maidens face,
Said: wherefore do thou seek renown in vanquisshing of such
As were but dastards? cope with me. If fortune be so much
My friend too give me victory, thou need not hold scorn
Too yield too such a noble man as I am. I am borne
The son of noble Megaree Onchestyes son, and he
Was son to Neptune. Thus am I great graundchyld by degree
In right descent, of him that rules the waters. Neither do
I out of kind degenerate from virtue meet thertoo.
Or if my fortune be so hard as vanquished for too be,
Thou shall obtain a famous name by ouercomming me.
In saying thus, Atlanta cast a gentle look on him
And dowting whither she rather had too lose the day or win,
Said thus. What God an enmy to the beawtyfull, is bent
Too bring this person to his end, and therefore has him sent
Too seek a wife with hazard of his life? If I should be
Myself the judge in this behalf, there is not sure in me
That does deserve so dearly too be earned. neither does
His beawty move my heart at all. yet is it such in sooth
As well might move me. But because as yet a child he is,
His person mooues me not so much as does his age Iwis.
Beesydes that manhod is in him, and mind vnfrayd of death:
Beesydes that of the watrye race from Neptune as he seth
He is the fowrth: beesydes that he does love me, and does make
So great account too win me too his wife, that for my sake
He is contented for too dye, if fortune be so sore
Against him too deny him me. Thou stranger hence therefore.
Away I say now while thou may, and shonne my bloody bed.
My marriage cruel is, and craves the losing of thy head.
There is no wench but that would such a husband gladly catch.
And she that wise were might desire too meet with such a match.
But why now after heading of so many, do I care
For thee? Look thou too that. For since so many men as are
Already put too slawghter can not warn thee too beeware,
But that thou will be weerye of thy life, dye: do not spare.
And shall he perrish then because he sought to live with me?
And for his love unworthily with death rewarded be?
All men of such a victory will speak too foul a shame.
But all the world can testifye that I am not too blame.
Would God thou would desist. Or else because thou are so mad,
I would too God a little more thy feet of swiftness had.
Ah what a maidens countenance is in this chyldish face?
Ah foolish boy Hippomenes, how wretched is thy case?
I would thou never had me seen. Thou worthy art of life.
And if so be I happy were, and that too be a wife
The cruel destnyes had not me forbidden, sure thou art
The only wight with whom I would be matched with all my heart.
This spoken: she yet raw, and but new stricken with the dart
Of Cupid, being ignorant, did love and knew it not
Anon her father and the folk assembled, willed that
They should begin their woonted race. Then Neptunes issue prayed
With careful heart and voice too me, and thus d^uoutly said.
O Venus, favor mine attempt, and send me down thine aid
Too compass my desired love which thou have on me laid.
His prayer movd me (I confess,) and long I not delayed
Before I helped him. Now there is a certain feeld the which
The Cyprian folk call Damasene, most fertile and most rich
Of all the Cyprian feelds: the same was consecrate too me
In ancient time, and of my Church the glebland wont too be.
Amid this feeld, with golden leaves there grows a goodly tree
The crackling boughs whereof are all of yellow gold. I came
And gathered golden Apples three: and bearing thence the same
Within my hand, immediately too Hippomen I gat
Invisible too all wyghts else save him and taught him what
Too do with them. The Trumpets blew: and girding forward, both
Set forth, and on the houering dust with nimble feet each goth.
A man would think they able were upon the Sea too go
And never wet their feet, and on the ails of corn also
That still is growing in the feeld, and never down them tread.
The man took courage at the showt and words of them that sed,
Now now is time Hippomenes too ply it, hye a pace:
Enforce thyself with all thy strength: lag not in any case:
Thou shall obtain. It is a thing right dowtfull whither he
At these well willing words of theyrs reioysed more, or she.
O Lord how often when she might outstrippe him did she stay,
And gazed long upon his face, right loath too go her way?
A weerye breath proceeded from their parched lips, and far
They had too run. Then Neptunes imp her swiftness too disbarre,
Trolld down a toneside of the way an Apple of the three.
Amazde thereat, and covetous of the goodly Apple, she
Did step aside and snatched up the rolling fruit of gold.
With that Hippomenes coted her. The folk that did behold,
Made noise with clapping of their hands. She recompenst her slothe
And loss of* time with footemanshippe: and straight ageine outgothe
Hippomenes, leaving him behind, and being stayed again
With taking up the second, she him ouertooke. And when
The race was almost at an end: He said: O Goddess, thou
That art the author of this gift, assist me freendly now,
And therwithall, of purpose that she might the longer be
In coming, he with all his might did bowl the last of three
A skew a toneside of the feelde. The Lady seemde too make
A dowt in taking of it up. I forced her too take
It up, and too the Apple I did put a heavy weight,
And made it of such massinesse she could not lift it straight.
And least that I in telling of my tale may longer be,
Than they in ronning of their race, outstripped quight was she.
And he that wan her, marying her enjoyed her for his fee.
Think thou I was not worthy thanks, Adonis thinks thou
I earned not that he too me should frankincence allow?
But he forgetful neither thanks nor frankincence did give.
By means whereof too sooden wrath he justly did me drive.
For being grieved with the spyght, because I would not be
Despysd of such as were too come, I thought it best for me
Too take such vengeance of them both as others might take heed
By them. And so against them both in anger I proceed.
A temple of the mother of the Gods that vowwed was
And builded by Echion in a darksome grove, they pass
There through my might Hippomenes was touched and stirred so,
That needs he would too Uenerie though out of season go.
Not far from this same temple was with little light aden
With pommye vawlted naturally, long consecrate ere then
For old religion, not unlike a cave: where priests of yore
Bestowed had of Images of wooden Gods good store.
Hippomenes entering herintoo defyld the holy place.
With his unlawful lust: from which the Idols turned their face.
And Cybell with the towred tops disdeyning, dowted whither
She in the lake of Styx might drown the wicked folk toogither.
The penance seemed over light. and therefore she did cawse
Thin yellow manes to grow upon their necks: and hooked paws
In stead of fingars too succeed. Their shoulders were the same
They were before: with woondrous force deep brested they beecame.
Their look beecame feerce, cruel, grim, and sour: a tufted tail
Stretched out in length far after them upon the ground does trayle.
In stead of speech they rore: in stead of bed they haunt the wood:
And dreadful unto others they for all their cruel mood
With tamed teeth chank Cybells bitts in shape of Lions. Shonne
These beasts dear heart: and not from these alonely see thou run,
But also from each other beast that turns not back too flight
But offreth with his boystows breast too try the chance of fight:
Anemis least thy valeantnesse be hurtful to us both.
This warning given, with yoked swannes away through air she goth.
But manhod by admonishment restreyned could not be.
By chance his hounds in following of the tracke, a Boar did see,
And rowsed him. And as the swyne was coming from the wood,
Adonis hit him with a dart a skew, and drew the blood.
The Boar straight with his hooked groyne ye huntingstaffe out drew
Bestayned with his blood, and on Adonis did pursew
Who trembling and retyring back, too place of refuge drew.
And hyding in his codds his tuskes as far as he could thrust
He laid him all along for dead upon the yellow dust.
Dame Venus in her chariot drawn with swannes was scarce arrived
At Cyprus, when she knew a far the sygh of him depryued
Of life. She turned her Cygnets back. and when she from the sky
Beehilld him dead, and in his blood beweltred for to lie:
She leaped down, and tare at once her garments from her brist,
And rent her hear, and beat upon her stomach with her fist,
And blaming sore the destnyes, said. Yet shall they not obtain
Their will in all things. Of my grief remembrance shall remain.
( Adonis) while the world does last. From year too year shall grow
A thing that of my heaviness and of thy death shall show
The lively likeness. In a flower thy blood I will bestow.
Had thou the power Persephonee rank sented Mints too make
Of women's limbs? and may not I like power upon me take
Without disdeine and spyght, too turn Adonis too a flower?
This sed, she sprinkled Nectar on the blood, which through the power
Thereof did swell like bubbles sheere that rise in weather clear
On water. And before that full an hour expyred weere,
Of all one color with the blood a flower she there did find
Even like the flower of that same tree whose fruit in tender rynde
Have pleasant graynes inclosd^. Howbeet the use of them is short.
For why the leaves do hang so looce through lightness in such sort,
As that the winds that all things perce, with every little blast
Do shake them of and shed them so as that they cannot last.
Finis decimi Libri.
THE.XI. BOOK OF Ouids Metamorphosis.
NOw while the Thracian Poet with this song delyghts y^ mynds
Of savage beasts, and draws both stones and trees against their kynds,
Behold the wives of Ciconie with reddeerskinnes about
Their furious brists as in the feeld they gadded on a rout,
Espyde him from a hillocks top still singing too his harp.
Of whom one shook her head at him, and thus began to carp.
Behold (says she) behold yoonsame is he that does disdeine
Us women. And with that same word she sent her lawnce amayne
At Orphyes singing mouth. The Lawnce armd round about with leaves,
Did hit him, and without a wound a mark behynd it leaves.
Another threw a stone at him, which vanquished with his sweet
And most melodius harmonye, fell humbly at his feet
As sorye for the furious act it purposed. But rash
And heady riot out of frame all reason now did dash,
And frantik outrage reigned. Yet had the sweetness of his song
Appeasd all weapons, saving that the noise now growing strong
With blowing shalmes, and beating drums, and bedlem howling out,
And clapping hands on every side by Bacchus drunken rout,
Did drown the sownd of Orphyes harp Then first of all stones were
Made ruddy with the prophets blood, and could not give him ear.
And first the flock of Bacchus froes by violence brake the ring
Of Serpents, birds, and savage beasts that for to here him sing
Sat gazing round about him there. And then with bluddy hands
They ran upon the prophet who among them singing stands.
They flockt about him like as when a sort of birds have found,
An Owl a day times in a tod: and hem him in full round,
As when a Stag by hungrye hownds is in a morning found,
The which forestall him round about and pull him to the ground.
Even so the prophet they assail, and throw their Thyrses green
At him, which for another use than that invented been.
Sum cast me clods, sum boughs of trees, and sum threw stones. And least
That weapon wherwithall too wreake their woodnesse which increast
Should want, it chaunst that Oxen by were tilling of the ground
And labring men with brawned arms not far fro thence were found
A digging of the hardened earth, and earning of their food,
With sweating brows. They seeing this same rout, no longer stood,
But ran away and left their tools behynd them. Every where
Through all the feeld their mattocks, rakes, and shouells scattred were.
Which when the cruel feends had caught, and had a sunder rent
The horned Oxen, back ageine to Orphy ward they went,
And (wicked wights) they murthred him, who never till that hour
Did utter words in vain, nor sing without effectual power.
And through that mouth of his (oh lord) which even the stones had heard,
And unto which the witless beasts had often given regard,
His ghost then breathing intoo air, departed. Even the fowls
Were sad for Orphye, and the beast with sorye syghing howles:
The rugged stones did moorne for him, the woods which many a time
Had followed him too here him sing, bewailed this same crime.
Yea even the trees lamenting him did cast their leauy hear
The rivers also with their tears (men say) increased were.
Yea and the Nymphs of brooks and woods upon their streams did sail
With scattred hear about their ears, in boats with sable sail.
His members lay in sundry steds. His head and harp both came
To Hebrus. and (a woondrous thing) as down the stream they swam,
His Harp did yield a moorning sound: his liuelesse ^oong did make
A certain lamentable noise as though it still yet spoke,
And both the banks in moorning wise made answer too the same.
At length a down their country stream too open sea they came,
And lyghted on Methymnye shore in Lesbos land. There
No sooner on the foreign coast now cast a land they were,
But that a cruel naturde Snake did straight upon them fly,
And licking on his ruffled hear the which was dropping dry,
Did gape too tire upon those lips that had been wont to sing
Most heavenly hymns. But Phoebus straight preventing y^ same thing,
Dispoynts the Serpent of his bit, and turns him into stone
With gaping chappes* Already was the Ghost of Orphye gone
To Plutos realm, and there he all the places ^ft beehilld
The which he heretoofore had seen. And as he sought the feeld
Of fair Elysion (where the ^oules of godly folk do woonne,)
He ^ound his wife Eury dicee, to whom he straight did roonne
And hilld her in embracing arms. There now he one while walks
Toogither with her cheek by cheek: another while he stalks
Before her. and another while he follows her. And now
Without all kind of forfeyture he saufly might a^ow
His looking bakward at his wife. But Bacchus grieved at
The murder of the Chapleine of his Orgies, suffered not
The mischeef vnreuengd too be. For by and by he bound,
The Thracian women by the feet with writhen root in ground,
As many as consenting too this wicked act were found.
And look how much that each of them the prophet did pursew,
So much he sharpening of their toes, within the ground them drew.
And as the bird that fynds her leg besnarled in the net
The which the fowlers suttlelye has clocely for her set,
And feels she cannot get away, stands flickering with her wings,
And with her fearful leaping up draws clocer still the strings:
So each of these when in the ground they fastened were, assayd
Aflayghted for to fly away. But every one was stayed
With winding root which hilld her down. her frisking could not boot.
And while she lookte what was become of To, of nail, and foot,
She saw her legs grow round in one, and turning intoo wood.
And as her thyghes with violent hand she sadly striking stood,
She felt them tree: her breast was tree: her shoulders eeke were tree.
Her arms long boughs ye might have thought, and not deceived be.
But Bacchus was not so content: he quyght forsook their land:
And with a better company removed out of hand
Unto the Uyneyarde of his own mount Tmolus, and the river
Pactolus though as yet no streams of gold it did deliver,
Ne spyghted was for precious sands. His old accustomd rout
Of woodwards and of frantic froes enuyrond him about.
But old Silenus was away. The Phrygian ploughmen found
Him reeling both for droonkennesse and age, and brought him bound
With garlands, unto Midas king of Phrygia, unto whom
The Thracian Orphye and the priest Eumolphus coming from
The town of Athens erst had taught the Orgies. When he knew
His fellow and companion of the self same badge and crew.
Upon the coming of this guest, he kept a feast the space
Of twyce fine days and twyce five nights toogither in that place
And now theleuenth time Lucifer had mustred in the sky
The heavenly host, when Midas commes too Lydia iocundly
And yeeldes the old Silenus too his fosterchyld. He glad
That he his fosterfather had eftsoons recovered, bad
King Midas ask him what he would. Right glad of that was he,
But not a whit at latter end the better should he be.
He minding too misuse his gifts, said: grant that all and some
The which my body towcheth bare may yellow gold become.
God Bacchus granting his request, his hurtful gift performd,
And that he had not better wished he in his stomach stormd.
Rejoicing in his harm away full merye goes the king:
And for too try his promis true he towcheth every thing.
Scarce giving credit too himself, he pulled young grieve twiggs
From of an Holmetree: by and by all golden were the spriggs
He took a flintstone from the ground, the stone likewise became
Pure gold. He towched next a cold of earth, and straight the same
By force of towching did become a wedge of yellow gold.
He gathered ears of rypened corn: immediately behold
The corn was gold. An Apple then he pulled from a tree:
Ye would have thought the Hesperids had given it him. If he
On Pillars high his fingars laid, they glistred like the son.
The water where he washed his hands did from his hands so run,
As Danae might have been therewith beguyld. He scarce could hold
His passing joys within his heart, for making all things gold.
While he thus ioyd, his officers did spread the board anon,
And set down sundry sorts of meat and mancheate thervppon.
Then whither his hand did towch the bread, the bread was massy gold:
Or whither he chawde with hungry teeth his meat, ye might behold
The piece of meat between his ^awes a plat of gold too be.
In drinking wine and water mixed, ye might discern and see
The liquid gold run down his throat. Amazed at the strange
Mischaunce, and being both a wretch and rich, he wished too change
His riches for his former state, and now he did abhor
The thing which even but late before he chiefly longed for.
No meat his hunger slakes: his throat is shrunken up with thurst:
And justly does his hateful gold torment him as accursed.
Then lifting up his sorry arms and hands too heaven, he cryde:
O father Bacchus pardon me. My sin I will not hyde.
Have mercy I beseech thee and vouchsauf too rid me quyght
From this same harm that seems so good and glorious unto sight.
The gentle Bacchus straight upon confession of his crime
Restored Midas too the state he had in former time.
And having made performance of his promis, he beereft him
The gift that he had granted him. And least he should have left him
Beedawbed with the dregs of that same gold which wickedly
He wisshed had, he willed him too get him by and by
Too that great river which does run by Sardis town, and there
Along the channel up the stream his open arms to bear
Until he comes too the spring: and then his head too put
Full underneath the foming spowt where greatest was the gut,
And so in wasshing of his limbs too wash away his crime.
The king (as was commanded him) against the stream did clyme.
And straight the power of making gold departing quyght from him,
Infects the river, making it with golden stream too swim.
The force whereof the banks about so soked in their veins,
That even as yet the yellow gold upon the cloddes remaynes.
Then Midas hating riches haunts the pasturegrounds and groves,
And up and down with Pan among the Lawnds and mountains roues.
But still a head more fat than wise, and dol^^sh wit he has,
The which as erst, yet once again must woork their master scath.
The mountain Tmole from loftye top too seaward looks down,
And spreading far his boorely sides, extends too the town
Of Sardis with the tonesyde and too Hypep with the toother.
There Pan among the fayrye elues that dawneed round toogither
In setting of his conning out for singing and for play
Upon his pype of reeds and war, presuming for too say*
Apollos music was not like too his, did take in hand
A far unequal match, whereof the Tmole for judge should stand.
The ancient judge sitts down upon his hill, and ridds his ears
From trees, and only on his head an Oken garlo^d wears,
Whereof the Acornes dangled down about his hollow brow.
And looking on the God of n^ate he said: ye need not now
Too tarry longer for your judge. Then Pan blew loud and strong
His country pype of reeds, and with his rude and homely song
Delighted Midas ears, for he by chance was in the throng.
When Pan had done, the sacred Tmole too Phoebus turned his look,
And with the turning of his head his busshye hear he shook.
Then Phoebus with a crown of ^ay upon his golden hear
Did sweep the ground with scarlet robe. In left hand he did bear
His vial made of precious stones and Iuorye intermirt.
And in his right hand for too strike, his bow was reedy fixed.
He was the verrye pattern of a good Musician right
Anon he began with conning hand the tuned strings too smyght.
The sweetness of the which did so the judge of them delyght,
That Pan was willed for to put his Reedepype in his case,
And not too fiddle nor too sing where vialls were in place.
The judgment of the holy hill was lyked well of all,
Save Midas, who found fault therewith and wrongful did it call.
Apollo could not suffer well his foolish ears too keep
Their human shape, but drew them wide, and made them long and deep.
And filld them full of whytish hears, and made them down too sag.
And through too much unstableness continually too wag.
His body keeping in the rest his manly figure still,
Was ponnisht in the part that did offend for want of skill.
And so a slow paaste Asses ears his head did after bear.
This shame endeuereth he too hyde. And therefore he did wear
A purple nyghtcappe ever since. But yet his Barber who
Was wont too notte him spy^d it: and being eager too
Disclose it, when he neither durst too utter it, nor could
It keep in secret still, he went and dug up the mowld,
And whispring softly in the pit, declaard what ears he spyde
His master have, and turning down the clowre ageine, did hyde
His blabbed words within the ground, and closing up the pit
Departed thence and never made mo words at all of it.
Soon after, there began a tuft of quiuering reeds too grow
Which being ripe bewrayd their seed and him that did them sow.
For when the gentle sowtherne wynd did lightly on them blow,
They vttred forth the words that had been buried in the ground
And so reproude the Asses ears of Midas with their sound.
Apollo after this revenge from Tmolus took his flight:
And sweeping through the air, did on the selfsame side alyght
Of Hellespontus, in the Realm of king Laomedon.
There stood upon the right side of Sigaeum, and upon
The left of Rhetye cliffe that time, an Altar buylt of old
Too Jove that heereth all men's words. Here Phoebus did behold
The foresaid king Laomedon beginning for too lay
Foundation of the walls of Troy: which woork from day too day
Went hard and slowly forward, and requyrd no little charge,
Then he toogither with the God that rules the surges large,
Did put themselves in shape of men, and bargaynd with the king
Of Phrygia for a sum of gold his woork too end too bring.
Now when the woork was done, the king their wages them denayd,
And falsely faaste them down with oaths it was not as they said.
Thou shall not mock us vnreuendgd (quod Neptune.) And anon
He caused all the surges of the sea too rush upon
The shore of covetous Troy, and made the country like the deep.
The goods of all the husbandmen away he quight did sweep,
And ouerwhelmd their feeldes with waves. And thinking this too small
A penance for the falsehod, he demanded therwithall
His daughter for a monster of the Sea. whom being bound
Untoo a rock, stout Hercules delivering ^aufe and sound,
Requyrd his steeds which were the hyre for which he did compound.
And when that of so great desert the king denyde the hyre,
The twyce forsworn false town of Troy he sacked in his ire.
And Telamon in honor of his service did enjoy
The Lady Hesion daughter of the covetous king of Troy.
For Peleus had already got a Goddess too his wife,
And lived unto both their joys a right renowned life.
And sure he was not prowder of his graundsyre, than of thee
That were become his fathrinlaw. For many mo than he
Have had the hap of mighty Jove the nephews for too be.
But never was it heeretoofore the chance of any one
Too have a Goddess too his wife, save only his alone.
For unto watery Thetis thus old Protevv did foretell.
Go marry: thou shall bear a son whose doings shall excel
His fathers far in feats of arms, and greater he shall be
In honor, high renown, and fame, than ever erst was he.
This caused Jove the watery bed of Thetis too forbear
Although his heart were more than warm with love of her, for fear
The world sum other greater thing than Jove himself should breed,
And willd the son of Aeacus this Peleus to succeed.
In that which he himself would feign have done, and for too take
The Lady of the sea in arms a moother her too make.
There is a bay of Thessaly that bends like a boawe.
The sides shoot forth, where if the sea of any depth did flow
It were a haven. Scarcely does the water hyde the sand.
It has a shore so firm, that if a man thereon do stand,
No print of foot remaynes behynd: it hinders not ones pace
Ne covered is with houering reeke. Adjoining too this place,
There is a grove of Myrtletrees with fruit of dowle color,
And in the midds thereof a Cave. I can not tell you whither
That nature or the art of man were maker of the same.
It seemed rather made by art. Often Thetis hither came
Stark naked, ryding bravely on a brydled Dolphins back.
There Peleus as she lay a sleep upon her often bracke.
And forbycause that at her hands entreatance nothing winnes,
He folding her about the neck with both his arms, begins
Too offer force. And surely if she had not fallen too wyles
And shifted oftentimes her shape, he had obteind erewhyles.
But she became sumtymes a bird: He hilld her like a bird.
Anon she was a massye log: but Peleus never stird
Awhit for that. Then thirdly she of speckled Tiger took
The ugly shape: for fear of whose most feerce and cruel look,
His arms he from her body twicht. And at his going thence,
In honor of the watery Gods he burned frankincence,
And powered wine upon the sea, with fat of neat and sheep:
Until the prophet that does dwell within Carpathian deep,
Said thus. Thou son of Aeacus, thy wish thou sure shall have
Alonely when she lies a sleep within her pleasant Cave,
Cast grinnes too trappe her vnbewares: hold fast with snarling knot:
And though she feign a hundredth shapes, deceive thee let her not.
But stick vn^oot what ere it be, until the time that she
Returns too the native shape she erst was wont too be.
When Protevv thus had sed, within the sea he duckt his head,
And suffered on his latter words the water for too spread.
The lyghtsum Titan downward drew, and with declyning chair
Approached too the western sea, when Neryes daughter fair
Returning from the sea, resorts too her accustomd cowch.
And Peleus scarcely had begone her naked limbs too towch,
But that she chaungd from shape to shape, until at length she found
Herself surprysd. Then stretching out her arms with sighs profound,
She said: Thou ouercommest me, and not without the aid
Of God: and then she Thetis like, appeerd in shape of maid.
The noble prince embracing her obteynd her at his will,
Too both their joys, and with the great Achylles did her fill.
A happy wight was Peleus in his wife: A happy wight
Was Peleus also in his son. And if ye him acquight
Of murthring Phocus, happy him in all things count ye might.
But giltye of his brothers blood, and bannisht for the same
From both his fathers house and Realm, too Trachin sad he came.
The son of lyghtsum Lucifer king Ceyx (who in face
Expressed the lively beawtye of his fathers heavenly grace,)
Without all violent rigor and sharp executions reignd
In Trachin. He right sad that time unlike himself, remaynd
Yet moorning for his brothers chance transformed late before.
When Peleus thither came, with care and travail tired sore,
He left his cattle and his sheep (whereof he brought great store)
Behynd him in a shady vale not far from Trachin town,
And with a little company himself went thither down.
As Soon as leave too come too Court was granted him, he bare
A branch of Olyf in his hand, and humbly did declare
His name and lynage. Only of his crime no word he spoke,
But of his slyght another cause pretensedly did make:
Desyring leave within his town or country too abide.
The king of Trachin gently thus too him ageine replyde.
Our bownty too the meanest sort (O Peleus) does extend:
We are not wont the desolate our country too forfend.
And though I be of nature most inclyned good too do:
Thine own renown, thy graundsyre Jove are forcements therevntoo.
Misspend no longer time in suit. I gladly do agree
Too grant thee what thou will desire. These things that thou do see
I would thou should account them as thine own, such as they be
I would they better were. With that he weeped. Peleus and
His friends desired of his greef the cause too understand.
He answered thus. Perchance ye think this bird that lives by pray
And putts all other birds in fear had wings and feathers ay.
He was a man. And as he was right feerce in feats of arms,
And stout and ready both too wreake and also offer harms:
So was he of a constant mind. Daedalion men him hight.
Our father was that noble star that brings the morning bright,
And in the welkin last of all gives place too Phoebus light.
My study was too maynteine peace, in peace was my delyght,
And for too keep me true too her too whom my faith is plyght.
My brother had felicite in war and bloody fight.
His prowess and his force which now does chase in cruel flight
The Dooues of Thisbye since his shape was altered thus a new,
Right puyssant Princes and their Realms did heeretoofore subdew.
He had a child called Chyone, whom nature did endew
With beawtye so, that when too age of fowreteene years she grew,
A thousand Princes liking her did for her favor sew.
By fortune as bright Phoebus and the son of Lady May
Came tone from Delphos, toother from mount Cyllen, by the way
They saw her both at once, and both at once where taken in love.
Apollo till the time of night differd his suit too move.
But Hermes could not bear delay. He stroked on the face
The maiden with his charmed rod which has the power too chase
And bring in sleep: the touch whereof did cast her in so dead
A sleep, that Hermes by and by his purpose of her sped.
As Soon as night with twinkling stars the welkin had beesprent,
Apollo in an old wives shape too Chyon clocely went,
And took the pleasure which the son of Maya had forehent.
Now when she full her time had gone, she bare by Mercurye
A son that hight Avvtolychus who provde a wyly pie,
And such a fellow as in theft and filching had no peer.
He was his fathers own son right: he could men's eyes so bleere,
As for too make y^ black things whyght, and whyght things black appear.
And by Apollo (for she bare a pair) was borne his brother
Philammon, who in music art excelled far all other,
As well in singing as in play. But what auayled it
Too bear such twins, and of two Gods in favor too have sit.
And that she too her father had a stowt and valiant knight,
Or that her graundsyre was the son of Jove that God of might?
Does glory hurt too any folk? It surely hurted her.
For standing in her own conceyt she did herself prefer
Before Diana, and dispraysd her face. who there with all
Inflaamd with wrath, said: well, with deeds we better please her shall.
Immediately she bent her bow, and let an arrow go,
Which struck her through the tongue, whose spite deserved wounding so.
Her tongue wext dumb, her speech began fail that erst was over ryfe,
And as she stryued for too speak, away went blood and life.
How wretched was I then O God? how struck it too my heart?
What words of comfort did I speak too ease my brothers smart?
Too which he gave his ear as much as does the stonny rock
Too hideous roaring of the waves that do against it knock.
There was no measure nor none end in making of his moan,
Nor in bewayling comfortless his daughter that was gone.
But when he saw her body burn, four times with all his might
He russhed forth too thrust himself amid the fire in sight.
Four times he being thence repulst, did put himself too flight.
And ran me whereas was no way, as does a Bullock when
A hornet stings him in the neck. Me thought he was as then
More wyghter far than any man. Ye would have thought his feet
Had had sum wings. So fled he quyght from all, and being fleet
Through eagerness too dye, he gat too mount Parnasos knappe
And there Apollo pitying him and rewing his missehappe,
When as Daedalion from the cliffe himself had headlong floong,
Transformd him too a bird, and on the soodaine as he hung
Did give him wings, and bowwing beak, and hooked talants keen,
And eeke a courage full as feerce as ever it had been.
And furthermore a greater strength he lent him therwithall,
Than one would think conveyed might be within a room so small.
And now in shape of Gossehawke he too none indifferent is,
But wreakes his teene on all birds. And because him self ere this
Did feel the force of sorrows sting within his wounded heart,
He makes others oftentimes too sorrow and too smart.
As Caeyx of his brothers chance this wondrous story seth,
Commes ronning thither all in haste and almost out of breath
Anaetor the Phocayan who was Pelyes herdman. He
Said: Pelye Pelye I do bring sad tidings unto thee.
Declare it man (quod Peleus) what ever that it be.
King Ceyx at his fearful words did stand in dowtfull stowne.
Thiz noonetyde (quod the herdman) Iche did drive your cattle down
Too zea, and zum a them did zit upon the yellow zand
And looked on the large main poole of water near at hand.
Zum roayled zoftly up and down, and zum a them did zwim
And bare their jolly horned heads abooue the water trim.
A Church stondes near the zea not decked with gold nor marble stone
But made of wood, and hid with trees that dreeping hang thereon.
A vissherman that zat and dryde hiz netts vppo the zhore
Did tellz that Nereus and his Nymphs did haunt the place of yore,
And how that they been Gods a zea. There butts a plot vorgrowne
With zallow trees upon the zame, the which is ouerblowne
With tides, and is a marsh. Urom thence a woolf an orped wight
With hideous noise of rustling made the grounds near hand afryght.
Anon he commes me buskling out bezmeared all his chappes
With blood daubaken and with vome as veerce as thunder clappes.
Hiz eyes did glaster red as vyre, and though he raged zore
Uor vamin and vor madness both, yet raged he much more
In madness. Uor he cared not his hunger vor too zlake,
Or i the death of oxen two or three an end too make.
But wounded all the herd and made a havoc of them all,
And zum of us too, in devence did happen vor too vall,
In danger of his deadly chappes, and lost our lives. The zhore
And zea is staynd with blood, and all the ven is on a rore.
Delay breeds loss. The case denies now dowting vor too stond,
While owght remaynes let all of us take weapon in our hond.
Lets arm our zelues, and let vz altoogither on him vall.
The herdman hilld his peace. The loss movde Peleus not at all.
But calling his offense too mind, he thought that Neryes daughter
The chyldlesse Lady Psamathe determynd with that slaughter.
Too keep an Obit too her son whom he before had killd.
Immediately upon this news the king of Trachin willd
His men too arm them, and too take their weapons in their hand,
And he addressed himself too be the leader of the band.
