His wife Alcyone by the noise admonisht of the same,
In dressing of her head, before she had it brought in frame,
Cast down her hear, and ronning forth caught Ceyx fast about
The neck, desyring him with tears too send his folk without
Himself, and in the life of him too save the lives of twain.
O Princess, cease your godly fear (quod Peleus then again.)
Your offer does deserve great thanks. I mind not war to make
Against strange monsters. I as now another way must take.
The seagods must be pacifyde. There was a Castle hye,
And in the same a lofty tower whose top does face the sky,
A joyful mark for maryners too guide their vessels by.
Too this same Turret up they went, and there with syghes behilld
The Oxen lying every where stark dead upon the feelde
And eeke the cruel stroygood with his bluddy mouth and hear.
Then Peleus stretching forth his hands too Seaward, prayed in fear
Too watrish Psamath that she would her sore displeasure stay,
And help him. She no whit relents too that that he did pray.
But Thetis for her husband made such earnest suit, that she
Obteynd his pardon. For anon the woolfe (who would not be
Revoked from the slaughter for the sweetness of the blood)
Persisted sharp and eager still, until that as he stood
Fast byghting on a Bullocks neck, she turned him intoo stone
As well in substance as in hew, the name of woolf alone
Reserved. For although in shape he seemed still yet one,
The verry color of the stone beewrayd him too be none,
And that he was not too be feared. How be it froward fate
Permitts not Peleus in that land too have a settled state.
He wandreth like an outlaw too the Magnets. There at last
Acastus the Thessalien purgd him of his murder past.
In this mean time the Trachine king sore vexed in his thought
With signs y^ both before and since his brothers death were wrought,
For counsel at the sacret Spelles (which are but toys too food
Fond fancyes, and not counselors in peril too do good)
Did make him reedy too the God of Claros for too go.
For heathenish Phorbas and the folk of Phlegia had as tho
The way too Delphos stopped, that none could travel too or fro.
But ere he on his journey went, he made his faithful make
Alcyone preeuye too the thing. Immediately their struck
A chillness too her verry bones, and pale was all her face
Like box and down her heavy cheeks the tears did gush a pace.
Three times about too speak, three times she washed her face with tears,
And stinting often with sobbes, she thus complained in his ears.
What fault of mine O husband dear has turned thy heart fro me?
Where is that care of me that erst was wont too be in thee?
And can thou having left thy dear Alcyone merrye be?
Do iourneyes long delyght thee now? does now mine absence please
Thee better then my presence does? Think I that thou at ease
Shall go by land? Shall I have cause but only for too moorne?
And not too be afraid? And shall my care of thy return
Be void of fear? No no. The sea me sore afraid does make.
Too think upon the sea does cause my flesh for fear too quake.
I saw the broken ribs of ships a late upon the shore.
And often on Tumbes I read their names whose bodies long before
The sea had swallowed. Let not fond vain hope ^educe thy mind,
That Aeolus is thy fathrinlaw who holds the boystous wynd
In prison, and can calm the seas at pleasure. When the wynds
Are once let looce upon the sea, no order then them bynds.
Then neither land has privilege, nor sea exemption fynds.
Yea even the clouds of heaven they vex, and with their meeting stout
Enforce the fire with hideous noise too brust in flasshes out.
The more that I do know them, (for right well I know their power,
And saw them often a little wench within my fathers bower)
So much the more I think them too be feared. But if thy will
By no intreatance may be turned at home too tarry still,
But that thou needs wilt go: then me dear husband with thee take.
So shall the sea us equally toogither toss and shake.
So woorser than I feel I shall be certain not too fear.
So shall we whatsoever happes toogither jointly bear.
So shall we on the broad main sea toogither jointly sail.
These woo^des and tears wherewith the imp of Aeolus did assail
Her husbond borne of heavenly race, did make his heart relent.
(For he lovd her no less than she lovd him.) But fully bent
He seemed, neither for too leave the journey which he meant
Too take by sea, nor yet too give Alcyone leave as tho
Companion of his perlous course by water for too go.
He many words of comfort spoke her fear away too chase.
But naught he could persuade therein too make her like the case.
This last asswagement of her greef he added in the end,
Which was the only thing that made her loving heart too bend.
All taryante will assuredly seem over long too me.
And by my fathers blasing beams I make my vow too thee
That at the furthest ere the time (if God thertoo agree)
The moon do fill her circle twyce, ageine I will here be.
When in sum hope of his return this promis had her set,
He willd a ship immediately from harbrough too be fet,
And thoroughly rigged for too be, that neither maast, nor sail,
Nor tackling, no nor other thing should apperteyning fail.
Which when Alcyone did behold, as one whoose heart misgaue
The happes at hand, she quaakt ageine, and tears out guss^ing drove.
And streyning Ceyx in her arms with pale and piteous look,
Poor wretched soul, her last farewell at length she sadly took,
And swounded flat upon the ground. Anon the watermen
(As Ceyx sought delays and was in dowt too turn again)
Set hand too Ores, of which there were two rows on either side,
And all at once with equal stroke the swelling sea deuyde.
She lifting up her watrye eyes behilld her husband stand
Upon the hatches making signs by beckening with his hand:
And she made signs to him ageine. And after that the land
Was far removed from the ship, and that the sight began
Too be unable too discern the face of any man,
As long as ere she could she looked upon the rowing keele.
And when she could no longer time for distance ken it we will,
She looked still upon the sails that flasked with the wynd
Upon the maast. And when she could the sails no longer find,
She gate her too her empty bed with sad and sorye heart,
And laid her down. The chamber did renew a fresh her smart,
And of her bed did bring too mind the dear departed part.
From harbrough now they quyght were gone: and now a plasant gale
Did blow. The master made his men their Ores aside too hale,
And hoysed up the toppesayle on the highest of the maast,
And clapped on all his other sails because no wind should waast.
Scarce full tone half, (or sure not much above) the ship had run
Upon the sea, and every way the land did far them shonne,
When toward night the wallowing waves began too waxen whyght,
And eeke the heady eastern wynd did blow with greater might,
Anon the Master cried strike the toppesayle, let the main
Sheate fly and fardle it too the yard. Thus spoke he, but in vain.
For why so hideous was the storm upon the soodeine brayd,
That not a man was able there too here what other said.
And loud the sea with meeting waves extreemely raging rores.
Yet fell they too it of them selves. Sum haalde aside the Ores:
Sum fensed in the Gallyes sides, sum down the sayleclothes rend:
Sum pump the water out, and sea too sea ageine do send.
Another hales the sayleyards down. And while they did each thing
Disorderly, the storm increast, and from each quarter ^^ing
The wyndes with deadly food, and bownce the raging waves toogither.
The Pilot being sore dismayed says plain, he knows not whither
Too wend himself, nor what too do or bid, nor in what state
Things stood. So howge the mischeef was, and did so ouermate
All art. For why of rattling ropes, of crying men and boys,
Of flusshing waves and thundering air, confused was the noise.
The surges mounting up aloft did seem too mate the sky,
And with their sprinckling for too wet the clouds that hang on hye.
One while the sea, when from the brink it raysd the yellow sand,
Was like in color too the same. Another while did stand
A color on it blacker than the Lake of Styx. Anon
It l^eth plain and loometh whyght with seething froth thereon*
And with the sea the Trachin ship ay alteration took.
One while as from a mountains top it seemed down too look
Too vallyes and the depth of hell. Another while beset
With swelling surges round about which near above it met,
It looked from the bottom of the whoorlepoole up aloft
As if it were from hell too heaven. A hideous flusshing often
The waves did make in beating full against the Gallyes side.
The Gallye b^ing stricken gave as great a sownd that ^yde
As did sumtyme the Battellramb of steel, or now the Gonne
In making battrye too a tower. And as feerre Lions run
Full brist with all their force against the armed men that stand
In order bent too keep them of with weapons in their hand,
Even so as often as the waves by force of wynd did rave:
So often upon the netting of the ship they maynely drove,
And mounted far above the same. Anon of fell the hoopes:
And having washed the pitch away, the sea made open loopes
Too let the deadly water in. Behold the clouds did melt,
And showers large came pooring down. The seamen that them felt
Might think that all the heaven had fallen upon them that same time,
And that the swelling sea likewise above the heaven would clyme.
The sails were thoroughly wet with showers. and with the heavenly rain
Was mixed the waters of the sea. no lyghts at all remain
Of sun, or moon, or stars in heaven. The darkness of the night
Augmented with the dreadful storm, takes dowble power and might.
Howbeet the flasshing lyghtnings often do put the same too flight,
And with their glauncing now and then do give a soodeme light.
The lightnings setts the waves on fire. Above the netting skippe
The waves, and with a violent force do light within the ship.
And as a souldyer stowter than the rest of all his band
That often assayles a city walls defended well by hand,
At length atteines his hope, and for too purchase praise withal
Alone among a thousand men getts up upon the wall:
So when the loftye waves had long the Gallyes sides assayd,
At length the tenth wave rising up with howger force and brayd,
Did never cease assaulting of the weery ship, till that
Upon the hatches like a fo victoriously it gat.
A part thereof did still as yet assault the ship without,
And part had gotten in. The men all trembling ran about,
As in a City commes too pass, when of the enmyes sum
Dig down the walls without, and sum already in are come.
All art and couning was too seek. Their hearts and stomachs fail:
And look how many furges came their vessel too assail,
So many deaths did seem too charge and break upon them all.
One weeps: another stands amazde: the third them blist does call
Whom buryall does remain. Too God another makes his vow,
And holding up his hands too heaven the which he sees not now,
Does pray in vain for help. The thought of this man is upon
His brother and his parents whom he clearly has forgone.
Another calls his house and wife and children unto mind,
And every man in general the things he left behynd.
Alcyone moves Ceyx heart. In Ceyx mouth is none
But only one Alcyone. And though she were alone
The wight that he desired most, yet was he verry glad
She was not there. Too Trachin ward too look desire he had,
And homeward feign he would have turned his eyes which never more
Should see the land. But when he knew not which way was the shore,
Nor where he was. The raging sea did roll about so fast:
And all the heaven with clouds as black as pitch was over cast,
That never night was half so dark. There came a flaw at last,
That with his violence brake the maste, and struck the stern away.
A billowe proudly pranking up as vaunting of his pray
By conquest gotten, walloweth hole and breaks not a sunder,
Beholding with a lofty look the waters woorking under.
And look as if a man should from the places where they grow
Rend down the mountains Athe and Rind, and whole them overthrow
Intoo the open sea: so soft the Billowe tumbling down,
With weight and violent stroke did sink and in the bottom drown
The Gallye. And the most of them that were within the same
Went down therewith and never up too open a^er came,
But dyed strangled in the gulf. Another sort again
Caught pieces of the broken ship. The king himself was feign
A shiuer of the sunken ship in that same hand to hold,
In which he erst a royal mace had hilld of yellow gold.
His father and his fathrinlawe he calls upon (alas
In vain.) But chiefly in his mouth his wife Alcyone was.
In heart was she: in tongue was she: He wisshed that his corse
Too land where she might take it up the surges might enforce:
And that by her most loving hands he might be laid in grave.
In swimming still (as often as the surges leave him gave
Too open his lips) he harped still upon Alcyones name,
And when he drowned in the waves he muttred still the same.
Behold, even full upon the wa^^ a (...) of water black
Did break, and underneath the sea the head of Ceyx stracke.
That night the lyghtsum Lucifer for sorrow was so dim,
As scarcely could a man discern or think it too be him.
And forasmuch as out of heaven he might not step aside,
With thick and darksum clouds that night his countnance he did hyd*
Alcyone of so great mischaunce not knowing aught as yet,
Did keep a reckening of the nights that in the while did flit,
And hasted garments both for him and for herself likewise,
Too wear at his homecomming which she vaynely did surmyse.
Too all the Gods devoutly she did offer frankincence:
But most above them all the Church of Juno she did sense.
And for her husband (who as then was none) she kneeld before
The Altar, wisshing health and soon arrival at the shore,
And that none other woman might before her be preferred.
Of all her prayers this one piece effectually was heard.
For Juno could not find in heart entreated for too be
For him that was already dead. But too thentent that she
From dame Alcyones deadly hands might keep her Altars free,
She said. Most faithful messenger of my commaundments, O
Thou Raynebowe, too the slugguish house of Slomber swiftly go.
And bid him send a Dream in shape of Ceyx too his wife
Alcyone, for too show her plain the losing of his life.
Dame Iris takes her pall wherein a thousand colors were
And bowwing like a stringed bow upon the clowdy sphere,
Immediately descended too the drowzye house of Sleep
Whose Court the clouds continually do clo^ely ouerdreepe.
Among the dark Cimmerians is a hollow mountain found
And in the hill a Cave that far does run within the ground, The house of
sleep.

The Chamber and the dwelling place where slothful sleep does cowch.
The light of Phoebus golden beams this place can never towch.
A foggye mist with dimnesse mixed streams upward from the ground,
And glimmering twylyght evermore within the same is found.
No watchful bird with barbed bill, and combed crown does call
The morning forth with crowing out. There is no noise at all
Of waking dog, nor gagling goose more waker than the hound
Too hinder sleep. Of beast ne wyld ne tame there is no sound.
No bowghes are stird with blastes of wynd. no noise of tatling tongue
Of man or woman ever yet within that bower roong
Dumb quiet dwells there. Yet from the Roches foot does go
The river of forgetfulness. which ronneth trickling so
Upon the little pebble stones which in the channel lie,
That unto sleep a great deal more it does provoke thereby.
Before the entry of the Cave, there grows of Poppye store.
With seeded heads, and other weeds innumerable more,
Out of the milkye iewce of which the night does gather sleeps,
And over all the shadowed earth with dankish deawe them dreepes.
Because the craking hindges of the door no noise should make,
There is no door in all the house, nor porter at the gate.
Amid the Cave, of Ebonye a bedsted stands hye,
And on the same a bed of down with keeuerings black does lie:
In which the drowzye God of sleep his lither limbs does rest.
About him, forging sundry shapes as many dreams lie pressed,
As ears of corn do stand in feeldes in harvest time, or leaves
Do grow on trees, or sea too shore of sandye cinder heaues.
As Soon as Iris came within this house, and with her hand
Had put aside the dazeling dreams that in her way did stand,
The brightness of her robe through all the sacred house did shine.
The God of sleep scarce able for too raise his heavy eyes,
A three or four times at the least did fall ageine too rest,
And with his nodding head did knock his chinne against his breast.
At length he shaking of himself, upon his elbowe leande.
And though he knew for what she came: he asked her what she meand.
O sleep (quod she,) the rest of things, O gentlest of the Gods,
Sweet sleep, the peace of mind, with whom crookt care is aye at odds:
Which cherrishest men's weery limbs appalld with toyling sore,
And make them as fresh too woork and lustye as before,
Command a dream that in their kinds can every thing express,
Too Trachine Hercles town himself this instant too address.
And let him lively counterfeit too Queen Alcyonea
The image of her husband who is drowned in the sea
By shipwrecke. Juno wills so. Her message being told,
Dame Iris went her way* she could her eyes no longer hold
From sleep. But when she felt it come she fled that instant time,
And by the boawe that brought her down too heave ageine did clyme.
Among a thousand sons and mo that father slomber had
He called up Morph the feyner of man's shape a craftye lad.
None other could so conningly express man's verrye face,
His gesture and his sound of voice, and manner of his pace,
Toogither with his woonted weed, and woonted phrase of talk.
But this same Morphye only in the shape of man does walk.
There is another who the shapes of beast or bird does take,
Or else appears unto men in likeness of a snake.
The Gods do call him Icilos, and mortal folk him name
Phobetor. There is also yet a third who from these same
Works diversely, and Phantasos he highteth. Intoo streams
This turns himself, and intoo stones, and earth, and timber beams,
And intoo every other thing that wants life. These three.
Great kings and Capteines in the night are woonted for too see.
The meaner and inferior sort of others haunted be.
Sir Slomber ouerpast the rest, and of the brothers all
Too do dame Iris message he did only Morphye call.
Which done he waxing luskish, straight laid down his drowzy head
And softly shroonk his layzye limbs within his sluggish bed.
Away flew Morphye through y^ air: no flickring made hi^ wings*
And came anon too Trachine. There his feathers of he flings,
And in the shape of Ceyx stands before Alcyones bed,
Pale, wan, stark naakt, and like a man that was but lately dead.
His berde seemd wet, and of his head the hear was dropping dry,
And leaning on her bed, with tears he seemed thus too cry.
Most wretched woman know thou thy loving Ceyx now
Or is my face by death disformd? behold me well, and thou
Shall know me. For thy husband, thou thy husbands Ghost shall see.
No good thy prayers and thy vows have done at all too me.
For I am dead. In vain of my return no reckning make.
The clowdy sowth amid the sea our ship did tardy take,
And tossing it with violent blastes asunder did it shake.
And floodes have filld my mouth which called in vain upon thy name.
No person whom thou may misdeeme brings tidings of the same
Thou hear not thereof by false report of flying fame.
But I myself: I presently my shipwrecke too thee show.
Arise therefore and woeful tears upon thy spouse bestow.
Put moorning raiment on, and let me not too Limbo go
Unmoorned for. In showing of this shipwrecke Morphye so
Did feyne the voice of Ceyx, that she could none other deem,
But that it should be his in deed. Moreover he did seem
Too weep in earnest: and his hands the verry gesture had
Of Ceyx. Queen Alcyone did groan, and being sad
Did stir her arms, and thrust them forth his body too embrace.
In stead whereof she caught but air. The tears ran down her face.
She cried, tarry: whither flyste? toogither let us go.
And all this while she was a sleep. Both with her crying so,
And flayghted with the image of her husbands ghastly spryght,
She started up: and sought about if find him there she might.
(For why her Grooms awaking with the shreeke had brought a light.)
And when she no where could him find, she began her face too smyght,
And tare her nyghtclothes from her breast, and struck it feercely, and
Not passing too vnty her hear she rent it with her hand.
And when her nurce of this her greef desyrde too understand
The cause: Alcyone is undone, undone and cast away
With Ceyx her dear spouse (she said.) Leave comforting I pray.
By shipwrecke he is perrisht: I have seen him: and I knew
His hands. When in departing I too hold him did pursew,
I caught a Ghost: but such a Ghost as well discern I might
Too be my husbands Nathelesse he had not too my sight
His woonted countenance, neither did his visage shine so bright,
As here toofore it had been wont. I saw him wretched wight
Stark naked, pale, and with his hear still wet: even verry here
I saw him stand. with that she looks if any print appear
Of footing where as he did stand upon the floor behynd.
This this is it that I did fear in far forecasting mind,
When flying me I thee desyrde thou should not trust the wynd.
But sith thou wentest too thy death, I would that I had gone
With thee* ah meet, it meet had been thou should not go alone
Without me. So it should have come too pass that neither I
Had ouerliued thee, nor yet been forced twice too dye.
Already, absent in the waves now tossed have I be.
Already have I perrished. And yet the sea has thee
Without me. But the cruelnesse were greater far of me
Than of the sea, if after thy decease I still would strive
In sorrow and in anguish still too pyne away alive.
But neither will I strive in care too lengthen still my life,
Nor (wretched wight) abandon thee: but like a faithful wife
At leastwyse now will come as thy companion. And the hearse
shall join us, though not in the selfsame coffin: yet in verse.
Although in tumb the bones of us toogither may not couch,
Yet in a graven Epitaph my name thy name shall touch.
Her sorrow would not suffer her too utter any more.
She sobd and sight at every word, until her heart was sore.
The morning came, and out she went right pensif too the shor^
Too that same place in which she took her leave of him before.
While there she musing stood, and said, he kissed me even here,
Here weyed he his Anchors up, here loosd he from the peer,
And while she called too mind the things there marked with her eyes:
In looking on the open sea, a great way of she spies
A certain thing much like a corse come houering on the wave.
At first she dowted what it was. As tide it nearer drove,
Although it were a good way of, yet did it plainly show
Too be a corce. And though that whose it was she did not know,
Yet forbycause it seemd a wrecke, her heart thereat did rise:
And as it had sum stranger been, with water in her eyes
She said, alas poor wretch who ere thou art, alas for her
That is thy wife, if any be. And as the waves did stir,
The body floted nearer land: the which the more that she
Behilld, the less began in her of stayed wit too be.
Anon it did arrive on shore. Then plainly she did see
And know it, that it was her feere. She shreeked it is he.
And therewithal her face, her hear, and garments she did tear,
And unto Ceyx stretching out her trembling hands with fear,
Said: cumst thou home in such a plyght too me O husband dear?
Returnst in such a wretched plyght? There was a certain peer
That builded was by hand, of waves the first assaults too break,
And at the hauons mouth too cause the tide too enter weak.
She lept thereon. (A wonder sure it was she could do so)
She flew, and with her newgrowen wings did beat the air as tho.
And on the waves a wretched bird she whisked too and fro.
And with her crocking neb then grown too slender bill and round,
like one that wayld and moorned still she made a moaning sound.
Howbeet as soon as she did touch his dumb and bloodlesse flesh,
And had embraast his loved limbs with wings made new and fresh,
And with her hardened neb had kissed him coldly, though in vain,
Folk dowt of Ceyx feeling it too raise his head did strain,
Or whither that the waves did lift it up. But surely he
It felt: and through compassion of the Gods both he and she
Were turned too birds. The love of them eeke subject too their fate,
Continued after: neither did the faithful bond abate
Of wedlock in them being birds: but stands in steadfast state.
They tread, and lay, and bring forth young and now the The kings fisher.
Alcyon sitts
In wintertime upon her nest (which on the water flitts
A seuennyght. During all which time the sea is calm and still,
And every man may too and fro sail saufly at his will.
For Aeolus for his ofsprings sake the winds at home does keep,
And will not let them go abroad for troubling of the deep.
An ancient father seeing them about the broad sea fly,
Did praise their love for lasting too the end so steadfastly.
His neighbor or the selfsame man made answer (such is chance)
Even this foul also whom thou see upon the surges glaunce
With spindle shanks, (he poynted too the wydegoawld Cormorant)
Before that he became a bird, of royal race might vaunt.
And if thou covet lineally his pedigree too seek,
His Auncetors were Ilus, and Assaracus, and eeke
Fair Ganymed who Jupiter did ravish as his joy,
Laomedon and Priamus the last that reygnd in Troy.
Stout Hectors brother was this man. And had he not in pryme
Of lusty youth been taken away, his deeds perchance in time
Had purchaast him as great a name as Hector, though that he
Of Dymants daughter Hecuba had fortune borne too be.
For Acsacus reported is begotten to have been
By escape, in shady Ida on a maiden fair and sheene
Whose name was Alyxothoe, a poor man's daughter that
With spade and mattocke for himself and his a living gat.
This Aesacus the City hates, and gorgious Court does shonne,
And in the vnambicious feeldes and woods alone does won.
He seeldoom haunts the town of Troy, yet having not a rude
And blockish wit, nor such a heart as could not be subdewd
By love, he spyde Eperie (whom often he had pursewd
Through all the woods) then sitting on her father Cebrius brim
A drying of her hear against the son, which hanged trim
Upon her back. As Soon as that the Nymph was ware of him,
She fled as when the grisild woolf does scare the fearful hynd
Or when the Fawcon far from brooks a Mallard happes too find.
The Troiane knight ronnes after her, and being swift through love,
Purseweth her whom fear does force apace her feet to move.
Behold an Adder lurking in the grass there as she fled,
Did byght her foot with hooked tooth, and in her body spread
His venim. She did cease her flight and soodein fell down dead.
Her lover being past his wits her carkesse did embrace,
And cryde, alas it irketh me, it irkes me of this chase.
But this I feared not. neither was the gain of that I willd
Worth half so much. Now two of us thee (wretched soul) have killd.
The wound was given thee by the snake, the cause was given by me.
The wickedder of both am I: who for too comfort thee
Will make thee satisfaction with my death. With that at last
Down from a rock (the which the waves had vndermynde) he cast
Himself intoo the sea. Howbeet dame Tethys pitying him,
Receyud him softly, and as he upon the waves did swim,
She covered him with feathers. And though feign he would have did,
She would not let him. Wroth was he that death was him denyde,
And that his soul compelld should be against his will too bide
Within his wretched body still, from which it would depart,
And that he was constreynd too live perforce against his heart.
And as he on his shoulders now had newly taken wings,
He mounted up, and down upon the sea his boddye dings.
His feathers would not let him sink. In rage he dyueth down,
And despratly he strives himself continually too drown.
His love did make him lean, long legs: long neck does still remain.
His head is from his shoulders far: of Sea he is most feign.
And for he underneath the waves delyghteth for too drive
A name according therevntoo the Latins do him give.
Finis vndecimi Libri.
THE.XII. BOOK OF Ouids Metamorphosis.
RIng Priam being ignorant that Aesacus his son
Did live in shape of bird, did moorne: and at a tumb whereon
His name was written, Hector and his brother solemly
Did keep an Obit. Paris was not at this obsequye.
Within a while with ravished wife he brought a lasting war
Home unto Troy. There followed him a thousand ships not far
Conspyrd toogither, with the aid that all the Greeks could find:
And vengeance had been taken forthwith but that the cruel wynd
Did make the seas vnsaylable, so that their ships were feign
At rode at fisshye Avvlys in Baeotia too remain.
Here as the Greeks according too their wont made sacrifyse
Too Jove, and on the Altar old the flame aloft did rise,
They spyde a speckled Snake creep up upon a planetree bye
Upon the top whereof there was among the branches hye
A nest, and in the nest eight birds, All which and eeke their dam
That flickering flew about her loss, the hungry snake did cram
Within his mawe. The standers by were all amazde thereat.
But Calchas Thestors son who knew what meening was in that,
Said* we shall win. Rejoice ye Greeks by us shall perish Troy
But long the time will be before we may our will enjoy.
And then he told them how the birds nine years did signify
Which they before the town of Troy not taking it should lie.
The Serpent as he wound about the boughs and branches green,
Became a stone, and still in stone his snakish shape is seen.
The seas continewed verry rough and suffered not their host
Embarked for too pass from thence too take the further coast.
Sum thought that Neptune favored Troy because himself did buyld
The walls thereof. But Calchas(who both knew, and never hilld
His peace in time) declared that the Goddess Phebe must
Appeased be with virgins blood for wrath conceived just.
As Soon as pity yielded had too case of puplicke weal,
And reason got the upper hand of fathers loving zeal,
So that the Lady Iphigen before the altar stood
Among the weeping ministers, too give her maidens blood:
The Goddess taking pity, cast a mist before their eyes,
And as they prayed and sti^d about too make the sacrifyse,
Conueyes her quight away, and with a Hynd her room supplies
Thus with a slaughter meet for her Diana being pleased,
The raging surges with her wrath toogither were appeasd,
The thousand ships had wynd at poope. And when they had abode
Much trouble, at the length all safe they gat the Phrygian rode.
Amid the world tweene heaven, and earth, and sea, there is a place,
Set from the bounds of each of them indifferently in space,
From whence is seen what ever thing is practisd any where,
Although the Realm be nere so far, and roundly too the ear
Commes whatsoever spoken is* Fame has his dwelling there.
Who in the top of all the house is lodged in a tower.
A thousand entryes, glades, and holes are f^amed in this bower.
There are no doors too shet. The doors stand open night and day.
The house is all of sounding brass, and roreth every way,
Reporting dowble every word it hears people say.
There is no rest within, there is no silence any where.
Yet is there not a yelling out: but humming, as it were
The sound of surges being heard far of, are like the sound
That at the end of thunderclappes long after does redound,
When Jove does make the clouds too crack, within the courts is preace
Of common people, which too come and go do never ceace.
And millions both of trothes and lies run gadding every where,
And words confusely fly in heaps. Of which, sum fill the ^are
That heard not of them erst, and sum Colearyers part do play
Too spread abroad the things they heard. And ever by the way
The thing that was invented grows much greater than before,
And every one that getts it by the end adds sumwhat more.
Light credit dwells there. There dwells rash error: There does dwell
Uayne joy: There dwells hartlesse fear, and Brute that loves too tell
Uncertayne news upon report, whereof he does not know
The author, and Sedition who fresh rumors loves too sow.
This Fame beholds what is done in heaven, on sea, and land,
And what is wrought in all the world he lays to understand.
He gave the Troyans warning that the Greeks with valiant men
And ships approached, that unawares they could not take them then.
For Hector and the Trojan folk well armed were at hand
Too keep the coast and bid them bace before they came a land.
Protesilay by fatal doom was first that did in feeld
Of Hectors spear: and after him great numbers mo were killd
Of valiant men. That battle did the Greeks full deerly cost.
And Hector with his Phrygian folk of blood no little lost,
In trying what the Greeks could do. The shore was red with blood.
And now king Cygnet Neptunes son had killed where he stood
A thousand Greeks. And now the stout Achilles causd to stay
His Charyot: and his lawnce did ^lea whole bands of men that day.
And seeking Cygnet through the feeld or Hector, he did stray.
At last with Cygnet he did meet. For Hector had delay
Until the tenth year afterward. Then hasting forth his horses
With flaxen manes, against his fo his Chariot he enforces.
And brandisshing his shaking dart, he said: O noble wight
A comfort let it be too thee that such a valiant knight
As is Achilles kills thee. In saying so he threw
A mighty dart, which though it hit the mark at which it flew,
Yet perst it not the skin at all. Now when this blunted blow
Had hit on Cygnets breast, and did no print of hitting show,
Thou Goddess son (quod Cygnet) for by fame we do the know)
Why woondrest at me for too see I can not wounded be?
( Achilles woondred much thereat.) This helmet which ye see
Bedect with horses yellow manes, this sheeld that I do bear,
Defend me not. For ornaments alonly I them wear.
For this same cause arms Mars himself likewise. I will disarme
Myself, and yet vnrazed will I pass without all harm.
It is too sum effect, not borne too be of Neryes race,
So that a man be borne of him that with threeforked mace
Rules Nereus and his daughters too, and all the sea beside.
This said, he at Achilles sent a dart that should abide
Upon his sheeld. It perced through the steel and through nine fold
Of Oxen hydes, and stayed upon the tenth. Achilles bold
Did wrest it out, and forcybly did throw the same again.
His body being hit ageine, unwounded did remain,
And clear from any print of wound. The third went eeke in vain.
And yitdid Cygnet too the same give full his naked brist.
Achilles chafed like a Bull that in the open list
With dreadful horns does push against the scarlet clothes that ther^
Are hanged up too make him feerce, and when he would them tear
Does find his wounds deluded. Then Achilles looked upon
His Iauelings socket, if the head thereof were looce or gone.
The head stacke fast. My hand byleeke is weakened then (quod he).
And all the force it had before is spent on one I see.
For sure I am it was of strength, both when I first down threw
Lyrnessus walls, and when I did I Will Tenedos subdew,
And eeke Axetions Thebe with her proper blood embrew.
And when so many of the folk of Tevvthranie I slew,
That with their blood Caycus stream became of purple hew.
And when the noble Telephus did of my Dart of steel
The dowble force, of wounding and of healing also feel.
Yea even the heaps of men slain here by me, that on this strond
Are lying still too look upon, do give too understand
That this same hand of mine both had and still has strength. This sed,
(As though he had distrusted all his doings ere that sted,)
He threw a Dart against a man of Lycia land that hight
Menetes, through whose Curets and his breast he struck him quyght.
And when he saw with dying limbs him sprawling on the ground,
He stepped too him straight, and pulld the Iaueling from the wound,
And said alowd: This is the hand, this is the self same dart
With which my hand did strike even now Menetes too the heart.
Against my toother Copemate will I use the same: I pray
Too God it may have like success. This sed, without delay
He sent it toward Cygnet, and the weapon did not stray,
Nor was not shunned. Insomuch it lighted full upon
His shoulder: and it gave a rappe as if upon sum ston
It lyghted had, rebownding back. Howbeeit where it hit,
Achilles saw it bloodye, and was vaynly glad of it.
For why there was no wound. It was Menetes blood. Then lept
He hastly from his Charyot down, and like a madman stepped
Too careless Cygnet with his sword. He saw his sword did pare
His Target and his morion both. But when it touched the bare,
His body was so hard, it did the edge thereof abate.
He could no lengar suffer him too triumph in that rate,
But with the pommell of his sword did thump him on the pate,
And bobd him well about the brewes a doozen times and more,
And preacing on him as he still gave back amaazd him sore,
And troubled him with buffetting, not respetting a whit.
Then Cygnet began too be afraid, and mistres beegan too flit
Before his eyes, and dimd his sight. And as he still did yield,
In giving back, by chance he met a stone amid the feeld,
Against the which Achilles thrust him back with all his might,
And throwing him against the ground, did cast him bolt vpryght.
Then bearing bostowsely with both his knees against his chest,
And leaning with his elbowes and his target on his breast,
He shet his headpeece cloce and just, and underneath his chin
So hard it straynd, that way for breath was neither out nor in,
And closed up the vent of life. And having gotten so
The upper hand, he went about too spoil his vanquished fo.
But naught he in his armor found, For Neptune had as tho
Transformd him too the foul whose name he bare but late ago.
This labor, this encounter brought the rest of many days,
And either party in their strength a while from battle stays.
Now while the Phrygians watch and ward upon the walls of Troy,
And Greeks likewise within their trench, there came a day of joy,
In which Achilles for his luck in Cygnets overthrow,
A Cow in way of sacrifyse on Pallas did bestow.
Whose inwards when he had upon the burning altar cast
And that the acceptable fume had through the air past
Too Godward, and the holy rytes had had their dewes, the rest
Was set on boords for men too eat in disshes fynely dressed.
The princes sitting down, did feed upon the roasted flesh,
And both their thirst and present cares with wine they did refresh.
Not Harpes, nor songs, nor hollow flutes too here did them delyght.
They talked till they nigh had spent the greatest part of night.
And all their communication was of feats of arms in fight
That had been done by them or by their foes. And every wight
Delyghts too vppen oftentimes by turn as came about
The perils and the narrow brunts himself had shifted out.
For what thing should be talked before Achilles rather? Or
What kind of things than such as these could seem more meeter for
Achilles too be talking of? But in their talk most breeme
Was then Achilles victory of Cygnet. It did seem
A wonder that the flesh of him should be so hard and tough
As that no weapon might have power too raze or perce it through,
But that it did abate the edge of steel: It was a thing
That both Achilles and the Greeks in woondrous maze did bring.
Then Nestor said: This Cygnet is the person now alone
Of your time that defied steel, and could be perst of none.
But I have seen now long ago one Cene of Perrhebye,
I saw one Cene of Perrhebye a thousand wounders defye
With vnatteynted body. In mount Othris he did dwell:
And was renowned for his deeds: (and which in him right well
A greater wonder did appear) he was a woman borne.
This uncouth made them all much more amazed than beforne,
And every man desired him to tell it. And among
The rest, Achilles said. Declare I pray thee (for we long
Too hear it every one of us) O eloquent old man
The wisdom of our age: what was that Cene and how he wan
Another than his native shape, and in what rode, or in
What fight or skirmish, tweene you first acquaintance did beegin,
And who in fine did vanquish him if any vanquished him.
Then Nestor. Though y^ length of time have made my senses dim,
And diverse things erst seen in youth now out of mine be gone:
Yet bear I still mo things in mind: and of them all is none
Among so many both of peace and war, that yet does take
More steadfast root in memory. And if that time may make
A man great store of things through long continuance for too see,
Two hundred years already of my life full passed be,
And now I go upon the third. This foresaid Ceny was
The daughter of one Elatey. In beawty she did pass
The maidens all of Thessaly. From all the Cities bye
And from thy Cities also O Achilles came (for why
She was thy countrywoman) store of wooers who in vain
In hope too win her love did take great travel suit and pain.
Thy father also had perchance attempted here too matched
But that thy moothers marriage was already then dispatched,
Or she at least affyanced. But Ceny match with none,
Howbeeit as she on the shore was walking all alone,
The God of sea did ravish her. (so fame does make report)
And Neptune for the great delight he had in Venus sport,
Said: Ceny, ask me what thou will, and I will give it thee.
This also bruted is by fame.) The wrong here done too me
(quod Ceny makes me wish great things. And therefore too thentent
I may no more constreyned be too such a thing, consent
I may no more a woman be. and if thou grant theretoo,
It is even all that I desire, or wish thee for too do.
In bacer tune these latter words were vttred, and her voice
Did seem a man's voice as it was in deed. For too her choice
The God of sea had given consent. He granted him beside
That free from wounding and from hurt he should from thence abide,
And that he should not dye of steel. Right glad of this same grant
Away went Ceny, and the feeldes of Thessaly did haunt,
And in the feats of Cheualrye from that time spent his life.
The over bold Piritho^s.
Ixions son had taken too his wife
Hippodame. And keuering boordes in bowres of boughs of trees
His Clowdbred brothers one by one he placed in degrees.
There were the Lords of Thessaly. I also was among
The rest, a cheerful noise of feast through all the Palace roong.
Sum made the altars smoke, and sum the brydale carrolls soong.
Anon commes in the maiden bryde a goodly wench of face,
With wives and maidens following her with comely gate and grace.
We said that sir Pirithous was happy in his wife:
Which handsell had deceived us wellneere through soodeine strife.
For of the cruel Centavvres thou most cruel Evvryt, tho
Like as thy stomach was with wine far over charged: so
As Soon as thou behilldst the bryde, thy heart began too frayne,
And doubled with thy droonkennesse thy raging lust did reign.
The feast was troubled by and by with tables overthrown.
The bryde was hayled by the head, so far was furye grown.
Feerce Evvryt caught Hippodame, and every of the rest
Caught such as ^ommed next to hand, or such as likte him best.
It was the lively image of a City taken by foes.
The house did ring of women's shreekes* we all up quickly rose.
And first said Theseus thus. What aylst? art mad O Evvrytus?
That dare (seeing me alive) mis^se Pirithous?
Not knowing that in one thou do abuse us both? And least
He might have seemd too speak in vain, he thrustway such as preast
About the bryde, and took her from them freating sore thereat.
No answer made him Evvrytus: (for such a deed as that
Defended could not be with words) but with his sawcye fist
He flew at gentle Theseus face, and b^^d him on the brist.
By chance hard by, an ancient cup of image woork did stand.
Which being howge himself more howge sir Theseus took in hand,
And threwt at Evvryts head. He spewd as well at mouth as wound
Mirt cloddes of blood, and brain and wine, and on the soyled ground
Lay sprawling bolt vpryght. The death of him did set the rest
His dowblelimbed brothers so on fire, that all the quest
With one voice cried out kill kill. The wine had given them heart.
Their first encounter was with cups and Cannes thrown overthwart,
And brittle tankerds, and with boawles, pannes, dishes, potts, and trayes,
Things serving late for meat and drink, and then for bluddy frayes.
First Amycus Ophions son with out remorse began
Too reeue and rob the brydehouse of his furniture. He ran
And pulled down a Lampbeame full of lyghtes, and lifting it
Aloft like one that with an Ax does fetch his blow too slit
An Oxis neck in sacrifyse, He on the forehead hit
A Lapith named Celadon, and crusshed so his bones
That none could know him by the face: both eyes flew out at ones.
His nose was beaten back and too his pallat battred slat.
One Pelates a Macedone exceeding wroth thereat,
Pulld out a maple tressles foot, and napt him in the necks,
That bobbing with his chin against his breast too ground he becks,
And as he spitted out his teeth with blackish blood, he lent
Another blow too Amycus which straight too hell him sent.
Gryne standing by and lowering with a fell grim visage at
The smoking Altars, said: why use we not these same? with that
He caught a mighty altar up with burning fire thereon,
And it among the thickest of the Lapithes threw anon.
And two he over whelmd therewith called Brote and Orion.
This Orions moother Mycale is known of certeintye
The Moon resisting too have drawn by witchcraft from the sky.
Full dearly shall thou by it (quod Exadius) may I get
A weapon: and with that in stead of weapon, he did set
His hand upon a vowed hearts horn that on a Pynetree hye
Was nayld, and with two tynes thereof he struck out either eye
Of Gryne: whereof sum stacke upon the horn, and sum did fly
Upon his beard, and there with blood like ielly mixed did lie,
A flaming fyrebrand from amids an Altar Rhaetus snatched.
With which upon the leftsyde of his head Charaxus latcht
A blow that cracked his skull. The blaze among his yellow hear
Ran sindging up, as if dry corn with lightning blas^ed were.
And in his wound the seared blood did make a grievous sound,
As when a piece of steel red who^ taken up with tongs is drowned
In water by the smith, it spirts and hisseth in the trowgh.
Charaxus from his curled hear did shake the fire. and thowgh
He wounded were, yet caught he up upon his shoulders twain.
A stone the Iawme of either door that well would load a wayne.
The mass thereof was such as that it would not let him hi^
His fo. I^ lighted short: and with the falling down of it
A mate of his that Comet hight, it all in pieces smit.
Then Rhaete restreyning not his joy, said thus: I would the rowt
Of all thy mates might in the selfsame manner prove them s^owt.
And with his halfeburnt brond the wound he searched new again,
Not ceasing for to lay on load upon his pate amayne,
Until his head was crushed, and of his scalp the bones did swim
Among his brains. In jolly ruff he passed straight from him
Too Coryt, and Euagrus, and too Dryant on a row.
Of whom when Coryt (on whose cheeks young mossy down began grow)
Was slain, what praise or honor ( quod Euagrus) have thou got
By killing of a boy? mo words him Rhetus suffered not
Too speak, but in his open mouth did thrust his burning brand,
And down his throteboll too his chest. Then whisking in his hand
His fyrebrand round about his head he feercely did assail
The valyant Dryant. but with him he could not so prevail.
For as he triumphed in his luck, proceeding for too make
Continual slaughter of his foes, sir Dryant with a stake
(Whose point was hardened in the fire) did cast at him a ^oyne
And thrust him through the place in which the neck and shoulders join.
He groand and from his cannell bone could scarcely pull the stake.
And being foiled with his blood too flight he did him take.
Arnaeus also ran away, and Lycidas likewise.
And Medon (whose right shoulderplate was also wounded) flies.
So did Pisenor, so did Cavvne, and so did Mermeros
Who late outronning every man, now wounded slower goes:
And so did Phole, and Menelas, and Abas who was wont
Too make a spoil among wild Boares as often as he did hunt:
And eeke the wyzarde Astylos who counseled his mates
Too leave that fray: but he too them in vain of leaving prates.
He eeke too Nessus (who for fear of wounding seemed shye)
Said, fly not thou shall escape this fray of Hercles bow too dye.
But Lycid and Evvrinomos, and Imbreus, and Are
Escapte not death. Sir Dryants hand did all alike them spare.
Cayneius also (though that he in flying were not slack,
Yet was he wounded on the face: For as he looked back,
A weapons point did hit him full midway between the eyes,
Whereas the nore and forehead meet. For all this deane, yet lies
Aphipnas snorting fast a sleep not mynding for to wake,
Wrapped in a cloak of Bearskinnes which in Ossa mount were take.
And in his lither hand he hilld a pot of wine. Whom when
That Phorbas saw (although in vain) not meddling with them, then
He set his fingars too the thong: and saying, thou shall drink
Thy wine with water taken from the Stygian fountaynes brink,
He threw his dart at him. The dart (as he that time by chance
Lay bolt upright upon his back) did through his throteboll glaunce.
He did and felt no pain at all. The black swart blood gusht out,
And on the bed and in the pot fell flushing like a spout.
I saw Petreius go about too pull out of the ground
An Oken tree. But as he had his arms about it round,
And shaakt it too and fro too make it looce, Pirithous cast
A Dart which nailed too the tree his wrything stomach fast.
Through prowess of Pirithous (men say) was Lycus slain.
Through prowess of Pirithous did Crome. But they both twain
Less honor too their conqueror were, than Dyctis was, or than
Was Helops. Helops with a dart was stricken which though ran
His head, and eutring at the right ear too the left ear went.
And Dyctis from a slipprye knappe down slyding, as he meant
Too shonne Perithous preaching on, fell headlong down, and with
His howgenesse brake the greatest Ash that was in all the frith,
And goard his gutts upon y^ stump. Too wreake his death co^mes Phare:
And from the mount a mighty rock with both his hands he tare:
Which as he was about too throw, Duke Theseus did prevent,
And with an Oken plant upon his mighty elbowe lent
Him such a blow, as that he brake the bones, and past no further.
For leisure would not serve him then his maimed corce too murder.
He lept on high Bianors back, who none was wont too bear
Besides himself. Against his sides his knees fast nipping were,
And with his left hand taking hold upon his foretoppe hear
He cuft him with his knubbed plant about the frowning face,
And made his wattled brows too break. And with his Oken mace
He overthrew Nedimnus: and Lycespes with his dart,
And Hippasus whose beard did hyde his breast the greater part:
And Riphey tallar than the trees, and Therey who was wont
Among the hills of Thessaly for cruel Bears too hunt,
And bear them angry home alive. It did Demoleon spyght
That Theseus had so good success and fortune in his fight.
An old long Pynetree rooted fast he straue with all his might
Too pluck up whole both trunk and root. which when he could not bring
Too pass, he brake it of, and at his emnye did it fling.
But Theseus by admonishment of heavenly Pallas (so
He would have folk believe it were) start back a great way fro
The weapon as it came. Yet fell it not without some harm.
It cut from Crantors left side bulk, his shoulder, breast, and arm.
This Crantor was thy fathers Squyre ( Achilles) and was given
Him by Amyntor ruler of the Dolops, who was driven
By battle for too give him as an hostage for the peace
Too be observed faithfully. When Peleus in the preace
A great way of behilld him thus fallen dead of this same wound,
O Crantor dearest man too me of all above the ground,
Hold here an obitgift he said: and both with force of heart
And hand, at stout Demoleons head he threw an asshen dart,
Which brake the watling of his ribs, and sticking in the bone,
Did shake. He pulled out the steal with much a do alone.
The head thereof stacke still behynd among his lungs and lyghts
Enforst too courage with his pain, he ryseth straight vprights,
And pawing at his emny with his horsish seete, he smyghts
Upon him. Peleus bare his strokes upon his burganet,
And fenst his shoulders with his sheeld, and evermore did set
His weapon upward with the point, which by his shoulders perst
Through both his breasts at one full blow. Howbeet your father erst
Had killed Hyle and Phlegrye, and Hiphinous aloof
And Danes who boldly durst at hand his manhod put in proof.
Too these was added Dorylas, who ware upon his head
A cap of woolues skin. And the horns of Oxen dy^d red
With blood were then his weapon. I (for then my courage gave
Me strength said: see how much thy horns less force than Iron have.
And therewithal with manly might a dart at him I d^aue.
Which when he could not shonne, he clapped his right hand flat upon
His forehead where the wound should be. For why his hand anon
Was nailed too his forehead fast. He roared out amayne.
And as he stood amazed and began too faint for pain,
Your father Peleus (for he stood hard by him) struck him under
The middle belly with his sword, and ripped his womb asunder.
Out girdes me Dorill straight, and trayles his guts upon ye ground
And trampling underneath his feet did break them, and they wound
About his legs so snarling, that he could no further go,
But fell down dead with empty womb Naught booted Cyllar tho
His beawtye in that frentick fray, (at leastwyse if we grant
That any might in that strange shape, of natures beawtye vaunt.)
His beard began but then too bud: his beard was like the gold:
So also were his yellowe lokes, which goodly too behold
Midway beneath his shoulders hung. There rested in his face
A sharp and lively cheerfulness with sweet and pleasant grace.
His neck, breast, shoulders, arms, and hands, as far as he was man,
Were such as never caruers woork yet stayne them could or can.
His nether part likewise (which was a horse) was every whit
Full equal with his upper part, or little worse than it.
For had ye given him horses neck, and head, he was a beast
For Castor too have ridden on. So bou^ly was his breast:
So handsome was his back too bear a saddle; and his hear
Was black as ieate, but that his tail and feet milk whyghtish were.
Full many Fema^es of ^is race did wish him too their make.
But only dame Hylonome for lover he did take.
Of all the halfbrutes in the woods there did not any dwell
More comely than Hylonome. She vi^e herself so well
In dalyance, and in loving, and in vttring of her love,
That she alone hilid Cyllarus. As much as did behoue
In suchye limbs, she trimmed them as most the eye might move.
With combing, smoothe she made her hear: she wallowed her full often
In Roses and in Rosemarye, or Uiolets sweet and soft:
Sumtyme she carried Lillyes whyght: and twyce a day she washed
Her visage in the spring that from the top of Pagase past:
And in the stream she twyce a day did bath her limbs: and on
Her leftsyde or her shoulders came the comlyest things, And none
But fynest skins of choycest beasts. Alike each loved other:
Toogither they among the hills roamd up and down: toogither
They went too covert: and that time toogither they did enter
The Lapithes house, and there the fray toogither did aduenter.
A dart on Cyllars left side came, (I know not who it sent)
Which sumwhat underneath his neck his breast a sunder splent.
As lightly as his heart was raazd, no sooner was the dart
Plucked out, but all his body wext stark cold and dyed swart.
Immediately Hylonome his dying limbs up stayed,
And put her hand upon the wound too stop the blood, and laid
Her mouth too his, and labored sore too stay his passing spryght.
But when she saw him thoroughly dead, then speaking words which might
Not too my hearing come for noise, she stikt herself upon
The weapon that had gored him, and did with him anon
Embracing him beetweene her arms. There also stood before
Mine eyes the grim Pheocomes both man and horse who wore
A Lions skin upon his ^acke fast knit with knotts before.
He snatching up a timber log (which scarcely two good teeme
Of Oxen could have stird) did throw the same with force extreme
At Phonolenyes son. The logge him all in fitters struck,
And of his head the braynepan in a thousand pieces brake,
That at his mouth, his ears, and eyes, and at his nosethrills too,
His crusshed brain came roping out as cream is wont too do
From siues or riddles made of wood, or as a Cullace out
From streyner or from Colender. But as he went about
Too strippe him from his harness as he lay upon the ground,
(Your father knows this full well) my sword his gutts did wound,
Teleboas and Cthonius both, were also slain by me.
Sir Cthonius for his weapon had a forked bough of tree.
The toother had a dart. His dart did wound. me you may see
The scarre thereof remaining yet. Then was the time that I
Should sent have been too conquer Troy. Then was the time that I
Might through my force and prowess, if not vanquish Hector stout,
Yet at the least have hilld him wag, I put you out of Dout.
But then was Hector no body: or but a babe. And now
Am I forspent and worn with years. What should I tell you how
Piretus did by Periphas? Or wherefore should I make
Long process for too tell you of sir Ampycus that struck
The fowrefoote Oecle on the face with dart of Cornell tree.
The which had neither head nor point? Or how that Macaree
Of Mountain Pelithronye with a leauer lent a blow
Too Erigdupus on the breast which did him overthrow?
Full well I do remember that Cymelius threw a dart
Which lyghted full in Nesseyes flank about his privy part.
And think not you that Mops the son of Ampycus could do
No good but only prophesye. This stout Odites whoo
Had both the shapes of man and horse, by Mopsis dart was slain,
And laboring for too speak his last he did but strive in vain.
For Mopsis dart toogither nayld his tongue and nether chappe,
And percing through his throat did make a wide and deadly gappe.
Five men had Cene already slain: their wounds I cannot say:
The names and number of them all right well I bear away.
The names of them were Stiphelus, and Brom, and Helimus,
Pyracmon with his forest bill, and stout Antimachus.
Out steps the biggest Centavvre there howge Latreus armed in
Alesus of Aemathias spoil slain late before by him.
His years were mid tweene youth and age, his courage still was young,
And on his abrun head hoar hears peerd here and there amoong
His furniture was then a sword, a target and a lawnce
Aemathian like. Too both the parts he did his face advance,
And brandishing his weapon brave, in circlewyse did prawnce
About, and stoutly spoke these words. And must I bear with you
Dame Cenye? for none other than a moother (I auow)
No better than a moother will I count thee while I live.
Remembrest not what shape by birth dame nature did the give
Forgettst thou how thou purchasedst this counterfetted shape
Of man? Consyderest what thou art by birth? and how for rape
Thou art become the thing thou art? Go take thy distaffe, and
Thy spindle, and in spinning yarne go exercise thy hand.
Let men alone with feats of arms. As Latreus made this stout,
And scornful taunting in a ring still turning him about,
This Cenye with a dart did hit him full upon the side
Where as the horse and man were joined toogither in a hyde.
The strype made Latreus mad: and with his lawnce in rage he stracke
Upon sir Cenyes naked ribs. The lawnce rebounded back
Like haylestones from a tyled house, or as a man should pat
Small stones upon a dromslets head. He came more near with that,
And in his brawned side did strive too thrust his sword. There was
No way for sword too enter in. Yet shall thou not so pass
My hands (said he.) Well since the point is blunted thou shall dye
Upon the edge: and with that word he fetched his blow awrye,
And sydling with a sweeping stroke along his belly smit.
The strype did give a clinke as if it had on marble hit.
And therewithal the sword did break, and on his neck did light.
When Ceny had sufficiently given Latreus leave too smyght
His flesh which was vnmaymeable. Well now (quod he) lets see,
If my sword able be or no too byght the flesh of thee.
In saying so, his dreadful sword as far as it would go
He underneath his shoulder thrust, and wrinching too and fro
Among his gutts, made wound in wound Behold with hydeous cry
The dowblemembred Centavvres sore abasht upon him fly,
And throw their weapons all at him. Their weapons down did fall
As if they had rebated been, and Cenye for them all
Abydes vnstriken through. Yea none was able blood too draw.
The strangeness of the case made all amazed that it saw.
Fie, fie for shame (quod Monychus) that such a rabble can
Not overcome one wight alone, who scarcely is a man.
Although (too say the very truth) he is the man, and we
Through faintness that that he was borne by nature for too be.
What profits these huge limbs of ours? what helps our dowble force?
Or what auayles our dowble shape of man as well as horse
By puissant nature joined in one? I can not think that we
Of sovereign Goddess Juno were begot, or that we be
Ixions sons, who was so stout of courage and so hault,
As that he durst on Iunos love attempt too give assault.
The emny that does vanquish us is scarcely half a man.
Whelme blocks, and stones, and mountains whole upon his hard brain pan:
And press ye out his lively ghost with trees. Let timber choke
His chappes, let weight enforce his death in stead of wounding stroke.
This said: by chance he gets a tree blown down by blustering blasts
Of Southern wynds, and on his fo with all his might it casts,
And gave example too the rest too do the like. Within
A while the shadows which did hyde mount Pehon waxed thin:
And not a tree was left upon mount Othris ere they went.
Sir Cenye underneath this great howge pyle of timber pent,
Did chauf and on his shoulders hard the heavy logges did bear.
But when above his face and head the trees up stacked were,
So that he had no venting place too draw his breath: One while
He faynted: and anotherwhyle he heaued at the pyle,
Too tumble down the loggs that lay so heavy on his back,
And for too win the open air ageine above the stacke:
As if the mountain Ida (lo) which yoonder we do see
So high, by earth quake at a time should chance to shaken be.
Men dowt what did become of him. Sum hold opinion that
The burden of the woods had driven his soul too Limbo flat.
But Mopsus said it was not so. For he did see a brown
Bird flying from amid the stacke and towering up and down.
It was the first time and the last that ever I behild
That foul. When Mopsus softly saw him soring in the feeld,
He looked wistly after him, and cried out on hye.
Hayle peerlesse perle of Lapith race, hayle Ceny, late ago
A valiant knight, and now a bird of whom there is no mo.
The author caused men believe the matter too be so.
Our sorrow set us in a rage. It was too us a greef
That by so many foes one knight was killd without releef.
Then ceast we not too wreake our ^eene till most was slain in fight,
And that the rest discomfit^d were fled away by night.
As Nestor all the process of this battle did reherce
Between the valiant Lapithes and misshapen Centavvres ferce,
Tlepolemus displeased sore that Hercules was past
With silence, could not hold his peace, but out these words did cast.
My Lord, I muse you should forget my fathers praise so quyght.
For often unto me himself was woonted too recite,
How that the clowdbred folk by him were chiefly put too flight.
Right sadly Nestor answered thus. Why should you me constreyne
Too call too mind forgotten greefs? and for to reere ageine
The sorrows now outworne by time? or force me too declare
The hatred and displeasure which I too your father bare?
In sooth his doings greater were than might be well believed.
He filled the world with high renown which nobly he atcheeued.
Which thing I would I could deny. For neither set we out
Deiphobus, Polydamas, nor Hector that most stout
And valiant knight the strength of Troy. For whoo will praise his fo?
Your father overthrew the walls of Messen long ago,
And razed Pyle, and Ely towns unworthy serving so.
And feerce against my fathers house he used both sword and fire.
And (not too speak of others whom he killed in his tree)
Twyce six we were the sons of Nele all lusty gentlemen.
Twyce six of us (excepting me) by him were murthred then.
The death of all the rest might seem a matter not so strange:
But strange was Periclymens death whoo had the power too change
And leave and take what shape he list (by Neptune too him given,
The founder of the house of Nele.) For when he had been driven
Too try all shapes, and none could help: he last of all became
The foul that in his hooked feet does bear the flasshing flame
Sent down from heaven by Jupiter. He practicing those birds,
With flapping wings, and bowwing beak, and hooked talants girds
At Hercle, and beescratcht his face. Too certain (I may say)
Thy father amde his shaft at him. For as he towering lay
Among the clouds, he hit him underneath the wing. The stroke
Was small: Howbeet because therewith the sinews being broke,
He wanted strength too maynteine flight, he fell me too the ground,
Through weakness of his wing. The shaft that sticked in the wound,
By reason of the burden of his body perst his side,
And at the leftsyde of his neck all bloodye forth did glyde.
Now tell me O thou beawtyfull Lord Amirall of the fleet
Of Rhodes, if me too speak the praise of Hercle it be meet.
But least that of my brothers deaths men think I do desire
A further vendge than silence of the prowess of thy sire,
I love thee even with all my heart, and take thee for my friend.
When Nestor of his pleasant tales had made this freendly end,
They called for a boll of wine, and from the table went,
And all the resdew of the night in sleeping soundly spent.
But neptune like a father took the matter sore too heart
That Cygnet too a Swan he was constreyned too convert.
And hating feerce Achilles, he did wreake his cruel teene
Upon him more discourteously than had beseeming been.
For when the wars well near full twyce five years had lasted. He
Unshorne Apollo thus bespake. O neuew unto me
Most dear of all my brothers impes, who helpedst me too lay
Foundation of the walls of Troy for which we had no pay,
And can thou syghes forbear too see the Asian Empire fall?
And does it not lament thy heart when thou too mind do call
So many thousand people slain in keeping Ilion wall?
Or (too thentent particlerly I do not speak of all)
Remembrest thou not Hectors Ghost whoo harryed was about
His town of Troy? where nerethelesse Achilles that same stout
And far in fight more butcherly, whoo stryues with all his might
Too stroy the work of me and thee, lives still in healthful plyght*
If ever he do come within my danger he shall feel
What force is in my tryple mace. But since with sword of steel
I may not meet him as my fo, I pray thee vnbeeware
Go kill him with a sodeine shaft and rid me of my care.
Apollo did consent: as well his uncle for too please,
As also for a pryuate grudge himself had for too ease.
And in a clowd he down among the host of Troy did slide,
Where Paris dribbling out his shaftes among the Greeks he spyde:
And telling him what God he was, said wherefore do thou waast
Thine arrows on the simple sort? It any care thou haste
Of those that are thy friends, go turn against Achilles head,
And like a man reuendge on him thy brothers that are dead.
In saying this, he brought him where Achilles with his brond
Was beating down the Troiane folk, and leueld so his hond
As that Achilles tumbled down stark dead upon the land.
This was the only thing whereof the old king Priam might
Take comfort after Hectors death. That stout and valiant knight
Achilles whoo had overthrown so many men in fight,
Was by that coward carpet knight beereeued of his life.
Whoo like a caytif stale away the Spartane princes wife.
But if of weapon womanish he had foreknowen it had
His destnye been too lose his life, he would have been more glad
That Queen Penthesileas bill had slain him out of hand.
Now was the fear of Phrygian folk, the only glory, and
Defense of Greeks, that peerless prince in arms, Achilles turned
Too ashes. That same God that had him armd, him also burnd.
Now is he dust: and of that great Achilles bydeth still.
A thing of naught, that scarcely can a little coffin fill.
Howbeet his worthy fame does live, and spreads over all
The world, a measure meet sor such a person too beefall.
This matcheth thee Achilles full. And this can never dye.
His target also (too thentent that men might plainly spy
What wyghts it was) did move debate, and for his armor burst
Out deadly food. Not Diomed, nor Ajax Oylye durst
Make claim or chalendge too the same, nor Atreus yoonger son,
Nor yet his elder, though in arms much honor they had won.
Alone the sons of Telamon and Laert did assay
Which of them two of that great pryse should bear the bell away.
But Agamemnon from himself the burden putts, and cleeres
His hands of envy, causing all the Capteines and the Peers
Of Greece too meet amid the camp toogither in a place,
Too whom he put the heering and the judgment of the case.
Finis duodecimi Libri.
THE.XIII. BOOK OF Ouids Metamorphosis.
THe Lords and Capteynes being set toogither with the King,
And all the soldiers standing round about them in a ring,
The owner of the seuenfold sheeld, too these did Ajax rise.
And (as he could not bridle wrath) he cast his frowning eyes
Upon the shore and on the fleet that there at Anchor lies
And throwing up his hands, O God and must we plead (quod he)
Our case before our ships? and must Ulysses stand with me?
But like a wretch he ran his way when Hector came with fire,
Which I defending from these ships did force him too retire.
It easyer is therefore with words in print too maynteine strife,
Than for too fight it out with fists. But neither I am ryfe
In words, nor he in deeds. For look how far I him excel
In battle and in feats of arms: so far bears he the bell
From me in talking. Neither think I requisite too tell
My acts among you. You your selves have seen them verry well.
But let Ulysses tell you his done all in hudther mudther,
And whervntoo the only night is privy and none other.
The pryse is great (I do confess.) For which we strive. But yet
It is dishonor unto me, for that in clayming it
So bace a person stands in contention for the same.
Too think it mine already, ought too counted be no shame
Nor pride in me: although the thing of right great value be
Of which Ulysses stands in hope. For now already he
Has won the honor of this pryse, in that when he shall sit
Besides the quisshon, he may brag he straue with me for it.
And though I wanted valiantness, yet should nobilitee
Make with me. I of Telamon am known the son too be
Who under valiant Hercules the walls of Troy did scale,
And in the ship of Pagasa too Colchos land did sail.
His father was that Aeacus whoo executes right
Among the ghostes where Sisyphus heaues up with all his might
The massye stone ay tumbling down. The highest Jove of all
Acknowledges this Aeacus, and does his son him call.
Thus am I Ajax the third from Jove. Yet let this Pedigree
O Achyues in this case of mine avaylable not be,
Unless I prove it fully with Achylles too agree
He was my brother, and I claim that was my brothers. Why
Should thou that art of Sisyphs blood, and for too filch and lie
Expressest him in every point, by foorged pedigree
Aly thee too the Aeacyds, as though we did not see
Thee too the house of Aeacus, a stranger for too be?
And is it reason that you should this armor me deny
Because I former was in arms, and needed not a spy
Too fetch me forth? Or think you him more woorthye it too have,
That came too warrefare hindermost, and feynd himself too rave,
Because he would have shund the war? until a suttler head
And more unprofitable for himself, sir Palamed
Escryde the crafty fetches of his fearful heart, and drew
Him forth a warfare which he sought so cowardly too eschew?
Must he now needs enjoy the best and richest armor? whoo
Would none at all have worn unless he forced were thertoo?
And I with shame be put beside my cousin germans gifts
Because too shun the formest brunt of wars I sought no shifts?
Would God this mischeef master had in verrye deed been mad,
Or else believed so too be: and that we never had
Brought such a panion unto Troy. Then should not Paeans son
In Lemnos like an outlawe too the shame of all us won.
Who lurking now (as men report) in woods and caves, does move
The verry flints with syghes and groans, and prayers too God above
Too send Ulysses his desert. Which prayer (if there be
A God) must one day take effect. And now beehold how he
By oath a Soldier of our Camp, yea and as well as we
A Captain too, alas, (who was by Hercules assignde
Too have the keeping of his shafts,) with pain and hungar pynde,
Is clad and fed with fowls, and dribs his arrows up and down
At birds, which were by destinye preparde too stroy Troy town.
Yet lives he because he is not still in company
With sly Ulysses. Palamed that wretched knight perdie,
Would eeke he had abandond been. For then should still the same
Have been alive: or at the least have did without our shame.
But this companion bearing (ah) too well in wicked mind
His madness which sir Palamed by wisdom out did find,
Appeached him of treason that he practysde too betray
The Greekish host. And for too bouch the fact, he showed straight way
A mass of goold that he himself had hidden in his tent,
And forged Letters which he feynd from Priam too be sent.
Thus either by his murthring men or else by banishment
Abateth he the Greekish strength. This is Ulysses fight.
This is the fear he puttes men in. But though he had more might
Than Nestor has, in eloquence he shall not compass me
Too think his leawd abandoning of Nestor for too be
No fault: who being cast behynd by wounding of his horse,
And slow with age, with calling on Ulysses waxing hoarce,
Was nerethelesse betrayed by him. Sir Diomed knows this crime
Is vnsurmysde. For he himself did at that present time
Rebuke him oftentimes by name, and feercely him upbraid
With flying from his fellow so who stood in need of aid.
With ryghtfull eyes does God behold the deeds of mortal men.
Lo, he that helped not his friend wants help himself again.
And as he did forsake his friend in time of need: so he
Did in the selfsame perrill fall forsaken for too be.
He made a rod too beat himself. He called and cried out
Upon his fellows. Straight I came: and there I saw the lout
Both quake and shake for fear of death, and look as pale as clout.
I set my sheeld between him and his foes, and him bestrid:
And savde the dastards life. small praise redoundes of that I did.
But if thou will contend with me, lets to the self same place
Agein: be wounded as thou wart: and in the foresaid case
Of fear, beset about with foes: cowch underneath my sheeld:
And then contend thou with me there amid the open feeld.
Howbeet, I had no sooner rid this champion of his foes,
But where for wounds he scarce before could totter on his toes,
He ran away apace, as though he naught at all did ayle.
Anon commes Hector too the feeld and brings at his tail
The Gods. Not only thy heart there ( Ulysses) did the fail,
But even the stowtest courages and stomachs began too quayle.
So great a terror brought he in. Yet in the midds of all
His bloody ruff, I coapt with him, and with a foyling fall
Did overthrow him too the ground. Another time, when he
Did make a chalendge, you my Lords by lot did choose out me,
And I did match him hand too hand. Your wisshes were not vain.
For if you ask me what success our combat did obtain,
I came away vnvanquished. Behold the men of Troy
Brought fire and sword, and all the feendes our nauye too destroy.
And where was slye Ulysses then with all his talk so smooth?
This breast of mine was feign too fence your thousand ships forsooth
The hope of your returning home. For saving that same day
So many ships, this armor give. But (if that I shall say
The truth) the greater honor now this armor bears away.
And our renownes toogither link. For (as of reason ought)
An Ajax for this armor, not an armor now is sought
For Ajax. Let Dulychius match with these, the horses whyght
Of Rhesus, dastard Dolon, and the coward carpetknyght
King Priams Helen, and the stelth of Palladye by night.
Of all these things was nothing done by day nor nothing wrought
Without the help of Diomed. And therefore if ye thought
Too give them too so small deserts, deuyde the same, and let
Sir Diomed have the greater part. But what should Ithacus get
And if he had them? Who does all his matters in the dark,
Who never wears armor, who shootes ay at his own mark
Too trappe his ^o by stelth unawares? The very headpeece may
With brightness of the glistring gold his privy feats bewray
And show him lurking. Neither well of force Dulychius were
The weight of great Achilles helm upon his pate too wear
It cannot but a burden be (and that right great) too bear
(With whose same shrimpish arms of his) Achilles mighty spear.
Again his target graven with the whole howge world thereon
Agrees not with a fearful hand, and chiefly such a one
As takes filching even by kind. Thou Lozell thou do seek
A gift that will but weaken thee* which if the folk of Greek
Shall give thee through their ouersyght, it will be unto thee
Occasion, of thine emnyes spoyld not feared for too be
And flight (wherein thou coward, thou all others may outbrag)
Will hindered be when after thee such masses thou shall drag.
Moreover this thy sheeld that feels so seeld the force of fight
Is sound. But mine is gasht and hakt and stricken thurrough quyght
A thousand times, with bearing blows. And therefore mine must walk
And put another in his stead. But what needs all this talk?
Lets now be seen another while what each of us can do.
The thickest of our armed foes this armor throw intoo,
And bid us fetch the same fro thence. And which of us does fetch
The same away, reward ye him therewith. Thus far did stretch
The words of Ajax. At the end whereof there did ensew
A muttring of the soldiers, till Laertis son the prew
Stood up, and raised soberly his eyliddes from the ground
(On which he had a little while them pitched in a stound)
And looking on the noblemen who longd his words too here
He thus began with comely grace and sober pleasant cheer.
My Lords, if my desire and yours might erst have taken place,
It should not at this present time have been a dowtfull case,
What person has most right too this great pryse for which we strive.
Achilles should his armor have, and we still him alive.
Whom since that cruel destiny too both of us denies,
^With that same word as though he wept, he wypte his watery eyes)
What wight of reason rather ought too be Achilles heir,
Than he through whom too this your camp Achilles did repair?
Alonly let it not avail sir Ajax here, that he
Is such a dolt and grossehead, as he shows himself too be
Ne let my wit (which ay has done you good O Greeks) hurt me.
But suffer this mine eloquence (such as it is) which now
Does for his master speak, and often ere this has spoke for you,
Be vndisdeynd. Let none refuse his own good gifts he brings.
For as for stock and auncetors, and other such like things
Whereof our selves no fownders are, I scarcely dare them grant
Too be our own. But forasmuch as Ajax makes his vaunt
Too be the fowrth from Jove: even Jove the founder is also
Of my house: and than four descents I am from him no mo.
Laertes is my father, and Arcesius his, and he
Begotten was of Jupiter. And in this pedigree
Is neither any damned soul, nor outlaw as ye see.
Moreover by my moothers side I come of Mercuree,
