Another honor too my house. Thus both by fathers side
And moothers (as you may perceive) I am too Gods alyde.
But neither for because I am a better gentleman
Than Ajax by the moothers side, nor that my father can
Avouch himself vngiltye of his brothers blood, do I
This armor claim. wey you the case by merits uprightly.
Provided no prerogatyue of birthryght Ajax bear,
For that his father Telamon, and Peleus brothers were.
Let only prowess in this pryse the honor bear away.
Or if the case on kinrid or on birthryght seem too stay,
His father Peleus is alive, and Pyrrhus eeke his son.
What title then can Ajax make. This geere of right should woone
Too Phthya, or too Scyros I Will. And Tevvcer is as well
Achilles uncle as is he. Yet does not Tevvcer mell.
And if he did, should he obtain? well since the case does rest
On trial which of us can prove his doings too be best,
I needs must say my deeds are mo than well I can express:
Yet will I show them orderly as near as I can guess.
Foreknowing that her son should dye, The Lady Thetis hid
Achilles in a maids attire. By which fine slyght she did
All men deceive, and Ajax too. This armor in a pack
With other women's tryflyng toys I carried on my back,
A bayte too treyne a manly heart. Appareld like a maid
Achilles took the spear and sheeld in hand, and with them played.
Then said I: O thou Goddess son, why should thou be afraid
Too raze great Troy, whoose overthrow for thee is only stayed?
And laying hand upon him I did send him (as you see)
Too valiant doings meet for such a valiant man as he.
And therefore all the deeds of him are my deeds. I did wound
King Teleph with his spear, and when he lay upon the ground,
I was entreated with the spear too heal him safe and sound.
That Thebe lies overthrown, is my deed you must think
I made the folk of Tenedos and Lesbos for too shrink.
Both Chryse and Cillas Phoebus towns and Scyros I did take.
And my right hand Lyrnessus walls too ground did level make.
I gave you him that should confound (besides a number mo)
The valiant Hector. Hector that our most renowned fo
Is slain by me. This armor here I sew agein too have
This armor by the which I found Achilles. I it gave
Achilles while he was alive: and now that he is gone
I claim it as mine own agein. What time the grief of one
Had perst the hearts of all the Greeks, and that our thousand sail
At Avvlis by Evvboya stayed, because the wyndes did fail,
Continewing either none at all or cleene against us long,
And that our Agamemnon was by destnyes ouerstrong
Commanded for too sacrifyse his gil^lesse daughter too
Diana, which her father then refusing for too do
Was angry with the Godds themselves, and though he were a king
Continued also fatherlyke: by reason, I did bring
His gentle nature too relent for public profits sake.
I must confess (whereat his grace shall no displeasure take)
Before a parciall judge I undertook a right hard case.
Howbeeit for his brothers sake, and for the royal mace
Committed, and his peoples weal, at length he was content
Too purchase praise with blood. Then was I too the moother sent,
Who not persuaded was too be, but compast with sum guyle.
Had Ajax on this errand gone, our ships had all this while
Line still there yet for want of wynd. Moreover I was sent
Too Ilion as ambassador. I boldly thither went,
And entered and behilld the Court, wherein there was as then
Great store of princes, Dukes, Lords, knyghts, and other valiant men.
And yet I boldly nerethelesse my message did at large
The which the whole estate of Greece had given me erst in charge.
I made complaint of Paris, and accusde him too his head.
Demanding restitution of Queen Helen that same sted
And of the bootye with her taken. Both Priamus the king
And eeke Antenor his alye the words of me did sting.
And Paris and his brothers, and the resdew of his train
That under him had made the spoil, could hard and scarce refrain
There wicked hands. You Menelay do know I do not feyne.
And that day was the first in which we jointly began sustain
A taste of perrills, store whereof did then behind remain.
It would be ouerlong too tell each profitable thing
That during this long lasting war I well too pass did bring,
By force as well as pollycie. For after that the furst
Encounter once was ouerpast, our emnyes never durst
Give battle in the open feeld, but hild themselves within
Their walls and bulwarks till the time the tenth year did begin,
Now what did thou of all that while, that can do naught but streeke?
Or too what purpose seruedst thou? For if thou my deeds seek,
I practysd sundry pollycies too trappe our foes unware:
I fortifyde our Camp with trench which heretoofore lay bare:
I hartned our companions with a quiet mind too bear
The longnesse of the weery war: I taught us how we were
Both too be fed and furnished: and too and fro I went
Too places where the Counsel thought most meet I should be sent.
Behold the king deceived in his dream by false pretense
Of Jove's commandment, bade us raise our seedge and get us hence.
The author of his doing so may well be his defense.
Now Ajax should have let this, and called them back ageine
Too sack the town of Troy. he should have fought with might and maine.
Why did he not restreyne them when they ready were too go?
Why took he not his sword in hand? why gave he not as tho
Sum counsel for the fleeting folk too follow at the brunt?
In faith it had a tryfle been too him that ay is wont.
Such vaunting in his mouth too have. But he himself did fly
As well as others. I did see, and was ashamed I
Too see thee when thou fledst, and did prepare so cowardly
Too sail away. And thervppon I thus aloud did cry.
What meene ye sirs? what madness does you move too go too ship?
And suffer Troy as good as taken, thus out of hand too slip?
What else this tenth year bear ye home than shame? with such like word
And other, (which the eloquence of sorrow did a^oord,)
I brought them from their flying ships. Then Agamemnon called
Toogither all the capteines who with fear were yet appalld.
But Ajax durst not then once cre^ke. Yet durst Thersites be
So bold as rail upon the kings, and he was paid by me
For playing so the saweye Jack. Then stood I on my toes
And too my fearful countrymen gave heart against their foes.
And shed new courage in their mynds through talk that fro me goes.
From that time forth what ever thing has valeantly atcheeued
By this good fellow been, is mine, whoo him from flight repreeued.
And now too touch thee: which of all the Greeks commends thee?
Or seeks thee? But Diomed communicates with me
His doings, and allows me, and thinks him well apayd
Too have Ulysses ever as companion at the brayd.
And sumwhat worth you will it grant (I trow) alone for me
Out of so many thousand Greeks by Diomed pikt too be.
No lot compelled me too go, and yet I setting light,
As well the perrill of my foes as danger of the night,
Killd Dolon who about the self same fea^e that night did stray,
That we went out for. But I first compelld him too bewray
All things concerning faythlesse Troy, and what it went about.
When all was learnd, and nothing left behynd too harken out,
I might have then come home with praise. I was not so content.
Proceeding fruther too the Camp of Rhesus straight I went,
And killed both himself and all his men about his tent.
And taking both his chariot and his horses which were whyght,
Returned home in triumph like a conqueror from fight.
Deny you me the armor of the man whoose steedes the fo
Required for his playing of the spy a night, and so
May Ajax be more kind too me than you are what should I
Declare unto you how my sword did waste right valeantly
Sarpedons host of Lycia? I by force did overthrow
Alastor, Crome* and Ceranos, and Haly on a row.
Alcander, and Noemon too, and Prytanis beside,
And Thoon and Theridamas, and Charops also did
By me, and so did Evvnomos enforst by cruel fate.
And many mo in sight of Troy I slew of bacer state.
There also are (O countrymen) about me woundings, which
The place of them make beawtyfull. See here (his hand did twich
His shirt aside) and credit not vain words. Lo here the bri^t
That always too be one in your affairs has never mist.
And yet of all this while no drop of blood has Ajax spent
Upon his fellows. Woundlesse is his body and vnrent.
But what skills that, as long as he is able for to vaunt
He fought against both Troy and Jove too save our fleet? I grant
He did so. For I am not of such nature as of spyght
Well doings too deface: so that he chalendge not the right
Of all men too himself alone, and that he yield too me
Sum share, whoo of the honor look a partener for too be.
Patroclus also having on Achilles armor, sent
The Trojans and their leader hence, too burn our nauye bent.
And yet thinks he that none durst meet with Hector saving he.
Forgetting both the king, and eeke his brother, yea and me.
Where he himself was but the nyneth, appointed by the king,
And by the fortune of his lot preferred too do the thing.
But now for all your valeantnesse, what Issue had I pray
Your combat? shall I tell? forsoothe, that Hector went his way
And had no harm. Now woe is me how greeueth it my heart
Too think upon that season when the bulwark of our part
Achilles did? When neither tears, nor greef, nor fear could make
Me for too stay, but that upon these shoulders I did take,
I say upon these shoulders I Achilles body took,
And this same armor claspt thereon, which now too wear I look.
Sufficient strength I have too bear as great a weight as this,
And eeke a heart wherein regard of honor rooted is.
Think you that Thetis for her son so instantly besought
Sir Vulcane this same heavenly gift too give her, which is wrought
With such exceeding cunning, too thentent a soldier that
Has neither wit nor knowledge should it wear? He knows not wha^
The things engraven on the sheeld do meene. Of Ocean se,
Of land, of heaven, and of the stars no skill at all has he.
The Bear that never dyues in sea he does not understand,
The Pleyads, nor the Hyads, nor the Cities that do stand
Upon the earth, nor yet the sword that Orion holds in hand.
He seeks too have an armor of the which he has no skill.
And yet in fynding fault with me because I had no will
Too follow this same paynfull war and sought too shonne the same,
And made it sumwhat longer time before I thither came,
He sees not how he speaks reproach too stout Achilles name.
For if too have dissembled in this case, ye count a crime,
We both offenders be. Or if protracting of the time
Ye count blame woorthye, yet was I the tymelyer of us twain.
Achilles loving moother him, my wife did me deteyne.
The former time was given too them, the rest was given too you.
And therefore do I little pass although I could not now
Defend my fault, since such a man of prowess, birth and fame
As was Achilles, was with me offender in the same.
But yet was he espied by Ulysses wit, but not
Ulysses by sir Ajax wit* And least ye wonder at
The railing of this foolish dolt at me, he does object
Reproach too you. For if that I offended too detect
Sir Palamed of forged fault, could you without your shame
Arreyne him, and ^ondemne him eeke too suffer for the same?
But neither could sir Palamed excuse him of the crime
So heinous and so manifest: and you your selves that time
Not only his indytement hard, but also did behold
His deed auowched too his face by bringing in the gold.
And as for Philoctetes, that he is in Lemnos, I
Deserve not too be touched therewith. Defend your crime: for why
You all consented thervntoo. Yet do I not deny,
But that I gave the counsel too convey him out of way
From toil of war and travel that by rest he might assay
Too ease the greatness of his peynes. He did theretoo obey
And by so doing is alive. Not only faithful was
This counsel that I gave the man, but also happy, as
The good success has showed since. Whom since the destnyes do^
Require in oue^throwing Troy, Appoint not me thertoo:
But let sir Ajax rather go. For he with ^loquence
Or by some subtle pollycie, shall bring the man fro thence
And pacyfie him raging through disease, and wrathful ire.
Nay. first the river Simois shall too his spring retire,
And mountain Ida shall thereon have stonding never a tree,
Yea and the faythlesse town of Troy by Greeks shall reskewd be,
Before that Ajax blockish wit shall aught at all avail,
When my attempts and practyses in your affairs do fail.
For though thou Philoctetes with the king offended be,
And with thy fellows euerychone, and most of all with me,
Although thou curse and ban me too the hellish pit for ay,
And w^sshest in thy pain that I by chance might cross thy way,
Of purpose for too draw my blood: yet will I give assay
Too fetch thee hither once ageine. And (if that fortune say
Amen,) I will as well have thee and eeke thine arrows, as
I have the Troiane prophet whoo by me surprysed was,
Or as I did the Oracles and Troiane fa^es disclose,
Or as I from her chapel through the thickest of her foes
The Phrygian Pallads image fetched: And yet does Ajax still
Compare himself with me. Ye know it was the destnyes will
That Troy should never taken be by any force, until
This Image first were got. and where was then our valiant knight
Sir Ajax? where the stately words of such a hardy wight?
Why fears he? why dares Ulysses ventring through the watch
Commit his person too the night his buysnesse too dispatch?
And through the pykes not only for too pass the guarded wall?
But also for too enter too the strongest tower of all?
And for too take the Idol from her Chapel and her shryne?
And bear her thence amid his foes? For had this deed of mine
Been left undone, in vain his sheeld of Oxen hydes seven fold
Should yet the Son of Telamon have in his left hand hold.
That night subdewed I Troy town. that night did I it win.
And opened it for you likewise with ease too enter in.
Cease too upbraid me by these looks and mumbling words of thine
With Diomed: his praise is in this fact as well as mine.
And thou thy self when for our ships thou did in reskew stand,
Wart not alone: the multitude were helping thee at hand.
I had but only one with me. Whoo (if he had not thought
A wyseman better than a strong, and that preferment ought
Not always follow force of hand) would now himself have sought
This Armor. So would toother Ajax better stayed do,
And feerce Evvrypyle, and the son of hault Andremon too.
No less might eeke Idominey, and eeke Meriones
His countryman, and Menelay. For every one of these
Are valiant men of hand, and not inferior unto thee
In martial feats. And yet they are contented ruled too be
By mine aduyce. Thou have a hand that serves well in fight.
Thou have a wit that stands in need of my direction right.
Thy force is witless. I have care of that that may ensew.
Thou well can fight: the king does choose the times for fyghting dew
By mine aduyce. Thou only with thy body can avail.
But I with body and with mind too profit do not fail,
And look how much the master does excel the gally slave,
Or look how much pre-eminence the Captain ought too have
Above his souldyer: even so much excel I also thee.
A wit far passing strength of hand enclosed is in me.
In wit rests chiefly all my force. My Lords I pray bestow
This gift on him who ay has been your watchman as ye know.
And for my ten years cark and care endured for your sake
Full recompense for my deserts with this same honor make.
Our labor draws too an end, all lets are now by me
Dispatched. And by bringing Troy in case too taken be
I have already taken it. Now by the hope that ye
Conceive, within a while of Troy the ruin for too see,
And by the Gods of whom a late our emnyes I bereft,
And as by wisdom too be done yet any thing is left,
If any bold auentrous deed, or any perlous thing,
That asks hazard both of life and limb too pass too bring,
Or if ye think of Troiane fates there yet does ought remain,
Remember me. or if from me this armor you restrain,
Bestow it on this same. With that he showed with his hand
Mineruas fatal image, which hard by in sight did stand.
The Lords were moved with his words, and then appeered plain
The force that is in eloquence. The learned man did gain
The armor of the valiant. He that did so often sustain
Alone both fire, and sword, and Jove, and Hector could not bide
One brunt of wrath. And whom no force could vanquish ere that tide,
Now only anguish ouercommes. He draws his sword and says:
Well: this is mine yet. Untoo this no claim Ulysses lays.
This must I use against myself: this blade that heretoofore
Has bathed been in Troiane blood, must now his master gor^
That none may Ajax overcome save Ajax. With that word.
Intoo his breast (not wounded erst) he thrust his deathfull sword.
His hand too pull it out ageine unable was. The blood
Did spout it out. Anon the ground bestayned where he stood,
Did breed the pretye purple flower upon a clowre of green,
Which of the wound of Hyacinth had erst engendered been.
The selfsame letters eeke that for the child were written than,
Were now again amid the flower new written for the man.
The former time complaynt, the last a name did represent.
Ulysses having won the pryse, within a while was sent
Too Thoants and Hypsiphiles realm the land defamde of old
For murdering all the men therein by women over bold.
At length attayning land and luck according too his mind,
Too carry Hercles arrows back he set his sails too wynd.
Which when he with the lord of them among the Greeks had brought,
And of the cruel war at length the utmost feate had wrought,
At once both Troy and Priam fell. And Priams wretched wife
Lost (after all) her woman's shape, and barked all her life
In foreign country. In the place that brings too a straight
The long spread sea of Hellespont, did Ilion burn in height.
The kindled fire with blazing flame continewed vnalayd,
And Priam with his aged blood Jove's Altar had berayd.
And Phoebus preestesse casting up her hands too heaven on hye,
Was dragd and haled by the hear. The Grayes most spyghtfully
(As each of them had prisoners taken in meede of victory)
Did draw the Troiane wives away, whoo lingering while they might
Among the burning temples of their Gods, did hang about
Their sacred shrynes and images. Astyanax down was cast
From that same turret from the which his moother in time past
Had showed him his father stand often fyghting too defend
Himself and that same famous realm of Troy that did descend
From many noble auncetors. And now the northern wynd
With prosperous blasts, too get them thence did put y^ Greeks in mind.
The shipmen went aboard, and hoyst up sails, and made fro thence.
A deew dear Troy (the women cryde) we haled are from hence.
And therwithall they kissed the ground, and left yet smoking still
Their native houses. Last of all took ship against her will
Queen Hecub: who (a piteous case too see) was found amid
The tumbes in which her sons were laid. And there as Hecub did
Embrace their chists and kiss their bones, Ulysses void of care
Did pull her thence. Yet raught she up, and in her boosom bare
Away a crum of Hectors dust, and left on Hectors grave
Her hory hears and tears, which for poor offerings she him gave.
Against the place where Ilion was, there is another land
Manured by the Biston men. In this same Realm did stand
King Polemnestors palace rich, too whom king Priam sent
His little infant Polydore too foster, too thentent
He might be out of danger from the wars: wherein he meant
Right wisely, had he not with him great riches sent, a bayt
Too stir a wicked covetous mind too treason and deceyt.
For when the state of Troy decayed, the wicked king of Thrace
Did cut his nur^echylds weazant, and (as though the sinful case
Toogither with the body could have quyght been put away)
He threw him also in the sea. It happened by the way,
That Agamemnon was compeld with all his fleet too stay
Upon the coast of Thrace, until the sea were wexen calm,
And till the hideous storms did cease, and furious wynds were fallen.
Here rising ghastly from the ground which far about him brake,
Achilles with a threatening look did like resemblance make,
As when at Agamemnon he his wrongful sword did shake,
And said: Unmyndfull part ye hence of me O Greeks? and must
My merits thanklesse thus with me be buried in the dust?
Nay, do not so. But too thentent my death dew honor have,
Let Polyxene in sacrifyse be slain upon my grave.
Thus much he said: and shortly his companions doing as
By vision of his cruel ghost commaundment given them was,
Did fetch her from her mothers lap, whom at that time, well near,
In that most great adversity alonly she did cheer.
The haultye and unhappy maid, and rather too be thought
A man than woman, too the tumb with cruel hands was brought,
Too make a cursed sacrifyse. Whoo mynding constantly
Her honor, when she standing at the Altar pressed too dye,
Perceyvd the savage ceremonies in making ready, and
The cruel Neoptolemus with naked sword in hand,
Stand staring with ungentle eyes upon her gentle face,
She said. Now use thou when thou will my gentle blood. The case
Requyres no more delay. bestow thy weapon in my chest,
Or in my throat: (in saying so she proffered bare her breast,
And eeke her throat.) Assure your selves it never shall be seen,
That any wight shall (by my will) have slave of Polyxeene.
Howbeet with such a sacrifyse no God ye can delyght.
I would desire no more but that my wretched moother might
Be ignorant of this my death. My moother hinders me,
And makes the pleasure of my death much lesser for too be.
Howbeeit not the death of me should justly grieve her heart:
But her own life. Now too thentent I freely may depart
Too Limbo, stand ye men aloof: and since I ask but right
Forbear too touch me. So my blood vnsteyned in his sight
Shall far more acceptable be what ever wight he be
Whom you prepare too pacifye by sacrifysing me.
Yet (if that these last words of mine may purchase any grace,)
I daughter of king Priam erst, and now in prisoners case,
Beeseeche you all vnraunsomed too render too my moother
My body: and for burial of the same too take none other
Reward than tears: for while she could she did redeem with gold.
This said: the tears that she forbear the people could not hold.
And even the verry priest himself full sore against his will
And weeping, thrust her through the breast which she hild stoutly still.
She sinking softly too the ground with faynting legs, did bear
Even too the verry latter gasp a coun^nance void of fear.
And when she fell, she had a care such parts of her too hyde,
As womanhod and chastity forbids too be spyde.
The Troiane women took her up, and moorning reckened
King Priams children, and what blood that house alone had shed.
They sight for fayer Polyxeene: they syghed eeke for thee
Whoo late wart Priams wife, whoo late wart counted for too be
The flower of Asia in his flower, and Queen of moothers all:
But now the bootye of the so as evil lot did fall,
And such a bootye as the sly Ulysses did not pass
Upon her, saving that erewhyle she Hectors moother was.
So hardly for his moother could a master Hector find.
Embracing in her aged arms the body of the mind
That was so stout, she powrd thereon with sobbing syghes vnsoft
The tears that for her husband and her children had so often
And for her country sheaded been. She weeped in her wound
And kissed her pretye mouth, and made her brist with strokes too sound,
According too her woonted guyse, and in the iellyed blood
Beerayed all her grisild hear, and in a sorrowful mood
Said these and many other words with bre^t bescratcht and rent.
O daughter mine, the last for whom thy moother may lament,
(For what remaynes?) O daughter thou art dead and gone. I see
Thy wound which at the verry heart strikes me as well as thee.
And least that any one of mine unwounded should depart,
Thou also gotten have a wound. Howbeet because thou wart
A woman, I believed thee from weapon too be free.
But notwithstanding that thou art a woman, I do see
Thee slain by sword. Even he that killed thy brothers kills thee,
Achilles the decay of Troy and maker bare of me.
What time that he of Paris shaft by Phoebus means was slain,
I said of feerce Achilles now no fear does more remain.
But then, even then he most of all was feared for too be.
The ashes of him rages still against our race I see.
We feel an emny of him dead and buried in his grave
Too feed Achilles fury, I a fruitful issue gave.
Great Troy lies under foot, and with a right great grievous fall
The mischeeues of the common weal are fully ended all.
But though too others Troy be gone, yet stands it still too me:
My sorrows run as fresh a race as ever and as free.
I late a go a souereine state, advanced with such store
Of daughters, sons, and sonneinlawes, and husband over more
And daughtrinlawes, am carried like an outlawe bare and poor,
By force and violence haled from my childrens tumbes, too be
Presented too Penelope a gift, whoo showing me
In spinning my appointed task, shall say: this same is she
That was sumtyme king Priams wife, this was the famous moother
Of Hector. And now after loss of such a sort of other,
Thou (whoo alonly in my grief my comfort did remain,)
Too pacifye our emnyes wrath upon his tumb art slain.
Thus bare I deathgyfts for my foes. Too what intent am I
Most wretched wight remaining still? why do I linger? why
Does hurtful age preserve me still alive? too what intent
Ye cruel Gods reserve ye me that has already spent
Too many years? unless it be new buryalls for too see?
And whoo would think that Priamus might happy counted be
Since Troy is razed? Happy man is he in being dead.
His life and kingdoome he forwent toogither: and this stead
He sees not thee his daughter slain. But peradventure thou
Shall like the daughter of a king have sumptuous buryall now,
And with thy noble auncetors thy body laid shall be.
Our lineage has not so good luck. the most that shall too thee
Be yielded are thy moothers tears, and in this foreign land
Too hyde thy murdered corce withal a little heap of sand.
For all is lost. Nay yet remaynes (for whom I well can find
In heart too live a little while) an imp unto my mind
Most dear, now only left alone, sumtyme of many mo
The yoongest, little Polydore, delivered late ago
Too Polemnestor king of Thrace whoo dwelles within these bounds
But wherefore do I stay so long in wasshing of her wounds,
And face berayd with gory blood? in saying thus, she went
Too seaward with an aged pace and hory hear beerent.
And (wretched woman) as she called for pitchers for too draw
Up water, she of Polydore on shore the carkesse saw,
And eeke y^ mighty wounds at which the Tyrants sword went thurrow.
The Troiane Ladies shreeked out. But she was dumb for sorrow.
The anguish of her heart forclosde as well her speech as eeke
Her tears deuowring them within. She stood astonyed leeke
As if she had been stone. One while the ground she staard upon.
Another while a ghastly look she kest too heaven. Anon
She looked on the face of him that lay before her killd.
Sumtymes his wounds, (his wounds I say) she specially behilld.
And therwithall she armd her self and furnished her with ire:
Wherethrough as soon as that her heart was fully set on fire,
As though she still had been a Queen, too vengeance she her bent
Enforcing all her wits too find some kind of ponnishme^t.
And as a Lion robbed of her whelps becomm^th wood,
And taking on the footing of her emnye where he stood,
Purs^weth him though out of sight: even so Queen Hecubee
(Now having meynt her tears with wrath) forgetting quyght that see
Was old, but not her princely hard, too Polemnestor went
The cursed murderer, and desyrde his presence too thentent
Too she^ too him a mass of gold (so made she her pretense)
Which for her little Polydore was hid not far from thence.
The Thracian king believing her, as eager of the pray,
Went with her too a secret place. And as they there did stay,
With flattring and deceytfull tongue he thus too her did say.
Make speed I prey thee Hecuba, and give thy son this gold.
I swear by God it shall be his, as well that I do hold
Already, as that thou shall give. Upon him speaking so,
And swearing and forswearing too, she looked sternely tho,
And being sore inflaamd with wrath, caught hold upon him, and
Straight calling out for succor too the wives of Troy at hand
Did in the traitors face bestow her nails, and scratched out
His eyes, her anger gave her heart and made her strong and stout.
She thrust her fingars in as far as could be, and did bore
Not now his eyes (for why his eyes were pulled out before)
But both the places of the eyes berayd with wicked blood.
The Thracians at their Tyrannes harm for anger wexing wood,
Began too scare the Troiane wives with darts and stones. Anon
Queen Hecub ronning at a stone, with gnarring seazd thereon,
And wirryed it beetweene her teeth. And as she opte her chappe
Too speak, in stead of speech she barkt. the place of this missehapp^
Remains still, and of the thing there done bears yet the name.
Long myndfull of her former illes, she sadly for the same
Went howling in the feeldes of Thrace. Her fortune moved not
Her Trojans only, but the Greeks her foes too ruthe: Her lot
Did move even all the Gods to ruthe: and se effectually,
That Hecub too deserve such end even Juno did deny.
Although the morning of the selfsame wars had fauorer been:
She had no leisure too lament the fortune of the Queen,
Nor on the slaughters and the fall of Ilion for too think.
A household care more nearer home did in her stomach sink,
For Memnon her beloved son, whom dying she behild
Upon the feerce Achilles spear amid the Phrygian f^^ld.
She saw it, and her ruddy hew with which she woonted was
Too dye the breaking of the day, did intoo palenesse pass:
And all the sky was hid with clouds. But when his corce was gone
Too burning ward, she could not find in heart too look thereon:
But with her hear about her ears she kneeled down before
The mighty Jove, and thus began speak unto him weeping sore.
Of all that have their dwelling place upon the golden sky
The lowest (for through all the world the feawest shrynes have I)
But yet a Goddess, I do come, not that thou should decree
That Altars, sh^ynes, and holidays be made too honor me.
Yet if thou mark how much that I a woman do for thee,
In keeping night within her bounds, by bringing in thee light,
Thou well may think me worthy sum reward too claim of right.
But neither now is that the thing the morning cares too have,
Ne yet her state is such as now dew honor for too crave.
Bereft of my dear Memnon who in fyghting valeantly
Too help his uncle, (so it was your will O Gods) did dye
Of stout Achilles sturdye spear even in his flowring pryme,
I sew too thee O king of Gods too do him at this time
Sum honor as a comfort of his death, and ease this heart
Of mine which greatly grieved is with wound of percing smart.
No sooner Jove had granted dame Aurora her desire
But that the flame of Memnons corce that burned in the fire
Did fall: and flaky rolls of smoke did dark the day, as when
A foggy mist steames upward from a River or a fen,
And suffers not the Son too shine within it. Black as cole
The cinder rose: and intoo one round lump assembling whole
Grew gross, and took both shape and hew. The fire did life it send,
The lyghtnesse of the substance self did wings unto it lend.
And at the first it flittred like a bird: and by and by
It flew a fethered bird in deed. And with that one began fly
Innumerable mo of selfsame brood: whoo once or twyce
Did sore about the fire, and made a piteous shreeking thryce.
The fowrthtyme in their flying round, th^mselues they all withdrew
In battles twain, and feercely forth of either side one flew
Too fight a combat. With their bills and hooked talents keen
And with their wings courageously they wreakt their wrathful teene.
And myndfull of the valiant man of whom they issued been,
They never ceased iobbing each upon the others breast,
Until they falling both down dead with fyghting ouerprest,
Had offered up their bodies as a worthy sacrifyse
Untoo their cousin Memnon who too Ashes burned lies.
These soodeine birds were named of the founder of their stock:
For men do call them Memnons birds. And every year a flock
Repair too Memnons tumb, where two do in the foresaid wise
In manner of a yeeremynd slay themselves in sacrifyse.
Thus where as others did lament that Dymants daughter bark^,
Auroras own greef busyed her, that smally she it marked
Which thing she too this present time with piteous tears does show:
For through the universal world she sheadeth moysting deawe.
Yet suffered not the destinyes all hope tooperrish quyght
Toogither with the town of Troy. That good and godly knight
The son of Venus bare away by night upon his back
His aged father and his Gods an honorable pack.
Of all the riches of the town that only pray he chose,
So godly was his mind: and like a bannisht man he goes
By water with his own young son Ascanius from the I Will
Antandros, and he shonnes the shore of Thracia which ere while
The wicked Tyrants treason did with Polydores blood defyle.
And having wynd and tide at will, he saufly with his train
Arrived at Apollos town where Anius then did reign.
Whoo being both Apollos priest and of that place the king,
Did enterteyne him in his house and unto church him bring,
And showed him both the City and the temples known of old,
And eeke the sacred trees by which Latona once took hold
When she of chyldbirth traveled As Soon as sacrifyse
Was done with Oxens inwards burnt according too the guyse,
And casting incence in the fire, and sheading wine thereon,
They joyful too the court returned, and there they took anon
Repaste of meat and drink. Then said the good Anchyses this
O Phoebus souereine priest, unless I take my marks amiss,
(As I remember) when I first of all this town did see,
Four daughters and a son of thine thou had here with thee.
King Anius shook his head whereon he ware a myter whyght,
And answered thus. O noble prince, in faith thou gessest right.
Of children five a father then, thou did me behold,
Whoo now (with such inconstancy are mortal matters rolld)
Am in a manner chyldlesse quyght. For what auayles my son
Whoo in the I Will of Anderland a great way hence does won?
Which country takes his name of him, and in the selfsayd place,
In stead of father, like a king he holds the royal mace.
Apollo gave his lot too him: And Bacchus for too show
His love, a greater gift upon his su^ters did bestow,
Than could be wished or credited. For whatsoever they
Did towche, was turned into corn, and wine, and oil streyghtway.
And so their was rich use in them. As Soon as that the fame
Hereof too Agamemnons ears the squorge of Trojans came,
Least you might taste your storms alone and we not feel the same
In part, an host he hither sent, and whither I would or no
Did take them from me, forcing them among the Greeks too go
Too feed the Greekish army with their heavenly gift. But they
Escaped whither they could by flight. A couple took their way
Too I Will Evvboya: toother two too Anderland did fly
Their brothers Realm. An host of men pursewd them by and by,
And threatened war unless they were delivered. Force of fear
Subdewing nature, did constreyne the brother (men must bear
With fearfulness) too render up his susters too their fo.
For neither was Aenaeas there, nor valiant Hector (who
Did make your war last ten years long) the country too defend.
Now when they should like prisoners have been fettred, in the end
They casting up their hands (which yet were free) too heaven, did cry
Too Bacchus for too succor them, who helped them by and by.
At leastwyse if it may be termd a help, in woondrous wise
Too alter folk. For never could I learn ne can surmyse
The manner how they lost their shape* The thing it self is known.
With fethered wings as whyght as snow they quyght away are flowen
Transformed intoo doou^house dooues thy wife dame Venus burdes.
When that the time of meat was spent with these and such like words,
The table was removed straight, and then they went too sleep.
Next morrow rising up as soon as day began too peep,
They went too Phoebus Oracle, which willed them too go
Untoo their moother country and the coasts their s^ocke came fro.
King Anius bare them company. And when away they shoold,
He gave them gifts. Anchises had a scepter all of goold.
Ascanius had a quiver and a Cloak right brave and tr^^.
Aenaeas had a standing Cup presented unto him.
The Thebane Therses whoo had been king Anius guest erewhyle
Did send it out of Thessaly: but Alcon one of Mile
Did make the cup. And he theron^a story portrayed out.
It was a City with seven gates in circuit round about,
Which men might easly all discern. The gates did represent
The Cities name, and showed plain what town thereby was meant.
Without the town were funerals a doing for the dead,
With herces, tapers, fyres, and tumbes. The wives with ruffled head
And stomachs bare pretended greef. The nymphs seemd tears too shead,
And wayle the drying of their wells. The leauelesse trees did sear.
And licking on the parched stones Goats romed here and there.
Behold amid this Thebane town was lively portrayed out
Echions daughters twain, of which the one with courage stout
Did profer both her naked throat and stomach too the knyfe:
And toother with a manly heart did also spend her life,
For saufgard of her countryfolk: And how that thervppon
They both were carried solemly on herces, and anon
Were burned in the cheifest place of all the Thebane town.
Then (least their lineage should decay whoo did with such renown,)
Out of the Ashes of the maids there issued two young men,
And they unto their moothers dust did obsequies again.
Thus much was graued curiously in ancient precious brass,
And on the brim a trayle of flowers of bearbrich gilded was.
The Trojans also gave too him as costly gifts again.
Because he was Apollos priest they gave too him as then
A Chist too keep in frankincence. They gave him furthermore
A Crown of gold wherein were set of precious stones great store.
Then calling too remembrance that the Trojans issued were
Of Tevvcers blood, they sayld too Crete. But long they could not there
Abide the infection of the air: and so they did forsake
The hundred Cities, and with speed to Itayle ward did make.
The winter wexed hard and rough, and tossed them verry sore.
And when their ships arrived were upon the perlous shore
Among the Strophad Isles, the bird Aello did them fear.
The costs of Dulich, Ithaca, and Same they passed were,
And eeke the Court of Neritus where wise Ulysses reignd,
And came too Ambrace for the which the Gods strong strife mayntein^.
There saw they turned into stone the judge whoose image yet
At Actium in Appollos Church in sign thereof does sit.
They vewed also Dodon grove where Oaks spoke: and the coast
Of Chaon where the sons of king Molossus s^apt a most
Ungracious fire by taking wings. From thence they coasted by
The country of the Pheaks fraught with fruit abundantly.
Then took they land in Epyre, and too Buthrotos they went
Whereas the Troiane prophet dwelt, whoose reign did represent
an image of their ancient Troy. There being certifyde
Of things too come by Helen (whoo while there they did abide
Informed them right faithfully of all that should betyde)
They passed into Sicilie. With corners three this land
Shootes out intoo the Sea: of which Pachinnus front does stand.
Against the southcoast: Lilibye does face the gentle west,
And Pelore unto Charlsis wayne does northward bear his breast.
The Troianes under Pelore gate with ores and prosperous tides
And in the even by Zanclye shore their fleet at anchor rydes.
Upon the leftsyde restlessely Charybdis ay does beat them.
And swalloweth ships and spewes them up as fast as it does eat them.
And Scylla beats on their right: which from the navel down
Is patched up with cruel curres: and upward too the crown
Does keep the countnance of a maid, And (if that all be true
That Poets feign) she was sumtyme a maid right fair of hew.
Too her made many wooers suit: all which she did eschew.
And going too the salt Sea nymphs (too whom she was right dear)
She vaunted, too how many men she gave the slip that year.
Too whom the Lady Galate in kembing of her hear
Said thus with syghes. But they that sought too thee (O Lady) were
None other than of humane kind, too whom without all fear
Of harm, thou myghtest (as thou do) give nay. But as for me
Although that I of Nereus and gray Doris daughter be,
And of my susters have with me continually a guard,
I could not escape the Cyclops love, but too my greef full hard.
(With that her tears did stop her speech.) As Soon as that the maid
Had dryde them with her marble thomb, and moande the nymph, she said.
Dear Goddess tell me all your greef, and hyde it not from me:
For trust me I will unto you both true and secret be.
Then unto Cratyes daughter thus the nymph her playnt did frame.
Of Fawn and nymph Simethis borne was Acis, whoo became
A joy too both his parents, but too me the greater joy.
For being but a sixteen years of age, this fair sweet boy
Did take me too his love, what time about his chyldish chin
The tender hear like mossy down too sprowt did first begin.
I loved him beyond all Gods forbod, and likewise me
The Giant Cyclops. neither (if demanded it should be)
I well were able for too tell you whither that the love
Of Acis, or the Cyclops hate did more my stomach move.
There was no odds between them. Oh dear Goddess Venus, what
A power haste thou? Behold how even this owgly Giant that
No spark of meekness in him has, whoo is a terror too
The verrye woods, whom never guest nor stranger came unto
Without displeasure, whoo the heavens and all the Gods despyseth,
Does feel what thing is love. The love of me him so surpryseth,
That Polypheme regarding not his sheep and hollow Cave,
And having care too please does go about too make him brave.
His stir stiff hear he kembeth now with strong and sturdy rakes,
And with a sithe does marcussotte his bristled berd: and takes
Delyght too look upon himself in waters, and too frame
His countnance. Of his murtherous heart the wyldnesse wexeth tame*
His vnastaunched thyrst of blood is quenched: ships may pass
And repasse saufly. In the while that he in love thus was,
One Telemus Evvrymeds son a man of passing skill
In birdflyght, taking land that time in Sicill, went until
The orped Giant Polypheme, and said: This one round eye
That now amid thy forehead stands shall one day ere thou dye
By sly Ulysses blinded be. The Giant laughed thereat,
And said O foolish soothsayre thou deceived art in that.
For why another (even a wench) already has it blinded.
Thus skorning him that told him truth because he was high minded,
He either made the ground too shake in walking on the shore,
Or rowzd him in his shadye Cave. With wedged point before
There shoots a hill intoo the Sea: whereof the sea does beat
On either side. The one eyd feend came up and made his seat
Thereon, and after came his sheep vndriuen. As Soon as he
Had at his foot laid down his staff which was a whole Pyne tree
Well able for too be a maast too any ship, he takes
His pype compact of fyuescore reeds, and therwithall he makes
So loud a noise that all the hills and waters therabout
Might easly ^eere the shirlnesse of the shepeherds whistling out.
I lying underneath the rock, and leaning in the lap
Of Acis marked these words of his which far I heard by hap.
More whyght thou art then Primrose leaf my Lady Galatee.
More fresh than meade, more tall and straight than lofty Aldertree*
More bright than glass, more wanton than the tender kid forsooth.
Than Cockleshelles continually with water worn, more smoothe.
More cheerful than the winters Sun, or Summers shadow cold,
More seemly and more comely than the Planetree too behold.
Of value more than Apples be although they were of gold.
More clear than frozen yea, more sweet than Grape through ripe ywis,
More soft than butter newly made, or down of Cygnet is.
And much more fair and beawtyfull than gardein too mine eye,
But that thou from my company continually do fly.
And thou the selfsame Galate art more tettish for too frame
Than Oxen of the wilderness whom never wight did tame.
More fleeting than the waves, more hard than warryed Oak too twyne,
More tough tha^ willow twiggs, more lyth tha^ is the wyld whyght vine.
More than this rock immovable, more violent than a stream.
More proud than Peacocke praysd, more feerce tha^ fire and more extreme.
More rough than Breers, more cruel than the new delivered Bear,
More merciless than trodden snake, than sea more deaf of ear.
And which (and if it lay in me I chiefly would restrain)
Not only swifter paced than the stag in chase on plain,
But also swifter than the wynd and flyghtfull air. But if
Thou knew me well, it would thee irke too fly and be a greef
Too tarrye from me. Yea thou would endeavor all thy power
Too keep me wholly too thy self. The Quarry is my bower
Heawen out of whole main stone. No Sun in summer there can swelt.
No nipping cold in wintertyme within the same is felt.
Gay Apples weying down the boughs have I, and* Grapes like gold,
And purple Grapes on spreaded Uynes as many as can hold.
Both which I do reserve for thee. Thyself shall with thy hand
The soft sweet strawbryes gather, which in woody shadow stand.
The Cornell berryes also from the tree thy self shall pull:
And pleasant plommes, sum yellow like new wax, sum blew, sum full
Of ruddy ^ewce. Of Chestnutts eeke (if my wife thou will be)
Thou shall have store: and frute^ all sorts: All trees shall serve for thee.
This Cattle here is all mine own. And many mo beside
Do either in the bottoms feed, or in the woods them hyde,
And many standing at their stalles do in my Cave abide.
The number of them (if a man should ask) I cannot show.
Tush beggars of their Cattle use the number for too know.
And for the goodness of the same, no whit believe thou me.
But come thyself (and if thou will) the truth there of too see.
See how their vdders full do make them straddle. Lesser ware
Shet up at home in cloce warm peends, are Lambs. There also are
In other pinfolds Kidds of selfsame yeaningtyme. Thus have
I always milk as white as snow. whereof I sum do save
Too drink, and of the rest is made good cheese. And furthermore
Not only stale and common gifts and pleasures whereof store
Is too be had at each man's hand, (as Leuerets, Kidds, and Dots,
A pair of pigeons, or a nest of birds new found, or Roes,)
Shall unto thee presented be. I found this toother day
A pair of Bearewhelpes, each so like the other as they lay
Upon a hill, that scarce ye each discern from other may.
And when that I did find them I did take them up, and say
These will I for my Lady keep for her therewith too play.
Now put thou up thy fair bright head good Galat I thee pray
Above the greenish waves: now come my Galat, come away.
And of my present take no scorn. I know my self too be
A iollye fellow. For even now I did behold and see
Mine image in the water sheere, and sure me thought I took
Delyght too see my goodly shape, and favor in the brook.
Behold how big I am not ^oue in heaven (for so you men
Report one ^oue too reign, of whom I pass not for too ken)
Is howger than this doughty corce of mine. A bush of hear
Does ouerdreepe my visage grim, and shadows as it were
A grove upon my shoulders twain. And think it not too be
A shame for that with bristled hear my body rough ye see.
A foul ^lfauored sight it is too see a leauelesse tree
A lothely thing it is, a horse without a mane too keep.
As feathers do become the birds, and wool becomes sheep,
Even so a beard and bristled skin becomes also men.
I have but one eye, which does stand amid my frunt. what then?
This one round eye of mine is like a mighty target. Why?
Uewes not the Sun all things from heaven? Yet but one only eye
Has he. moreover in your Seas my father bears the sway.
Him will I make thy fathrinlaw. Have mercy I the pray,
And harken too mine humble suit. For only unto thee
Yield I. Even I of whom both heaven and Jove despised be
And eeke the percing thunderbolt, do stand in awe and fear
Of thee O Nerye. Thine ill will is greeuouser too bear
Than is the deadly Thunderclappe. Yet could I better find
In heart too suffer this contempt of thine with pacient mind
If thou did shonne all other folk as well as me. But why
Rejecting Cyclops do thou love dwarf Acis? why say I
Preferst thou Acis unto me? well let him liked be
Both of himself, and also (which I would be loathe) of thee.
And if I catch him he shall feel that in my body is
The force that should be. I shall paunch him quick. Those limbs of his
I will in pieces tear, and strew them in the feeldes, and in
Thy waters, if he do thee haunt. For I do swelt within.
And being chaafte the flame does burn more feerce too my unrest.
Me thinks mount Aetna with his force is closed in my breast.
And yet it nothing moves thee. As Soon as he had talked
Thus much in vain, (I saw well all) he rose: and fuming stalkt
Among his woods and woonted Lawndes, as does a Bulchin, when
The Cow is from him taken. He could him no where rest as then.
Anon the feend espied me and Acis where we lay,
Before we wist or feared it: and crying out began say
I see ye. and confounded might I be with endless shame,
But if I make this day the last agreement of your game.
These words were spoke with such a reere as verry well became
An angry Giant. Aet^a shook with lowdnesse of the same.
I scaard therewith dopt underneath the water, and the knight
Simethus turning straight his back, did give himself too flight,
And cried help me Galate, help parents I you pray,
And in your kingdom me receive whoo perrish must streyghtway.
The roundeyd devil made pursewt: and rending up a fleece
Of Aetna Rock, threw after him: of which a little piece
Did Acis overtake. and yet as little as is was,
It overwhelmed Acis whole. I wretched wight (alas)
Did that which destnyes would permit. Forthwith I brought too pass
That Acis should receive the force his father had before.
His scarlet blood did issue from the lump, and more and more
Within a while the redness began too vannish: and the hew
Resembled at the first a brook with rain distroubled new,
Which wexeth clear by length of time. Anon the lump did clyue,
And from the hollow cliffe thereof high reeds sprang up alive.
And at the hollow issue of the stone the bubling water
Came trickling out. And by and by (which is a woondrous matter)
The stripling with a wreath of reed about his horned head
Auaunst his body too the waste. Whoo (save he was that stead
Much biggar than he erst had been, and altoogither gray)
Was Acis still. and being turned too water, at this day
In shape of river still he bears his former name away.
The Lady Galat ceast her talk and straight the company brake.
And Neryes daughters parting thence, swam in the gentle lake.
Dame Scylla home ageine returned. (She durst not her betake
Too open sea) and either roamd upon the sandy shore
Stark naakt, or when for weariness she could not walk no more,
She then withdrew her out of sight and gate her too a poole,
And in the water of the same, her heated limbs did cool.
Behold the fortune. Glaucus (whoo then being late before
Transformed in Evvboya I Will upon Anthedon shore,
Was new becomne a dweller in the sea) as he did swim
Along the coast was taken in love at sight of Scylla trim,
And spoke such words as he did think might make her tarry still.
Yet fled she still, and swift for fear she gate her too a hill
That butted on the Sea. right steep and upward sharp did shoot
A loftye top with trees, beneathe was hollow at the foot.
Here Scylla stayed and being sauf by strongnesse of the place,
(Not knowing if he monster were, or God, that did her chase,)
She looked back. And woondring at his color and his hear
With which his shoulders and his back all wholly covered were,
She saw his nether parts were like a fish with tail wrythde round
Who leaning too the nearest Rock, said thus with loud creere sound.
Fair maid, I neither monster am nor cruel savage beast:
But of the sea a God, whoose power and favor is not least.
For neither Protevv in the sea nor Triton have more might
Nor yet the son of Athamas that now Palaemon hight.
Yet once I was a mortal man. But you must know that I
Was given too seawoorkes, and in them me only did apply.
For sumtyme I did draw the drag in which the fishes were,
And sumtyme sitting on the clisses I angled here and there.
There butteth on a fair green mede a bank whereof tone half
Is cloasd with sea, the rest is clad with herbs which never calf,
Nor horned Ox, nor seely sheep, nor shakheard Goat did feed.
The busye Be did never there of flowers sweet smelling speed.
No gladsum garlonds ever there were gathered for the head.
No hand those flowers ever yet with hooked sithe did shred.
I was the first that ever set my foot upon that plot.
Now as I dryde my dropping netts, and laid abroad my lotte,
Too tell how many fishes had bychaunce too net been sent,
Or through their own too light beeleefe on bayted hook been hent:
(The matter seems like a lie, but what auayles too lie?)
As Soon as that my pray had towcht the grass, it by and by
Began too move, and flask their finnes, and swim upon the dry,
As in the Sea. And as I pawsd and woondred at the sight,
My ^raught of fishes euerychone too seaward took their flight,
And leaping from the shore, forsook their newfound master quyght.
I was amazed at the thing: and standing long in dowt,
I sought the cause if any God had brought this same about,
Or else sum iewce of herb. And as I so did musing stand,
What herb (quod I) has such a power? and gathering with my hand
The grass, I bote it with my toothe. My throat had scarcely yet,
Well swallowed down the uncouth iewce, when like an agew fit
I felt mine inwards soodeinly too shake, and with the same,
A love of other nature in my breast with violence came.
And long I could it not resist: but said dear land adeew*
For never shall I haunt thee more. And with that word I thre^*
My body in the sea. The Gods thereof receiving me,
Uouchsaued in their order me installed for too be.
Desyring old Oceanus and Thetis for their sake,
The rest of my mortality away from me too take.
They hallowed me, and having said nine times the holy rym^
That purges all prophanednesse, they charged me that time
Too put my brestbulk underneath a hundred streams. Anon
The brooks from sundry coasts and all the Seas did ride upon
My head. From whence as soon as I returned, by and by
I felt my self far otherwise through all my limbs, than I
Had been before. and in my mind I was another man.
Thus far of all that me befell make just report I can.
Thus far I bear in mind. The rest my mind perceived not.
Then first of all this hory green gray grisild beard I got,
And this same bush of hear which all along the seas I sweep,
And these same mighty shoulders, and these grayish arms, and feet
Coonfounded intoo finned fish. But what avails me
This goodly shape, and of the Gods of sea too loved be?
Or for too be a God my self? if they delyght not thee?
As he was speaking this, and still about too utter more,
Dame Scylla him forsook: whereat he wexing angry sore.
And being quickened with repulse, in rage he took his way
Too Circes Titans daughters Court which full of monsters lay.
Finis Libri decimi tertij.
THE.XIIII. BOOK OF Ouids Metamorphosis.
NOw had the Evvboyan fisherman (whoo lately was becomme
A God of sea too dwell in sea for ay,) already swomme
Past Aetna which upon the face of Giant Typho lies,
Toogither with the pasture of the Cyclops which defyes
Both Plough and harrowe, and by teemes of Oxen sets no store:
An Zancle, and cracked Rhegion which stands a toother shore:
And eeke the rough and shipwrecke sea which being hemmed in
With two main lands on either side, is as a bound betwin
The fruitful Realms of Italy and Sicill. From that place
He cutting through the Tyrrhene sea with both his arms a pace,
Arrived at the grassye hills and at the Palace hye
Of Circe Phoebus imp which full of sundry beasts did lie.
When Glaucus in her presence came, and had her greeted, and
Received freendly welcomming and greeting at her hand,
He said. O Goddess pity me a God I thee desire.
Thou only (if at least thou think me worthy so great hyre)
Can ease this love of mine. No wight does better know than I
The power of herbs, whoo late ago transformed was thereby.
And now too open unto thee of this my greef the ground,
Upon the Italyan shore against Messene walls I found
Fair Scylla. Shame it is too tell how scornful she did take
The gentle words and promises and suit that I did make.
But if that any power at all consist in charms, then let
That sacret mouth of thine cast charms: or if more force be set
In herbs too compass things withal, then use the herbs that have
Most strength in woorking. Neither think, I hither come too crave
A medcine for too heal myself and cure my wounded heart:
I force no end. I would have her be partener of my smart.
But Circe (for no natures are more lightly set on fire
Than such as she is) (whither that the cause of this desire
Were only in herself, or that Dame Venus bearing ay
In mind her fathers deed in once disclosing of her play,
Did stir her here unto) said thus. It were a better way
For thee too fancye such a one whoose will and whole desire
Is bent too thine, and whoo is sindgd with selfsame kind of fire.
Thou woorthye art of suit too thee. and (credit me) thou should
Be wood in deed, if any hope of speeding give thou would.
And therefore dowt not. Only of thy beawtye lyking have.
Lo, I whoo am a Goddess and the imp of Phoebus brave,
Whoo can so much by charms, whoo can so much by herbs, do vow
My self too thee. If I disdeine, disdeine me also thou.
And if I yield, yield thou likewise: and in one only deed
Avenge thy self of twain. Too her entreating thus too speed,
First trees shall grow (quod Glaucus) in the sea, and reeke shall thryue
On tops of hills, ere I (as long as Scylla is alive)
Do change my love. The Goddess wext right wroth: and since she could
Not hurt his person being fallen in love with him, ne would:
She spyghted her that was preferred before her. And upon
Displeasure taken of this repulse, she went her way anon.
And wicked weeds of grisly iewce toogither she did bray,
And in the braying, witching charms she over them did say.
And putting on a russet cloak, she passed through the rowt
Of savage beasts that in her court came fawning round about,
And going unto Rhegion clifte which stands against the shore
Of Zancle, entered by and by the waters that do rore
With violent tides, upon the which she stood as on firm land,
And ran and never wet her feet awhit. There was at hand
A little plash that bowwed like a bow that stands bent,
Where Scylla woonted was too rest herself, and thither went
From rage of sea and air, what time the son amid the sky
Is whotest making shadows short by mounting up on hye.
This plash did Circe then infect against that Scylla came,
And with her poisons which had power most monstrous shapes too frame
Defiled it. She sprincled there the iewce of venymd weeds.
And thryce nine times with witching mouth she softly mumbling, reeds
A charm right dark of uncouth words. No sooner Scylla came
Within this plash, and too the waast had waded in the same,
But that she saw her hinderloynes with barking buggs atteint.
And at the first, not thinking with her body they were meynt
As parts thereof, she started back, and rated them. And sore
She was afraid the eager curres should byght her. But the more
She shonned them, the surer still she was too have them there.
In seeking where her loins, and thyghes, and feet and ancles were,
Chappes like the chappes of Cerberus in stead of them she found.
Naught else was there than cruel curres from belly down too ground.
So underneath misshapen loins and womb remaining sound,
Her mannish mastyes backs were ay within the water drowned.
Her lover Glaucus wept thereat, and Circes bed refusde
That had so passing cruelly her herbs on Scylla used.
But Scylla in that place abode. And for the hate she bore
Too Circe ward, (as soon as meet occasion serude therefore)
She spoyld Ulysses of his mates. And shortly after, she
Had also drowned the Troiane fleet, but that (as yet we see)
She was transformd too rock of stone, which shipm^n warely shonne.
When from this Rock the Troiane fleet by force of Ores had won,
And from Charybdis greedye gulf, and were in manner ready
Too have arryude in Italy, the wynd did rise so heady,
As that it drove them back upon the coast of Affricke. There
The Tyrian Queen (whoo afterward impatiently should bear
The going of this Troiane prince away) did enterteine
Aenaeas in her house, and was right glad of him and feign.
Upon a Pyle made underneath pretense of sacrifyse
She goard herself upon a sword, and in most woeful wise
As she herself had been beguyld: so she beguyled all.
Eftsoone Aenaeas flying from the newly reered wall
Of Carthage in that sandy land, retired back again
Too Sicill, where his faithful friend Acestes reignd. And when
He there had done his sacrifyse, and kept an Obit at
His fathers ^umb, he out of hand did mend his Gallyes that
Dame Iris Iunos messenger had burned up almost.
And sailing thence he kept his course aloof along the coast
Of Aeolye and of Vulcanes Isles the which of brimston smoke.
And passing by the Meremayds rocks, (His Pilot by a s^roke
Of tempest being drowned in sea) he sayld by Prochite, and
Inarime, and (which upon a barreine hill does stand)
The land of Ape I Will, which does take that name of people slye
There dwelling. For the Sire of Gods abhorring utterly
The leawdnesse of the Cercops, and their wilful periurye,
And eeke their guylefull dealing did trans^orme them euery^ho^^
Intoo an euillfauored kind of beast: that being none
They might yet still resemble men. He knit in lesser space
Their members, and he beat me flat their noses too their face,
The which he filled furrowlike with wrinckles every where.
He clad their bodies over all with fallow coulourd hear,
And put them intoo this same I Will too dwell for ever there.
But first he did bereeue them of the use of speech and tongue,
Which they too cursed periurye did use both old and young.
Too chatter hoarcely, and too shreeke, too iabber, and too squeake,
He has them left, and for too moppe and mow, but not too speak.
Aenaeas having past this I Will, and on his right hand left
The town of Naples, and the tumb of Mysen on his left,
Toogither with the fenny grounds: at Cumye landed, and
Went unto longlyvde Cybills house, with whom he went in hand
That he too see his fathers ghost might go by Auerne deep.
She long upon the earth in s^ownd her eyes did fixed keep.
And at the length as soon as that the spryght of prophesye
Was entered her, she raising them did thus ageine reply.
O most renowned wight, of whom the godliness by fire
And valeantnesse is tryde by sword, great things thou do require.
But fear not Troiane. for thou shall be lord of thy desire.
Too see the reverend image of thy dear beeloued sire,
Among the fair Elysian feeldes where godly folk abide,
And all the lowest kingdoomes of the world I will thee guide.
No way too virtue is restreynd. This spoken, she did show
A golden bowgh that in the wood of Proserpine did grow,
And willed him too pull it from the tree. He did obey:
And saw the power of dreadful hell, and where his graundsyres lay
And eeke the aged Ghost of stowt Anchises. Furthermore
He lernd the customs of the land arryvd at late before,
And what adventures should by war betyde him in that place.
From thence retyring up ageine a slow and weery pace,
He did assuage the tediousness by talking with his guide.
For as he in the twylyght dim this dreadful way did ride,
He said: whither present thou thyself a Goddess be.
Or such a one as God does love most deerly, I will thee
For ever as a Goddess take, and will acknowledge me
Thy servant, for saufguyding me the place of death too see,
And for thou from the place of death haste brought me sauf and free.
For which desert, what time I shall atteyne too open air,
I will a temple to thee buyld right sumptuous, large, and fair,
And honor thee with frankincence. The prophetisse did cast
Her eye upon Aenaeas back, and syghing said at last.
I am no Goddess. Neither think thou can with conscience right,
With holy incence honor give too any mortal wight.
But too thentent through ignorance thou err not, I had been
Eternal and of worldly life I should none end have seen,
If that I would my maydenhod on Phoebus have bestowde.
Howbeeit while he stood in hope too have the same, and trowde
Too overcome me with his gifts, thou maid of Cumes (quod he)
Choose what thou will, and of thy wish the owner thou shall be.
I taking full my hand of dust, and showing it him there,
Desired like a fool too live as many years as were
Small graynes of cinder in that heap. I quight forgot too crave
Immediately, the race of all those years in youth too have.
Yet did he grant me also that, upon condition I
Would let him have my maydenhod, which thing I did deny.
And so rejecting Phoebus gift a single life I led.
But now the blessefull time of youth is altoogither fled,
And irksome age with trembling pace is stolen upon my head.
Which long I must endure. For now already as you see
Seven hundred years are come and gone and that the number be
Full matched of the granes of dust, threehundred haruestes mo
I must three hundred vintages see more before I go.
The day will come that length of time shall make my body small,
And little of my withered limbs shall leave or naught at all.
And none shall think that ^uer God was taken in love with me.
Even out of Phoebus knowledge then perchance I grown shall be.
Or at the least that ever he me lov^e he shall deny.
So sore I shall be altered. And then shall no man's eye
Discern me. Only by my voice I shall be known. For why
The fates shall leave me still my voice for folk too know me by.
As Sybill in the vaulted way such talk as this did frame,
The Troiane knight. Aenaeas up at Cumes fro Limbo came,
And having done the sacrifyse accustomd for the same,
He took his journey too the coast which had not yet the name
Received of his nurce. In this same place he found a mate
Of wise Ulysses Macare of Neritus whoo late
Before, had after all his long and tediouse toils, there stayed.
He spying Achemenides (whom late ago afraid
They had among mount Aetnas Cliffs abandond when they fled
From Polypheme:) and woondring for too see he was not dead,
Said thus. O Achemenides* what chance, or rather what
Good God has savde the life of thee? What is the reason that
A barbrous ship bears thee a Greek? or whither saylest thou?
Too him thus, Achemenides his own man freely now
And not forgrowen as one forlorn, nor clad in bristled hyde,
Made answer. Yet ageine I would I should in perrill bide
Of Polypheme, and that I might those chappes of his behold
Beesmeared with the blood of men, but if that I do hold
This ship more dear than all the Realm of wise Ulysses, or
If lesser of Aenaeas I do make account than for
My father. neither (though I did as much as done might be,)
I could enough be thankful for his goodness toward me.
That I still speak and breathe: That I the Sun and heaven do see:
Is his gift. Can I thanklesse then or myndlesse of him be?
That down the round eyed giants throat this soul of mine went not?
And that from henceforth when too dye it ever be my lot
I may be laid in grave, or sure not in the Giants mawe?
What heart had I that time (at least if fear did not withdraw
Both heart and sense) when left behynd, you taking ship I saw?
I would have called after you but that I was afraid
By making outcrye too my to myself too have beewrayd.
For even the noise that you did make did put Ulysses ship
In danger. I did see him from a cragged mountain strippe
A mighty rock, and intoo sea it throw midway and more.
Ageine I saw his giants pawe throw howge big stones great store
As if it were a sling. And sore I feared least your ship
Should drowned by the water be that from the stones did skippe,
Or by the stones themselves, as if my self had been therein.
But when that flight had saved you from death, he did begin
On Aetna syghing up and down too walk: and with his paws
Went groping of the trees among the woods. And forbycause
He could not see, he knocked his shinnes against the rocks each where.
And stretching out his grisly arms (which all beegrymed were
With baken blood) too seaward, he the Greekish nation band,
And said. O if that sum good chance might bring unto my hand
Ulysses or sum mate of his, on whom too wreake mine ire.
Upon whose bowels with my teeth I like a Hawke might tire:
Whose living members might with these my talants teared been.
Whoose blood might bubble down my throat: whose flesh might pant between
My jaws: how light or none at all this losing of mine eye
Would seem? These words and many mo the cruel feend did cry.
A shuddring horror perced me too see his smudged face,
And cruel hands, and in his frunt the foul round eyelesse place,
And monstrous members, and his beard bestowbered with the blood
Of man. Before mine eyes then death the smallest sorrow stood.
I looked every minute too be seized in his pawe.
I looked ever when he should have cramd me in his mawe.
And in my mind I of that time me thought the image saw
When having dingd a doozen of our fellows too the grownd
And lying like a Lion feerce or hunger sterued hownd
Upon them, very eagerly he down his greedy gut
Their bowwels and their limbs yet more than half alive did put,
And with their flesh toogither crasht the bones and maree whyght.
I trembling like an aspen leaf stood sad and bloodlesse quyght.
And in beholding how he fed and belked up again
His bloody vittells at his mouth, and vttred out amayne
The clottred gobbets mixed with wine, I thus surmysde: like lot
Hangs over my head now, and I must also go too pot.
And hyding me for many days, and quaking horribly
At every noise, and dreading death, and wisshing for too dye,
Appeasing hunger with the leaves of trees, and herbs and mast,
Alone, and poor, and footel^sse, and too death and penance cast,
A long time after I espyde this sh^ppe a far at last,
And ronning downward too the sea by fignes did succor seek.
Where fynding grace, this Troianeshippe received me a Greek.
But now I prey thee gentle friend declare thou unto me
Thy Capteines and thy fellows luck that took the sea with thee.
He told him how that Aeolus the son of Hippot, hea
That keeps the wyndes in prison cloce did reign in Tuskane sea,
And how Ulysses having at his hand a noble gift
The wynd enclosde in leather bags, did sail with prosperous drift
nine days toogither: insomuch they came within the sight
Of home: but on the tenth day when the morning began give light,
His fellows being somewhat touched with couetousenesse and spyght.
Supposing that it had been gold, did let the wyndes out quyght.
The which returning whence they came, did drive them back a main
That in the Realm of Aeolus they went a land again.
From thence (quod he) we came unto the ancient Lamyes town
Of which the feerce Antiphates that season ware the crown.
A cowple of my mates and I were sent unto him: and
A mate of mine and I could scarce by flight escape his hand.
The third of us did with his blood embrew the wicked face
Of leawd Antiphate, whoo with sword us flying thence did chase,
And following after with a rowt threw stones and loggs which drowned
Both men and ships. Howbeeit one by chance escaped sound,
Which bare Ulysses and my self. So having lost most part
Of all our dear companions, we with sad and sorry heart
And much complaining, did arryue at yoonder coast which you
May ken far hence. A great way hence (I say) we see it now
But trust me truly over near I saw it once. And thou
Aenaeas Goddess Venus son the iustest knight of all
The Troiane race (for since the war is done, I can not call
thee to) I warn thee get thee far from C^rces dwelling place.
For when our ships arrived there, remembering eft the case
Of cruel king Antiphates, and of that hellish wight
The round eyed giant Polypheme, we had so small delyght
Too visit vncowth places, that we said we would not go.
Then cast we lotts. The lot fell out upon myself as tho,
And Polyte, and Eurylocus, and on Elpenor who
Delyghted tootoomuch in wine, and eyghteene other mo.
All we did go too Circes house. As Soon as we came thither,
And in the portall of the Hall had set our feet toogither,
A thousand Lions woolues and bears did put us in a fear
By meeting us. But none of them was too be feared there.
For none of them could do us harm: but with a gentle look
And following us with fawning feet their wanton tails they shook.
Anon did Damzells welcome us and led us through the hall
(The which was made of marble stone, floor, arches, roof, and wall)
Too Circe. She sat underneath a trauerse in a chair
Aloft right rich and stately, in a chamber large and fair.
She ware a goodly longtreynd gown: and all her rest attire
Was every whit of goldsmithes woork. There sat me also by her
The Seanymphes and her Ladies whoose fine fingers never knew
What ^oozing wool did meene, nor threede from whorled spindle drew,
They sorted herbs, and picking out the flowers that were mixed,
Did put them intoo mawnds, and with indifferent space betwixt,
Did lay the leaves and stalks on heaps according too their hew.
And she herself the woork of them did ouersee and view.
The virtue and the use of them right perfectly she knew,
And in what leaf it lay, and which in mixture would agree.
And so perusing every her^ by g^od aduysement, she
Did wey them out. As Soon as she us entering in did see,
And greeting had both given and taken, she looked cheerfully,
And granting all that we desyrde, commanded by and by
A certain potion too be made of barley parched dry
And wine and honey mixed with cheese. and with the same she slye
Had meynt the iewce of certain herbs which vnespyde did lie
By reason of the sweetness of the drink. We took the cup
Delivered by her wicked hand, and quaft it clearly up
With thirstye throtes. Which done, and that the cursed witch had smit
Our highest hear tippes with her wand, (it is a shame, but yet
I will declare the truth) I wext all rough with bris^led hear,
And could not make complaint with words. In stead of speech I there
Did make a rawghtish grunting, and with groueling face began bear
My visage downward too the ground. I felt a hooked groyne
Too wexen hard upon my mouth, and brawned neck too join
My head and shoulders. And the hands with which I late ago
Had taken up the charmed cup, were turned t^o feet as tho.
Such force there is in Sorcerie. In fine with other mo
That tasted of the selfsame sauce, they shet me in a Stye.
From this missehappe Furilochus alonly scapte. For why
He only would not taste the cup. which had he not fled fro,
He should have been a bristled beast as well as we. And so
Should none have borne Ulysses word of our mischaunce, nor he
Have come too Circe too revenge our harms and set us free.
The peaceprocurer Mercury had given too him a whyght
Fair flower whoose root is black, and of the Gods it Moly hight
Assurde by this and heavenly hestes, he entered Circes bower.
And being bidden for too drink the cup of balefull power,
As Circe was about too stroke her wand upon his hear,
He thrust her back, and put her with his naked sword in fear.
Then fell they too agreement straight, and faith in hand was plyght.
And being made her bedfellowe, he claymed as in right
Of dowrye, for too have his men ageine in perfect plyght.
She sprincled us with better iewce of vncowth herbs, and struck
The awk end of her charmed rod upon our heads, and spoke
Words too the former contrary. The more she charmd, the more
Arose we upward from the ground on which we daarde before.
Our bristles fell away, the ^lift our cloven elees forsook.
Our shoulders did return agein: and next our elbowes took
Our arms and hands their former place. Then weeping we enbrace
Our Lord, and hing about his neck whoo also wept apace.
And not a word we rather spoke than such as might appear
From hearts most thankful too proceed. We tarried their a year
I in that while saw many things, and many things did here.
I marked also this one thing with store of other geere
Which one of Circes four cheef maids (whoose office was always
Upon such hallowes too attend) did secretly bewray
Too me. For in the while my Lord with Circe kept alone,
This maid a yoongmannes image sheawd of fair whyght marble stone
Within a Chauncell. On the head thereof were garlonds store
And eeke a woodspecke. And as I demanded her wherefore
And whoo it was they honored so in holy Church, and why
He bare that bird upon his head: She answeering by and by
Said: learn hereby sir Macare too understand the power
My Lady has, and mark thou well what I shall say this hour.
There reignd erewhyle in Italy one Picus Saturnes son
Whoo loved warlike horse and had delyght too see them run.
He was of feature as ye see. And by this image here
The verry beawtye of the man does lyuelely appear.
His courage matched his personage. And scarcely had he well
Seen twentye years. His countnance did allure the nymphs y^ dwell
Among the Latian hills. The nymphs of fountains and of brooks,
As those that haunted Now called Tyber.
Albula were ravished with his looks
And so were they that Numicke bears, and Anio too, and Alme
That ronneth short, and heady Nar, and Farfar cool and calm.
And all the nymphs that used too haunt Dianas shadye poole,
Or any lakes or meeres near hand, or other waters cool.
But he disdeyning all the rest did set his love upon
A lady whom Venilia bare (so fame reports) on
The stately mountain Palatine by Ianus that does bear
The dowble face. As Soon as that her years for marriage were
Thought able, she pre^erring him before all other men,
Was wedded too this Picus whoo was king of Lavvrents then.
She was in beawtye excellent. but yet in singing, much
More excellent: and thervppon they naamd her Singer. Such
The sweetness of her music was, that she therewith delyghts
The savage beasts, and caused birds too cease their wandering flyghts,
And moved stones and trees, and made the ronning streams too stay.
Now while that she in woman's tune records her pleasant lay
At home, her husband rode abroad upon a lustye horse
Too hunt the Boar, and bare in hand two hunting staves of force.
His cloak was crymzen butned with a golden button fast.
Intoo the selfsame forest eeke was Phoebus daughter past
From those same feeldes that of herself the name of Circe bear,
Too gather vncowth herbs among the fruitful hillocks there.
As Soon as lurking in the shrubbes she did the king espy,
She was a^trawght. Down fell her herbs too ground. And by and by
Through all her bones the flame of love the maree began too frye.
And when she from this forced heat had called her wits again,
She purposde too bewray her mind. But unto him as then
She could not come for swiftness of his horse and for his men
That guarded him o^ every side. Yet shall thou not (quod she)
So shift thee fro my hands although the wynd should carrye thee,
If I do know myself, if all the strength of herbs fail not,
Or if I have not quyght and cleene my charms and spelles forgotte.
In saying these same words, she made the likeness of a Boar
Without a body, causing it too swiftly pass before
King Picus eyes, and for too seem too get him too the wood,
Where for the thickenesse of the trees a horse might do no good.
Immediately the king unawares a who^e pursuit did make
Upon the shadow of his pray, and quikly did forsake
His foming horses sweating back: and following vain wan hope,
Did run a foot among the woods, and through the bushes crope.
Then Circe fell a mumbling spelles, and praying like a witch
Did honor strange and vncowth Gods with vncowth charms, by which
She used too make the moon look dark, and wrappe her fathers head
