In watery clouds. And then likewise the heaven was ouerspred
With darkness, and a foggye mist steamd upward from the ground.
And near a man about the king too guard him could be found,
But every man in blind by ways ran scattring in the chase,
Through her inchauntments. At the length she getting time and place,
Said* By those lyghtsum eyes of thine which late have ravished mine,
And by that goodly personage and lovely face of thine,
The which compels me that am a Goddess too enclyne
Too make this humble suit too thee that art a mortal wight,
Assuage my flame, and make this son (whoo by his heavenly sight
Foresees all things) thy fathrinlawe: and hardly hold not scorn
Of Circe whoo by long descent of Titans stock am borne.
Thus much said Circe. He right feerce rejecting her request,
And her, said: whooso ere thou art go set thy heart at rest.
I am not thine, nor will not be. Another holds my heart:
And long God grant she may it hold, that I may never start
Too leawdnesse of a foreign lust from bond of lawful bed,
As long as Ianus daughter my sweet singer is not dead.
Dame Circe having often renewd her suit in vain before,
Said: dearly shall thou by thy scorn. For never shall thou more
Return too Singer. Thou shall learn by proof what one can do
That is provoked, and in love, yea and a woman too.
But Circe is both stird too wrath, and also taken in love,
Yea and a woman. Twyce her fa^e too westward she did move,
And twyce too Eastward. Thryce she laid her rod upon his head.
And therwithall three charms she cast. Away king Picus fled.
And woondring that he fled more swift than ^arst he had been wont,
He saw the feathers on his skin, and at the sodein brunt
Became a bird that haunts the wooddes. whereat he taking spyght,
With angrye bill did job upon hard Oaks with all his might,
And in his mood made hollow holes upon their boughs. The hew
Of Crimzen which was in his cloak, upon his feathers grew.
The gold that was a clasp and did his cloak toogither hold,
Is feathers, and about his neck goes circlewyse like gold.
His servants luring in that while often over all the ground
In vain, and fynding no where of their king no incling, found.
Dame Circe. (For by that time she had made the ay^r sheere,
And suffered both the son and wyndes the mistye steames too clear)
And charging her with matter true, demanded for their king,
And offering force, began their darts and Iauelings for too fling.
She sprincling noysom venim straight and iewce of poisoning might,
Did call toogither Eribus and Chaos, and the night,
And all the feendes of darkness, and with howling out along
Made prayers unto Hecate. Scarce ended was her song,
But that (a woondrous thing too tell) the woods lept from their place
The ground did groan: the trees near hand looked pale in all the chase:
The grass besprent with drops of blood looked red: the stones did seem^
Too roar and bellow hoarce: and dogs too howl and raze extreme:
And all the ground too crawle with snakes black scaald: and ghastly spryghts
Fly whisking up and down. The folk were flayghted at these syghts.
And as they woondring stood amaazd, she strokte her witching wand
Upon their faces. At the touch whereof, there out of hand
Came woondrous shapes of savage beasts upon them all. Not one
Retained still his native shape. The setting son was gone
Beyond the utmost c^ast of Spain, and Singer longd in vain
Too see her husband. Both her folk and people ran again
Through all the woods. And ever as they went, they sent their eyes
Before them for too find him out, but no man him espyes.
Then Singer thought it not enough too weep and tear her hear,
And beat herself (all which she did.) She gate abroad, and there
Raundgd over all the broad wyld feelds like one besyds her wits.
Six nights and full as many days (as fortune led by fitts)
She strayd me over hills and dales, and never tasted rest,
Nor meat, nor drink of all the while. The seventh day, sore oppressed
And tired both with travel and with sorrow, down she sat
Upon cold Tybers bank, and there with tears in moorning rate
She warbling on her greef in tune not shirle nor over hye,
Did make her moan, as does the swan: whoo ready for too dye
Does sing his burial song before. Her maree molt at last
With moorning, and she pynde away: and finally she past
Too lither air. But yet her fame remained in the place.
For why the ancient husbandmen according too the case,
Did name it Singer of the nymph that dyed in the same.
Of such as these are, many things that year by fortune came
Both too my heering and my sight. We we^ing resty then
And sluggs by discontinuance, were commanded yet again
Too go a board and hoyse up sails. And Circe told us all
That long and dowtfull passage and rowgh seas should us befall.
I promis thee those words of hers me thoroughly made afraid:
And therefore hither I me gate, and here I have me stayed.
This was the end of Macars tale. And ere long time was gone,
Aenaeas Nurce was buried in a tumb of marble stone,
And this short verse was set thereon. In this same verry place
My Nurcechyld whom the world does know too be a child of grace
Delivering me Ca^eta quick from burning by the Grayes,
Has burnt me dead with such a fire as justly winnes him praise.
Their Cables from the grassye strond were loozde, and by and by
From Circes slanderous house and from her treasons ^arre they fly.
And making too the thickgrowen groves where through the yellow dust
The shady Tyber intoo sea his gu^^hing stream does thrust,
Aenaeas got the Realm of king Latinus Favvnus son
And eeke his daughter, whom in f^yght by force of arms he won.
He enterprysed war against a Nation feerce and strong.
And Turn was wroth for holding of his wife away by wrong.
Against the Shyre of Latium met all Tyrrhene, and long
With busys car^ hawlt victory by force of arms was sought.
Each party too augment their force by foreign succor wrought.
And many sent the Rutills help, and many came too aid
The Troianes: neither was the good Aenaeas ill apayd
Of going too Euanders town. But Venulus in vain
Too outcast Diomeds city went his succor too obtain.
This Diomed under Davvnus king of Calabrye did found
A mighty town, and with his wife in dowrye hild the ground.
Now when from Turnus, Venulus his message had declaard,
Desyring help: The Actolian knight said none could well be spaard.
And in excuce, he told him how he neither durst be bold
Too pressed his fathers folk too war of whom he had no hold,
Nor any of his countrymen had left as then alive
Too arm. And least ye think (quod he) I do a shift contryue,
Although by vppening of the thing my bitter greef reuyue
I will abide too make a new rehersall. After that
The Greeks had burned Troy and on the ground had laid it flat,
And that the Prince of Narix by his rauishing the maid
In Pallas temple, on us all the penance had displayed
Which he himself deserud alone: Then scattred here and there
And harryed over all the seas, we Greeks were feign too bear
Night, thunder, tempest, wrath of heaven and sea, and last of all
Sore shipwrecke at mount Capharey too mend our harms withal.
And least that me too make too long a process ye might deem
In setting forth our heavy happes, the Greeks might that time seem
Right rewfull even too Priamus. Howbeet Minerua she
That wears armor took me from the waves and saved me.
But from my fathers Realm ageine by violence I was driven.
For Venus bearing still in mind the wound I had her given
Long time before, did woork reuendge. By means whereof such toil
Did toss me on the sea, and on the land I found such broyle
By wars, that in my heart I thought them blist of God whom erst
The violence of the raging sea and hideous wynds had perst,
And whom the wrathful Capharey by shipwrecke did confound:
Often wisshing also I had there among the rest been drowned.
My company now having felt the woorst that sea or war
Could work, did faint, and wished an end of straying out so far.
But Agmon whot of nature and too feerce through slaughters made
Said. What remains sirs through which our patience cannot wade?
What further spyght has Venus yet too woork against us more?
When worse misfortunes may be feared than have been felt before,
Then prayer may aduauntadge men, and vowwing may them boot.
But when the woorst is past of things, then fear is under foot.
And when that bale is hygh^st grown, then boot must next ensew.
Although she here me, and do hate us all (which thing is true)
That serve here under Diomed: Yet set we light her hate.
And dearly it should stand us on too purchase high estate.
With such stowt words did Agmon stir dame Venus unto ire
And raysd ageine her settled grudge. Not many had desire
Too here him talk thus out of square. the most of us that are
His friends rebuk^e him for his words. And as he did prepare
Too answer, both his voice and throat by which his voice should go,
Were small: his hear too feathers turned: his neck was clad as tho
With feathers: so was brist and back. The greater feathers stacke
Upon his arms: and intoo wings his elbowes bowwed back.
The greatest portion of his feet was turned intoo toes.
A hardened bill of horn did grow upon his mouth and noze,
And sharpened at the nether end. His fellows Lycus, I Would,
Rethenor, Nyct, and Abas all stood woondring by his side.
And as they woondred, they receyvd the selfsame shape and hew.
And finally the greater part of all my band up flew,
And clapping with their newmade wings, about the ores did gird.
And if ye do demand the shape of this same dowtfull bird,
Even as they be not verry Swannes: so draw they verry near
The shape of Cygnets whyght. The Elk.
With much a do I settled here,
And with a little remnant of my people do obtain
The drygrownds of my fathrinlaw king Davvnus whoo did reign
In Calabry. ^hus much the son of Oenye said. Anon
Sir Venulus returning from the king of Calydon,
Forsook the coast of Puteoll and the feeldes of Messapie,
In which he saw a darksome den forgrowne with busshes hye*
And watered with a little spring. The halfegoate Pan that hour
Possessed it: but heertoofore it was the fayryes bower.
A shepeherd of Appulia from that country scaard them fur^^.
But afterward recovering heart and hardynesse they durst
Despise him when he chaced them, and with their ^^mble feet
Continewed on their dawncing still in time and measure meet
The shepeherd fownd me fault with them: and with his lowtlike leapes
Did counterfette their minyon dawnce, and rapped out by heaps
A rabble of unsavory taunts even like a country cloyne,
Too which, most leawd and filthy terms of purpose he did join.
And after he had once begone, he could not hold his tongue,
Until that in the timber of a tree his throat was cloong.
For now he is a tree, and by his iewce discern ye may
His manners. For the Olyfwyld does sensibly bewray
By berryes full of bitterness his railing tongue. For ay
The harshnesse of his bitter words the berryes bear away.
Now when the kings Ambassador returned home without
The succor of the Aetolian prince, the Rutills being stout
Made luckelesse war without their help: and much on either side
Was shed of blood. Behold king Turn made burning bronds too glyde
Upon their ships, and they that had escaped water, stood
In fear of fire. The flame had sindgd the pitch, the wax, and wood,
And other things that nourish fire, and ronning up the maste
Caught hold upon the sails, and all the takling began too waste,
The Rowers seats did also smoke: when calling too her mind
That these same ships were pynetrees erst and shaken with the wynd
On Ida mo^nt, the moother of the Gods dame Cybel filld
The air with sound of belles, and noise of shalmes. And as she hilld
The reins that ruled the Lions tame which drew her charyot, She
Said thus. O Turnus all in vain these wicked hands of thee
Do cast this fire. for by myself dispoynted it shall be.
I wilnot let the wasting fire consume these ships which are
A parcel of my forest I Would of which I am most chare.
It thundered as the Goddess spoke, and with the thunder came
A storm of rain and skipping hayle. and soodeyne with the same
The sons of Astrey meeting feerce and feyghting verry sore,
Did trouble both the sea and air and set them on a rore.
Dame Cybel using one of (...) serve her turn that tide,
Did break the Cables at (...) which the Troiane ships did ride,
And bare them pro^e, (...) underneath the water did them drive
The Timber of them so^tning ^urnd (...) ^odyes straight alive.
The stemmes were turned too heads, the (...) too swimming feet and toes,
The sides too ribs, the keele that through the middle gally goes
Became the ridgebone of the back, the sails and tackling, hear:
And intoo arms on either side the sayleyards turned were.
Their hew is duskye as before, and now in shape of maid
They play among the waves of which even now they were afraid.
And being Seanymphes, whereas they were bred in mountains hard,
They haunt for ay the water soft, and never afterward
Had mind too see their natyue soil. But yet forgetting not
How many perils they had felt on sea by lucklesse lot,
They often put their helping hand too ships distressed by wynd,
Unless that any carried Greeks. For bearing still in mind
The burning of the town of Troy, they hate the Greeks by kind.
And therefore of Ulysses ships right glad they were too see
The shiuers. and as glad they were as any glad might be,
Too see Alcinous ships wex hard and turned intoo stone.
These ships thus having gotten life and being turned each one
Too nymphs, a body would have thought the miracle so great
Should intoo Turnus wicked heart sum godly fear have beat,
And made him cease his wilful war. But he did still persist.
And either party had their Gods their quarrel too assist,
And courage also: which as good as Gods might well be thought.
In fine they neither for the Realm nor for the scepter sought,
Nor for the Lady Lauine: but for conquest. And for shame
Too seem too shrink in leaving war, they still prolongd the same.
At length dame Venus saw her son obtain the upper hand
King Turnus fell, and eeke the town of Ardea which did stand
Right strong in high estate as long as Turnus lived. But
As Soon as that Aenaeas sword too death had Turnus put,
The town was set on fire: and from amid the embers flew
A foul which till that present time no person ever knew,
And beete the ashes feercely up with flapping of his wing.
The leanenesse, palenesse, doleful sound, and every other thing
That may express a City sakt, yea and the Cities name
Remained still unto the bird. And now the verrye same
With Hernesewes feathers does bewail the town whereof it came.
And now Aenaeas prowess had compelled all the Gods
And Juno also (whoo with him was most of all at odds)
Too cease their old displeasure quyght. And now he having laid
Good ground whereon the growing wealth of July might be stayed,
Was ripe for heaven. And Venus had great suit already made
Too all the Gods. and cleeping Jove did thus with him persuade.
Dear father whoo have never been vncurtuous unto me,
Now show the greatest courtesy (I pray thee) that may be.
And on my son Aenaeas (whoo a graundchyld unto thee
Has got of my blood) if thou will vouchsafe him awght at all)
Uouchsafe sum Godhead too bestow, although it be but small.
It is enough that once he has already seen the Realm
Of Pluto utter pleasurelesse, and passed Styxis stream.
The Gods assented: neither did Queen Juno then appear
In countnance strange, but did consent with glad and merry cheer.
Then Jove. Aenaeas worthy is a saynct in heaven too be.
Thy wish for whom thou do it wish I grant thee frank and free.
This grant of his made Venus glad. She thankt him for the same.
And glyding through the air upon her yoked doves, she came
Too Lavvrent shore, where clad with reed the river Numicke deep
Too seaward (which is near at hand) with stealing pace does creep.
She bade this river wash away what ever mortal were
In good Aenaeas body, and them under sea too bear.
The horned brook fulfilld her hest, and with his water sheere
Did purge and clenze Aenaeas from his mortal body clear.
The better portion of him did remain unto him sownd.
His moother having hallowed him did noynt his body rownd
With heavenly odours, and did touch his mouth with Ambrosie
The which was mixed with Ne^ar sweet, and made him by and by
A God too whom the Romans give the name of Indiges,
Endeuering with their temples and their altars him too please.
Ascanius with the dowble name from thence began too reign,
In whom the rule of Alba and of Latium did remain.
Next him succeeded Siluius, whoose son Latinus hild
The auncien^ name and scepter which his graundsyre erst did weeld.
The famous Ep^t after this Latinus did succeed.
Then Capys and king Capetus. But Capys was indeed
The formest of the two. From this the scepter of the Realm
Descended unto Tyberine, whoo drowning in the stream
Of Tyber left that name theretoo. This Tyberine begat
Feerce Remulus and Acrota. By chance it happened that
The elder brother Remulus for counterfetting often
The thunder, with a thunderbolt was killed from aloft.
From Acrota whoose stayednesse did pass his brothers skill,
The crown did come too Auentine, whoo in the selfsame hill
In which he reigned buried lies, and left thertoo his name.
The rule of nation Palatine at length too Proca came.
In this Kings reign It may be interpreted Applebee.
Pomona livd. There was not too be found
Among the woodnymphes any one in all the Latian ground
That was so conning for too keep an Ortyard as was she,
Nor none so paynefull too preserve the fruit of every tree.
And thervppon she had her name. She past not for the woods
Nor rivers, but the villages and boughs that bare both buddes
And plentuous fruit. In sted of dart a shredding hook she bare,
With which the ouerlusty boughs she eft away did pare
That spreaded out too far, and eft did make therewith a rift
Too greffe another imp upon the stock within the clift.
And least her trees should die through drought, with water of the springs
She moystech of their sucking roots the little crumpled strings.
This was her love and whole delyght. And as for Venus deeds,
She had no mind at all of them. And forbycause she dreedes
Enforcement by the country folk, she walld her yards about,
Not suffering any man at all too enter in or out.
What have not those same nimble laddes so apt too frisk and dance
The Satyrs done? or what the Pannes that wantonly do praunce,
With borned forheads? and the old Silenus whoo is ay
More youthful than his years? and eeke the feend that scares away
The thieves and robbers with his hook, or with his privy part?
Too win her love? But yet than these a far more constant heart
Had sly Turner.
Vertumnus, though he sped no better than the rest.
O Lord, how often being in a moawers garment dressed,
Bare he in bundells sheaues of corn? and when he so was dyght,
He was the verry pattern of a harvest moawer right.
Often bynding newmade hay about his temples he might seem
A haymaker. Often times in hand made hard with woork extreme
He bare a goade, that men would sweere he had but newly then
Unyoakt his weerye Oxen. Had he taken in hand again
A shredding hook, ye would have thought he had a gardener been,
Or proyner of sum vynes. Or had you him with ladder seen
Upon his neck, a gatherer of fruit ye would him deem.
With sword a soldier, with his rod an Angler he did seem.
And finally in many shapes he sought too find access
Too joy the beawty but by sight, that did his heart oppress.
Moreover, putting on his head a woman's wimple gay,
And staying by a staff, graye hears he forth too sight did lay
Upon his forehead, and did feyne a beldame for too be.
By means whereof he came within her goodly ortyards free.
And woondring at the fruit, said. Much more skill have thou I see
Than all the Nymphs of Albula. Hayle Lady mine, the flower
Unspotted of pure maydenhod in all the world this hour.
And with that word he kissed her a little: but his kiss
Was such as true old women would have never given ywis.
Then sitting down upon a bank, he looked upward at
The branches bent with haruests weight. Against him where he sat
A goodly Elm with glistring grapes did grow: which after he
Had praised, and the vine, likewise that ran upon the tree,
But if (quod he) this Elm without the vine did single stand,
It should have nothing (saving leaves) too be desired: and
Ageine if that the vine which ronnes upon the Elm had not
The tree too lean unto, it should upon the ground ly flat.
Yet art not thou admonisht by example of this tree
Too take a husband, neither do thou pass too married be.
But would too God thou would. Sure Queen Helen never had
Mo ^uters, nor the Lady that did cause the battle mad
Between the halfbrute Centavvres and the Lapythes, nor the wife
Of bold Ulysses whoo was eeke ay fearful of his life,
Than thou should have. For thousands now (eue^ now most chiefly when
Thou seem suitors too abhor) desire thee, both of men,
And Gods and halfgoddes, yea and all the fayryes that do dwell
In Albane hills. But if thou will be wise, and myndest well
Too match thy self, and wilt give ear too this old woman here,
(Too whom thou more than too them all art (trust me) leef and dear,
And more than thou thyself beleevst) the common matches flee,
And choose Vertumnus too thy make. And take thou me too be
His pledge. For more he too himself not known is, than too me.
He roues not like a ronneagate through all the world abroad,
This country heerabout (the which is large) is his abode.
He does not (like a number of these common wooers) cast
His love to every one he sees. Thou art the first and last
That ever he set mind upon. Alonly unto thee
He vows himself as long as life does last. Moreover he
Is youthful, and with beawtye sheene endewd by natures gift,
And aptly intoo any shape his person he can shift.
Thou can not bid him be the thing, (though al things you should name)
But that he fitly and with ease will straight becomme the same.
Besides all this, in all one thing both twain of you delyght,
And of the fruits that you love best the firstlings are his right:
And gladly he receyues thy gifts. But neither couets he
Thy Apples, Plommes, nor other fruits new gathered from the tree,
Nor yet the herbs of pleasant sent that in thy gardynes be:
Nor any other kind of thing in all the world, but thee.
Have mercy on his fervent love, and think himself too crave
Here present by the mouth of me, the thing that he would have.
And fear the God that may revenge: as Venus whoo does hate
Hard hearted folks, and Rhamnuse whoo does either soon or late
Express her wrath with myndfull wreake. And too thentent thou may
The more beware, of many things which time by long delay
Has taught me, I will show thee one which over all the land
Of Cyprus blazed is abroad, which being ryghtly skand
May easly bow thy hardened heart and make it for too yild.
One Iphis borne of low degree by fortune had behild
The Lady Anaxarete descended of the race
Of Tevvcer, and in vewwing her the fire of love a pace
Did spread it self through all his bones. With which he stryuing long,
When reason could not conquer rage because it was too strong,
Came humbly too the Ladies house: and one while laying open
His wretched love before her nurce, besought her by the hope
Of Lady Anaxarete her nurcechylds good success,
She would not be against him in that case of his distress.
Anoother while entreating fair sum friend of hers, he prayed
Him earnestly with careful voice, of furthrance and of aid.
Oftymes he did prefer his suit by gentle letters sent.
Often garlonds moysted with the deawe of tears that from him went
He hanged on her posts. Often times his tender sides he laid
Against the threshold hard, and often in sadness did upbraid
The lock with much ungentleness. The Lady crueller
Than are the rising narrow seas, or falling kiddes, and far
More hard than steel of Noricum, and than the stonny rock
That in the quarrye has his root, did him despise and mock.
Beside her doings mercylesse, of statelynesse and spyght
She adding proud and skornefull words, defrauds the wretched wight
Of verry hope. But Iphis now unable any more
Too bear the torment of his greef, still standing there before
Her gate, spoke these his latest words, well Anaxarete,
Thou have the upper hand. Henceforth thou shall not need too be
Agreeued any more with me. Go triumph hardely:
Go vaunt thy self with joy: go sing the song of victory:
Go put a crown of glittring bay upon thy cruel head.
For why thou have the upper hand, and I am gladly dead.
Well steely hearted well: rejoice. Compeld yet shall thou be
Of sumwhat in me for too have a lyking. Thou shall see
A point wherein thou may me deem most thankful unto thee,
And in the end thou shall confess the great desert of me.
But yet remember that as long as life in me does last,
The care of thee shall never from this heart of mine be cast.
For both the life that I do live in hope of thee, and toother
Which nature gives, shall have end and pass away toogither.
The tidings neither of my death shall come too thee fame.
Myself (I do assure thee) will be bringer of the same.
Myself (I say) will present be that those same cruel eyes
Of thine may feed themselves upon this linelesse corce of mine.
But yet O Gods, (if you behold men's deeds) remember me.
(My tongue will se^ue too pray no more) and cause that I may be.
Longtyme hereafter spoken of: and length the life by fame
The which ye have abridgd in years. In saying of this same
He lifted up his watrye eyes and arms that wexed wan
Too those same stulpes which often he had with garlondes decked ere than,
And fastening on the top thereof a halter thus did say.
Thou cruel and ungodly wight, these are the wreathes that may
Most pleasure thee. And with that word he thrusting in his head,
Even then did turn him toward her as good as being dead,
And wretchedly did totter on the post with strangled throat.
The wicket which his feerefull feet in sprawling maynely smote,
Did make a noise: and flying open bewrayd his doing plain.
The servants shreekt, and lifting up his body, but in vain,
Conveyed him too his moothers house, his father erst was slain.
His moother laid him in her lap, and cleeping in her arms
Her sons cold body, after that she had bewayld her harms
With words and doings mootherlyke, the corce with moorning cheer
Too buryall sadly through the town was borne upon a beer.
The house of Anaxarete by chance was near the way
By which this piteous pomp did pass, and of the doolefull lay
The sound came too the ears of her, whom God already began
Too strike* Yet let us see (quod she) the buryall of this man.
And up the high wide windowde house in saying so, she ran.
Scarce had she well on Iphis looked that on the beer did lie,
But that her eyes were stark: and from her limbs the blood began fly.
In stead thereof came palenesse in. And as she backward was
In mind too go, her feet stacke fast and could not stir. And as
She would have cast her countnance back, she could not do it. And
The stonny hardness which a late did in her stomach stand,
Within a while did ouergrow her whole from sole too crown.
And least you think this geere surmysde, even yet in Salamin town
Of Lady Anaxarete the image stands plain.
The temple also in the which the image does remain,
Is unto Venus consecrate by name of looker out.
And therefore weying well these things, I prey thee look about
Good Lady, and away with pride: and be content too frame
Thy self too him that loves thee and cannot quench his flame.
So neither may the Lentons cold thy budding frutetrees kill
Nor yet the sharp and boystous wyndes thy flowring Gardynes spill.
The God that can upon him take what kind of shape he list
Now having said thus much in vain, omitted too persist
In b^^^ames shape, and shewde himself a lusty gentleman,
Appeering too her cheerfully, even like as Phoebus when
He having ouercomme the clouds that did withstand his might,
Does blaze his brightsum beams agein with fuller heat and light.
He offered force. but now no force was needful in the case.
For why she being caught in love with beawty of his face,
Was wounded then as well as he, and began to yield a pace.
Next Proca reignd Amulius in Avvsonye by wrong.
Till Numitor the ryghtfull heir deposed verry long,
Was by his daughters sons restorde. And on the feastfull day
Of Pale, foundation of the walls of Rome they began too lay.
Soon after Tacye, and the Lords of Sabine stird debate:
And Tarpey for her traytrous deed in opening of the gate
Of Tarpey tower was pressed too death according too desert
With armor heapt upon her head. Then feerce and stowt of heart
The Sabines like too toonglesse woolues without all noise of talk
Assayld the Romans in their sleep, and too the gates began stalk
Which Ilias son had closed fast with locks and bars. But yet
Dame Juno had set open one, and as she opened it
Had made no noise of craking with the hindges, so that none
Perceyud the opening of the gate but Venus allalone.
And she had shet it up, but that it is not lawful too
One God too undo any thing another God has do.
The waternymphes of Avvsonie hild all the grounds about
The Church of Ianus where was store of springs fresh flowing out.
Dame Venus prayed these nymphs of help. And they con^i^ering that
The Goddess did request no more but right, denyde it not.
They opened all their fountain veins and made them flow apace.
Howbeet the passage was not yet too Ianus open face
Forclosed: neither had as yet the water stopped the way.
They put rank brimstone underneath the flowing spring that day,
And eeke with smokye rozen set their veins on fire for ay.
Through force of these and other things, the vapor perced low
Even down unto the verry roots on which the springs did grow.
So that the waters which a late in coldness might compare
Even with the frozen Alps, now whot as burning furnace are.
The two gate posts with sprinkling of the fyry water smoakt.
Whereby the gate beehyghted too the Sabines quyght was choakt
With rising of this fountain strange, until that Marsis knight
Had armed him. Then Romulus did boldly offer fight.
The Roman ground with Sabines and with Romans both were spread.
And with the blood of fathrinlawes which wicked sword had shed
Flowde mixed the blood of sonn^inlawes. Howbeet it seemed best
Too both the parties at the length from battle for too rest,
And not too fight too vttrance: And that Tacye should becoome
Copartner with king Romulus of souereintye in Room.
Within a while king Tacye did: And both the Sabines and
The Romans under Romulus in equal right did stand.
The God of battle putting of his glittring helmet then,
With such like words as these bespake the fire of Gods and men.
The time O father (in as much as now the Roman state
Is wexen strong upon the good foundation laid alate,
Depending on the stay of an^) is comme for thee too make
Thy promis good which thou of me and of thy graundchyld spoke.
Which was too take him from the earth and in the heaven him stay.
Thou once (I marked thy gracious words and bare them well away)
Before a great assembly of the Gods did too me say.
There shall be one whom thou shall raise above the starry sky.
Now let they saying take effect. Jove granting by and by
The air was hid with darksom clouds, and thunder forth did fly,
And lyghtning made the world aghast. Which Mars perceiving too
Be lackye tokens for himself his enterprise too do,
Did take his (...) upon his spear and boldly lept intoo
His bloodye charyot. And he lent his horses with his whippe
A yirking lash, and through the air full smoothely down did ^lippe.
And staying on the woody top of mountain Palatine,
He took away king Romulus whoo there did then defyne
The pryuate caces of his folk unseemly for a king.
And as a leaden peller broad enforced from a ^ling
Is wont too dye amid the sky: even so his mortal flesh
Sank from him down the subtle air. In sted whereof a fresh
And goodly shape more stately and more meet for sacred shryne.
Succeeded, like our Quirin that in stately robe does shine.
Hersilia for her feere as lost, of moorning made none end,
Until Queen Juno did command dame Iris too descend
Upon the Raynebowe down, and thus her message for too do.
O of the Latian country and the Sabine nation too
Thou peerlesse perle of womanhod, most worthy for too be
The wife of such a noble prince as heertoofore was he,
And still too be the wife of him canonized by name,
Of Quirin: cease thy tears. And if thou have desire the same
Thy holy husband for too see, ensew me too the queache
That grows green on Quirins hill, whoose shadows ouerreache
The temple of the Roman King. Dame Iris did obey.
And ^yding by her painted bow, in former words did say
(...) too Hersilia. She scarce lifting up her eyes,
With sober countnance answered. O thou Goddess (for surmyse
I cannot whoo thou art, but yet I well may understand
Thou art a Goddess) leede me O dear Goddess leede me, and
My husband too me show. Whom if the fatal susters three
Will of they: gracious goodness grant me leave but once too see,
I shall account me intoo heaven received for too be.
Immediately with Thavvmants imp too Quirins hill she went.
There glyding from the sky a star straight down too ground was sent,
The sparks of whoose bright blazing beams did burn Hersilias hear.
And with the star the ayre^ did vher hear too heauenward bear.
The buylder of the town of Room receiving straight the same
Between his old acquaynted hands, did alter both her name
And eeke her body, calling her dame Ora. And by this
She jointly with her husband for a Goddess woorshipt is.
Finis Libri decimi quarti.
THE.XV. BOOK OF Ouids Metamorphosis.
A Person in the while was sought sufficient too sustain
The burden of so great a charge, and worthy for too reign
In stead of such a mighty prince. The noble Nume by fame
(Whoo harped then upon the truth before too pass it came)
Appointed too the Empire was. This Numa thought it not
Enough that he the knowledge of the Sabine rites had got.
The deepenesse of the noble wit too greater things was bent,
Too search of things the natures out. The care of this intent
Did cause that he from Curie and his native Country went
With peynfull travel, too the town where Hercules did host.
And asking who it was of Greece that in the Italian coast
Had buylt that town, an aged man well seen in stories old,
Too satisfye his mind therein the process thus him told.
As Hercules enriched with the Spannish kyne did hold
His voyage from the Ocean sea, men say with lucky cut
He came a land on Lacine coast. And while he there did put
His beace too grazing, he himself in Crotons house did rest
The greatest man in all those parts and unto strangers best:
And that he there refresht him of his tedious travel, and
That when he should depart, he said. where now thy house does stand,
Shall in thy childers childrens time a City builded be.
Which words of his have proved true as plainly now we see.
For why there was one Myscelus a Greek, Alemons son,
A person more in favor of the Gods than any one
In those days was. The Hercules.
God that bears the boystous club did stay
Upon him being fast a sleep, and said: go seek straight way
The stonny stream of Aeserie. Thy native soil for ay
Forsake. And sore he threatened him unless he did obey.
The God and sleep departed both toogither. Up did rise
Al^mons son, and in himself did secretly deuyse
Upon this vision. Long his mind strove dowtfull too and fro.
The God had go. His country laws did say he should not go,
And death was made the penalty for him that would do so.
Clear Titan in the Ocean sea had hid his lyghtsomme head,
And duskye night had put up hers most thick with stars bespred.
The selfsame God by Myscelus did seem too stand ef^soone,
Commanding him the selfsame thing that he before had done,
And threatening mo and greater plages unless he did obey.
Then being stricken sore in fear he went about streyghtway
His household from his natyue land too foreign too convey.
A rumor heerevppon did rise through all the town of Arge
And disobedience of the law was laid too his charge.
As Soon as that the case had first been pleaded and the deed
Apparently perceived, so that witness did not need,
Arreyned and ^orlorne too heaven he cast his hands and eyes,
And said: O God whoose labors twelve have purchaste thee the skies,
Assist me I the pray. For thou art author of my crime.
When judgment should be given it was the guyse in ancient time
With whyght stones too acquit the clear, and eeke with black too cast
The giltye. That time also so the heavy sentence past.
The stones were cast unmerciful all black intoo the pot.
But when the stones were powered out too number, there was not
A black among them. All were whyght. And so through Hercles power
A gentle judgment did proceed, and he was quit that hour.
Then gave he thanks too Hercules, and having prosperous blast,
Cut over the Ionian sea, and so by Tarent past
Which Spartanes buylt, and Cybaris, and Neaeth salentine,
And Thurine bay, and Emese, and eeke the pastures fine
Of Calabrye. And having scarce well sought the coasts that lie
Upon the sea, he found the mouth of fatal Aeserye.
Not far from thence, he also found the tumb in which the ground
Did kiuer Crotons holy bones, and in that place did found
The City that was willed him, and gave theretoo the name
Of him that there lay buried. Such original as this same
This City in the Italian coast is said too have by fame.
Here dwelt a man of Samos I Will, who for the hate he had
Too Lordlynesse and Tyranny, though vnconstreynd was glad
Too make himself a bannisht man. And though this person weere
Far distant from the Gods by site of heaven: yet came he near
Too them in mind. And he by sight of soul and reason clear
Behild the things which nature does too fleshly eyes deny.
And when with care most vigilant he had assuredly
Imprinted all things in his heart, he set them openly
Abroad for other folk too learn. He taught his silent sort
(Which woondred at the heavenly words their master did report)
The first fonndation of the world: the cause of every thing:
What nature was: and what was God: whence snow and lyghtning spring:
And whither Jove or else the wynds in breaking clouds do thunder:
What shakes y^ earth: what law ye stars do keep their courses under:
And what soever other thing is hid from common sense.
^e also is the first that did inioyne an abstinence
Too feed of any living thing. He also first of all
Spoke thus: although right lernedly, yet too effect but small.
Ye mortal men forbear too frank your flesh with wicked food.
Ye have both corn and fruits of trees and grapes and herbs right good.
And though that sum be harsh and hard: yet fire may make them well
Both soft and sweet. Ye may have milk, and honny which does smell
Of flowers of time. The lauas earth does yield you plentiously
Most gentle food, and riches too content both mind and ^ye.
There needs no slaughter nor no blood too get your living by.
The beasts do break their fast with flesh: and yet not all beasts neither.
For horses, sheep, and Rotherbeastes too live by grass had lever.
The nature of the beast that does delyght in bloody food,
Is cruel and unmerciful. As Lions feerce of mood,
Armenian Tigers, Bears, and Woolues. Oh what a wickedness
It is too cra^ the mawe with mawe, and frank up flesh with flesh,
And for one living thing too live by killing of another:
As whoo should say, that of so great abundance which our moother
The earth does yield most bountuously, none other might delyght
Thy cruel teethe too chawe upon, than grisly wounds that might
Express the Cyclops guyse? or else as if thou could not stawnche
The hunger of thy greedye gut and evil mannerd pawnche,
Unless thou stroyd sum other wight. But that same ancient age
Which we have naamd the golden world, cleene void of all such rage,
Livd blessedly by fruit of trees and herbs that grow on ground,
And stained not their mouths with blood. Then birds might safe and sound
Fly where they listed in the air. The hare vnscaard of hound
Went pricking over all the feeldes. No angling hook with bayt
Did hang the seely fish that bote mistrusting no deceyt.
All things were void of guylefulnesse: no treason was in trust:
But all was freendshippe love and peace. But after that the lust
Of one (what God so ere he was) disdeyning former fare,
Too cram that cruel croppe of his with fleshmeate did not spare,
He made a way for wickedness. And first of all the knyfe
Was staynd with blood of savage beasts in ridding them of life.
And that had nothing been amiss, if there had been the stay.
For why we grant, without the breach of godliness we may
By death confound the things that seek too take our lives away.
But as too kill them reason was: even so agein their was
No reason why too eat their flesh. This leawdnesse thence did pass
On further still. Whereas there was no sacrifyse beforne,
The Swyne (because with) hoked groyne he wrooted up the corn,
And did deceive the tillmen of their hope next year thereby)
Was deemed worthy by desert in sacrifyse too dye.
The Goat for byghting vynes was slain at Bacchus altar whoo
Wreakes such misdeedes. Their own offense was hurtful to these two.
But what have you poor sheep misdoone a cattle meek and meeld,
Created for too maynteine man, whoose fulsomme duggs do yield
Sweet Nectar, whoo does clothe us with your wool in soft array?
Whoose life does more us benefit than does your death farreway?
What trespass have the Oxen done? a beast without all guyle
Or craft, unhurtful, simple, borne too labor every while?
In faith he is unmindful and unworthy of increace
Of corn, that in his heart can find his tilman too releace
From plowgh, too cut his throat: that in his heart can find (I say)
Those necks with hatchets of too strike, whoose skin is worn away
With labring ay for him: whoo turned so often his land most tough,
Whoo brought so many haruestes home. yet is it not enough
That such a great outrageousenesse committed is. They father
Their wickedness upon the Gods. And falsely they do gather
That in the death of peynfull Ox the highest does delyght.
A sacrifyse unblemished and fairest unto sight,
(For beawtye woorketh them their bane) adornd with garlonds, and
With glittring gold, is cyted at the altar for too stand.
There heeres he words (he wotes not what) y^ which y^ priest does pray,
And on his forehead suffers him between his horns too lay
The ears of corn that he himself has wrought for in the clay,
And stayneth with his blood the knyfe that he himself perchance
Has in the water sheere ere then behild by soodein glaunce.
Immediately they haling out his hartstrings still alive,
And poring on them, seek therein Gods secrets too re^ryue.
Whence commes so greedy appetyte in men, of wicked meat?
And dare ye O ye mortal men adventure thus too eat?
Nay do not (I beseech ye) so. But give good ^are and heed
Too that that I shall warn you of, and trust it as your creed,
That whenever you do eat your Oxen, you deuowre
Your husbandmen. And forasmuch as God this instant hour
Does move my tongue too speak, I will obey his heavenly power.
My God Apollos temple I will set you open, and
Disclose the woondrous heavens themselves, and make you understand
The Oracles and secrets of the Godly majesty.
Great things, and such as wit of man could never yet espy,
And such as have been hidden long, I purpose too descrye.
I mind too leave the earth, and up among the stars too slye.
I mind too leave this grosser place, and in the clouds too fly,
And on stowt Atlas shoulders strong too rest my self on hye,
And looking down from heaven on men that wander here and there
In dreadful fear of death as though they void of reason were,
Too give them exhortation thus: and plainly too vnwynd
The whole discourse of destiny as nature has assignd.
O men amaazd with dread of death, why fear ye Limbo Styx,
And other names of vanity, which are but Poets tricks?
And perrills of another world all false surmysed geere?
For whither fire or length of time consume the bodies here,
Ye well may think that further harms they cannot suffer more.
For souls are free from death. Howbeet, they living evermore
Their former dwellings are receyud and live ageine in new.
For I myself (right well in mind I bear it too be true)
Was in the time of Trojan war Euphorbus Panthevves son
Quyght through whoose heart the deathfull spear of Menelay did run.
I late age in Iunos Church at Argos did behold
And knew the target which I in my left hand there did hold.
Al things do change. But nothing sure does perrish. This same spright
Does fleet, and fisking here and there does swiftly take his flight
From one place too another place, and enters every wight,
Removing out of man too beast, and out of beast too man.
But yet it never perrisheth nor never perrish can.
And even as supple wax with ease receives fygures strange,
And keeps not ay one shape, ne bydes assured ay from change,
And yet continues always wax in substance: So I say
The soul is ay the selfsame thing it was and yet astray
It fleeteth intoo sundry shapes. Therefore least Godliness
Be vanquished by outrageous lust of belly beastlynesse,
Forbear (I speak by prophecy) your kinsfolks ghostes too chase
By slaughter: neither nourish blood with blood in any case.
And since on open sea the wynds do blow my sails apace,
In all the world there is not that that stands at a stay.
Things eb and flow: and every shape is made too pass away.
The time itself continually is fleeting like a brook.
For neither brook nor lyghtsomme time can tarrye still. But look
As every wave dryues other forth, and that that commes behynd
Both thrusts and is thrust itself: Even so the times by kind
Do fly and follow both at once, and evermore renew.
For that that was before is left, and straight there does ensew
Anoother that was never erst. Each twincling of an eye
Does change. We see that after day commes night and darks the sky,
And after night the lyghtsum Sun succeeds orderly.
Like color is not in the heaven when all things weery lie
At midnyght ^ound a sleep, as when the daystarre clear and bright
Commes forth upon his milkwhyght steed. Ageine in other plyght
The morning Pallants daughter fair the messenger of light
Delivers intoo Phoebus hands the world of cleerer hew.
The circle also of the son what ^yme it ryseth new
And when it sets, looks red* but when it mounts most hye,
Then looks it whyght, because that there the nature of the sky
Is better, and from filthye dross of earth does further fly
The image also of the Moon that shyneth ay by night,
Is never of one quantity. For that that gives light
Too day, is lesser than the next that follows, till the full.
And then contrarywyse each day her light away does pull.
What? see thou not how that the year as representing plain
The age of man, departes itself in quarters four? first bayne
And tender in the spring it is, even like a sucking babe.
Then green, and void of strength, and lush, and foggye, is the blade*
And cheeres the husbandman with hope. Then all things flourish gay.
The earth with flowers of sundry hew then seems for too play,
And virtue small or none too herbs there does as yet belong.
The year from spring tide passing forth too summer, wexeth strong,
Becomes like a lusty youth. For in our life through out
There is no time more plentiful, more lusty whote and stout.
Then follows Harvest when the heat of youth grows sumwhat cold,
Ripe, meeld, disposed mean betwixt a yoongman and an old,
And sumwhat sprent with grayish hear. Then ugly winter last
Like age steals on with trembling steps, all bald, or ouercast
With shirle thin hear as whyght as snow. Our bodies also ay
Do alter still from time too time, and never stand at stay.
We shall not be the same we were too day or yisterday.
The day has been we were but seed and only hope of men,
And in our moothers womb we had our dwelling place as then,
Dame Nature put too conning hand and suffered not that we
Within our moothers streyned womb should ay distressod be,
But brought us out too air, and from our prison set us free.
The child newborn lies void of strength. Within a season tho
He wexing fowerfooted lernes like savage beasts too go.
Then sumwhat foltring, and as yet not firm of foot, he stands
By getting sumwhat for too help his sinews in his hands.
From that time growing strong and swift, he passes forth the space
Of youth: and also wearing out his middle age a pace,
Through drooping ages steepye path he ronneth out his race
This age does undermine the strength of former years, and throws
It down. which thing old Milo by example plainly shows.
For when he saw those arms of his (which heeretoofore had been
As strong as ever Hercules in woorking deadly teene
Of biggest beasts) hang flapping down, and naught but empty skin,
He wept. And Helen when she saw her aged wrincles in
A glass wept also: musing in herself what men had seen,
That by two noble princes sons she twyce had ravished been.
Thou time the eater up of things, and age of spyghtfull teene.
Destroy all things. And when that long continuance has them bit,
You leysurely by lingering death consume them every whit.
And these that we call Elements do never stand at stay.
The enterchaunging course of them I will before ye lay.
Give heed thertoo. This endless world conteynes therein I say
Four substances of which all things are gendred. Of these four
The Earth and Water for their mass and weight are sunken lower.
The other cowple Air, and Fire the purer of the twain
Mount up, and naught can keep the^ down. And though there do remain
A space between each one of them: yet every thing is made
Of themsame four, and intoo them at length ageine do fade.
The earth resolving leysurely does melt too water sheere.
The water fined turns too air. The air eeke purged clear
From grossness, spyreth up aloft, and there becomes fire.
From thence in order contrary they back ageine retire.
Fire thickening passes intoo Air, and Air wexing gross,
Returns too water: Water eeke congealing intoo dross,
Becomes earth. No kind of thing keeps ay his shape and hew.
For nature loving ever change repayres one shape a new
Upon another* neither does there perrish aught (trust me)
In all the world, but altring takes new shape. For that which we
Do term by name of being borne, is for too gin too be
Another thing than that it was: And likewise for too dye,
Too cease too be the thing it was. And though that varyably
Things pass perchance from place too place: yet all from whence they came
Returning, do vnperrisshed continue still the same.
But as for in one shape, be sure that nothing long can last.
Even so the ages of the world from gold too Iron past.
Even so have places oftentimes exchaunged their estate.
For I have seen it sea which was substanciall ground alate,
Ageine where sea was, I have seen the same become dry land,
And shelles and scales of Seafish far have lain from any strond.
And in the tops of mountains high old Anchors have been ^ound.
Deep valleys have by watershotte been made of level ground,
And hills by force of gulling often have intoo sea been worn.
Hard gravel ground is sumtyme seen where marris was beforne,
And that that erst did suffer drowght, becomes standing lakes.
Here nature sends new springs out, and there the old in takes.
Full many rivers in the world through earthquakes heretoofore
Have either chaundgd their former course, or dryde and run no more.
So Lycus being swallowed up by gaping of the ground,
A greatway of fro thence is in another channel found.
Even so the river Erasine among the feeldes of Arge
Sinkes onewhyle, and another while ronnes great ageine at large*
Caycus also of the land of Mysia (as men say)
Misliking of his former head, ronnes now another way.
In Sicill also Amasene ronnes sumtyme full and hye,
And sumtyme stopping up his spring, he makes his channel dry.
Men drank the waters of the brook Anigrus heretoofore,
Which now is such that men abhor too towche them any more.
Which commes too pass, (unless we will discredit Poets quyght)
Because the Centaures vanquisshed by Hercules in fight
Did wash their wounds in that same brook. But does not Hypanis
That springs in the Scythian hills, which at his fountain is
Right pleasant, afterward becomme of brackish bitter taste?
Antissa, and Phenycian Tire, and Pharos in time past
Were compast all about with waves: but none of all these three
Is now an I Will. Ageine the town of Levvcas once was free
From sea, and in the ancient time was joined too the land.
But now enuirond round about with water it does stand.
Men say that Sicill also has been joined too Italy
Until the sea consumde the bounds beetweene, and did supply
The room with water. If ye go too seek for Helicee
And Burye which were Cities of Achaia, you shall see
Them hidden under water, and the shipmen yet do show
The walls and steeples of the towns drowned under as they row.
Not far from Pitthey Troyzen is a certain high ground found
All void of trees, which heeretoofore was plain and level ground,
But now a mountain for the wyndes a (woondrous thing too say)
Enclosed in the hollow caves of ground, and seeking way
Too pass therefro, in struggling long too get the open skye^
In vain, (because in all the cave there was no vent whereby
Too issue out,) did stretch the ground and make it swell on hye,
As does a bladder that is blown by mouth, or as the skin
Of horned Goat in bottlewyse when wynd is gotten in.
The swelling of the foresaid place remaynes at this day still,
And by continuance waxing hard is grown a pretye hill.
Of many things that come too mind by heersay, and by skill
Of good experience, I a few will utter too you mo.
What? does not water in his shapes change straungely too and fro:
The well of horned Hammon is at noonetyde passing cold.
At morn and even it wexeth warm. At midnyght none can hold
His hand therein for passing heat. The well of Athamane,
Is said too kindle wood what time the moon is in the wane.
The Cicons have a certain stream which being droonk does bring
Men's bowwelles intoo Marble hard: and whatsoever thing
Is towcht therewith, it turns too stone. And by your bounds behold
The rivers Crathe and Sybaris make yellow hear like gold
And Amber. There are also springs (which thing is far more strange)
Which not the body only, but the mind do also change.
Whoo has not hard of Salmacis that foul and filthye sink?
Or of the lake of Aethyop, which if a man do drink,
He either ronneth mad, or else with woondrous drowzinesse
Forgoeth quyght his memory. Whoo ever does repress
His thirst with drawght of Clitor well, hates wine, and does delyght
In only water: either for because there is a might
Contrary unto warming wine by nature in the well,
Or else because (for so the folk of Arcadye do tell)
Melampus Amythaons son (when he delivered had
King Prae^us daughters by his charms and herbs from being mad,)
Cast intoo that same water all the baggage wherewithal
He purdgd the madness of their mynds. And so it did befall,
That lothsomnesse of wine did in those waters ay remain.
Ageine in Lyncest contrary effect too this does reign.
For who ^o drinks too much thereof, he ^eeleth here and there
As if by quaffing wine no whyt alayd he droonken were.
There is a Lake in Arcadye which Pheney men did name
In ancient time, whoose dowtfulnesse deserves justly blame.
A night times take thou heed of it, for if thou taste the same
A nyghttymes, it will hurt. but if thou drink it in the day
It hurts not. Thus lakes and streams (as well perceive ye may)
Have diverse powers and diversely. Even so the time has been
That Delos which stands steadfast now, on waves was floting seen.
And Galyes have been sore afraid of frusshing by the Isles
Symplegads which toogither dashed upon the sea erewhyles,
But now do stand immovable against both wind and tide.
Mount Aetna with his burning ^ouens of brimstone shall not bide
Ay fyrye: neither was it so for ever erst. For whither
The earth a living creature be, and that too breathe out hither
And thither flame, great store of vents it have in sundry places,
And that it have the power too shift those vents in diverse caces,
Now damming these, now opening those, in moving too and fro:
Or that the whisking wynds restreynd within the earth bylowe,
Do beat the stones against the stones, and other kind of stuff
Of fyrye nature, which do fall on fire with every puff:
As Soon as those same wynds do cease, the caves shall straight be cold.
Or if it be a Rozen mowld that soon of fire takes hold,
Or brimstone mixed with clayish soil on fire does lightly fall:
Undowtedly as soon as that same soil consumed shall
No longer yield the fatty food too feed the fire withal,
And ravening nature shall forgo her woonted nourishment,
Then being able too abide no longer famishment,
For want of sustenance it shall cease his burning. I do find
By fame, that under Charlsis wayne in Pallene are a kind
Of people which by dyuing thryce three times in Triton lake
Becomme all fethred, and the shape of birds upon them take.
The Scythian witches also are reported for too do
The selfsame thing (but hardly I give credit thervntoo)
By smearing poison over all their bodies. But (and if
A man too matters tryde by proof may saufly give belief,)
We see how flesh by lying still a while and ketching heat
Does turn too little living beasts. And yet a further feate,
Go kill an Ox and burye him, (the thing by proof man sees)
And of his rotten flesh will breed the flower gathering Bees,
Which as their father did before, love feeldes exceedingly,
And unto woork in hope of gain their busye limbs apply.
The Hornet is engendered of a lustye buried Steed.
Go pull away the cleas from Crabbes that in the sea do breed,
And burye all the rest in mowld, and of the same will spring
A Scorpion which with writhen tail will threaten for too sting.
The Caterpillars of the feelde the which are wont too weaue
Hoar filmes upon the leaves of trees, their former nature leave,
(Which thing is known too husbandmen) and turn too Butterflyes.
The mud has in it certain seed whereof green frosshes rise.
And first it brings them footelesse forth. Then after, it does frame
Legs apt too swim: and furthermore of purpose that the same
May serve them for too leap a far, their hinder part is much
More longer than their forepart is. The Bearwhelp also which
The Bear has newly littred, is no whelp immediately.
But like an evil favored lump of flesh alive does lie.
The dam by licking shapeth out his members orderly
Of such a syse, as such a piece is able too conceive.
Or mark ye not the Bees of whom our honey we receive,
How that their young ones which do lie within the sixsquare wax
Are limblesse bodies at the first, and after as they wex
In process take both feet and wings? What man would think it true
That Lady Venus simple birds the Dooues of silver hew,
Or Iunos bird that in his tail bears stars, or Jove's stowt knight
The Earne, and every other foul of whatsoever flight,
Could all be hatched out of eggs, unless he did it know?
Sum folk do hold opinion when the backebone which doothe grow
In man, is rotten in the grave, the pith becommes a snake.
Howbeete of other things all these their first beginning take.
One bird there is that does renew itself and as it were
Beget it self continually. The Syrians name it there
A Phoenix. Neither corn nor herbs this Phoenix lives by.
But by the iewce of frankincence and gum of Amomye.
And when that of his life well full fyuehundred years are past,
Upon a Holmerree or upon a Date tree at the last
He makes him with his talants and his hardened bill a nest.
Which when that (...) with Casia sweet and Nardus soft has dressed,
And strowed it with Cynnamom and Myrrha of the best,
He rucketh down upon the same, and in the spyces dies.
Soon after, of the fathers corce men say there does arise
Another little Phoenix which as many years must live
As did his father. He (as soon as age does strength him give
Too bear the burden) from the tree the weyghty nest does lift,
And godlyly his cradle thence and fathers herce does shift.
And flying through the subtle air he gettes too Phoebus town,
And there before the temple door does lay his burden down.
But if that any noueltye worth woondring be in these,
Much rather may we wonder at the Hyen if we please.
Too see how interchaungeably it one while does remain
A female, and another while becomes male again.
The creature also which does live by only air and wynd,
All colors that it leans too does counterfeit by kind.
The Grapegod Bacchus, when he had subdewd the land of Inde,
Did find a spotted beast called Lynx, whoose urine (by report)
By towching of the open air congealeth in such sort,
As that it does becomme a stone. So Coral (which as long
As water hydes it is a shrub and soft) becomes strong
And hard as soon as it does towch the air. The day would end,
And Phoebus panting steedes should in the Ocean deep descend,
Before all alterations I in words could comprehend.
So see we all things chaungeable. One nation gathers strength:
Another wexeth weak: and both do make exchange at length.
So Troy which once was great and strong as well in wealth as men,
And able ten years space too spare such store of blood as then,
Now being bace has nothing left of all her wealth too show,
Save ruins of the ancient works which grass does ouergrowe,
And tumbes wherein their auncetours lie buried on a row.
Once Sparta was a famous town: Great Mycene florisht trim:
Both Athens and Amphions towers in honor once did swim.
A pelting plot is Sparta now: great Mycene lies on ground.
Of Theab the town of Oedipus what have we more than sound?
Of Athens king Pandions town what rests more than name?
Now also of the race of Troy is rising (so says fame)
The City Room, which at the bank of Tyber that does run
Down from the hill of Appennyne) already has begone
With great aduysement for too lay foundation of her state.
This town then changes by increase the form it had alate,
And of the universal world in time to comme shall hold
The souereintye so prophesies and lotts (men say) have told.
And as (I do remember me) what time that Troy decayed,
The prophet Helen Priams son these words ensewing said
Before Aenaeas dowting of his life in weeping plyght:
O Goddess son, believe me (if thou think I have foresyght
Of things too comme) Troy shalnot quyght decay while thou do live.
Both fire and sword shall unto thee thy passage freely give.
Thou must from hence: and Troy with thee convey away in haste,
Until that both thyself and Troy in foreign land be plaast
More freendly than thy native soil. Moreover I foresee,
A City by the offspring of the Trojans buylt shall be,
So great as never in the world the like was seen before
Nor is this present, neither shall be seen for evermore.
A number of most noble peers for many years before
Shall make it strong and puyssant: But he that shall it make
The souereine Lady of the world, by right descent shall take
His first beginning from thy son the little Iule. And when
The earth has had her time of him, the sky and welkin then
Shall have him up for evermore, and heaven shall be his end.
Thus far I (well remember me) did Helens words extend
Too good Aenaeas. And it is a pleasure unto me
The City of my countrymen increasing thus too see:
And that the Grecians victory becommes the Trojans weal.
But least forgetting quyght themselves our horses hap too steal
Beyond the mark: the heaven and all that under heaven is found,
Does alter shape. So does the ground and all that is in ground.
And we that of the world are part (considring how we be
Not only flesh, but also sowles, which may with passage free
Remove them intoo every kind of beast both tame and wyld)
Let live in saufty honestly with slaughter vndefyld,
The bodies which perchance may have the sprits of our brothers,
Our sisters, or our parents, or the spirits of sum others
Alyed too us either by sum freendshippe or sum kin,
Or at the least the souls of men abiding them within.
And let us not Thyestes like thus furnish up our boordes
With bloodye bowels. Oh how leawd example he auoordes?
How wickedly prepares he himself too murder man?
That with a cruel knyfe does cut the throat of Calf, and can
Unmouably give heering too the lowing of the dam?
Or stick the kid that wayleth like the little babe? or eat
The foul that he himself before had often fed with meat?
What wants of utter wickedness in woorking such a feate?
What may he after pass too do? well either let your steeres
Wear out themselves with woork, or else impute their death too years.
Against the wynd and weather cold let Wethers yield ye cotes.
And vdders full of batling milk receive ye of the Goats.
Away with sprindges, snares, and grinnes, away with Risp and net.
Away with guylefull feats: for fowls no lymetwiggs see ye set.
No feared feathers pitche ye up too keep the Reddeere in,
Ne with deceytfull bayted hook seek fishes for too win.
If awght do harm, destroy it. but destroyt and do no more.
Forbear the flesh: and feed your mouths with fitter food therefore.
Men say that Numa furnisshed with such philosophye
As this and like, returned too his native soil, and by
Entreatance was content of Room too take the souereintye.
Right happy in his wife which was a nymph, right happy in
His guydes which were the Muses nine, this Numa did begin
Too teach Religion. by the means whereof he shortly drew
That people unto peace whoo erst of naught but battle knew.
And when through age he ended had his reign and eeke his life,
Through Latium he was moorned for of man and child and wife
As well of high as low degree. His wife forsaking quyght
The City, in vale Aricine did hyde her out of sight,
Among the thickest groves* and there with syghes and playnts did let
The sacrifyse of Diane whom Orestes erst had fet
From Taurica in Chersonese, and in that place had set.
How often ah did the woodnymphes and the waternymphes persuade
Egeria for too cease her moan? what means of comfort made
They? Ah h^w often Theseus son her weeping thus bespake?
O Nymph, thy moorning moderate: thy sorrow sumwhat slake:
Not only thou have cause too heart thy fortune for too take.
Behold like happes of other folks, and this mischaunce of thine
Shall grieve thee less. would God examples (so they were not mine)
Might comfort thee. But mine perchance may comfort thee. If thou
In talk by hap haste heard of one Hippolytus ere now,
That through his fathers light belief, and stepdames craft was slain,
It will a wonder seem too thee, and I shall have much pain
Too make thee too believe the thing. But I am very he.
The daughter of Pasyphae in vain often tempting me
My father chamber too defyle, surmysde me too have sought
The thing that she with all her heart would feign I should have wrought.
And whither it were for fear I should her wickedness bewray,
Or else for spyght because I had so often said her nay,
She chardgd me with her own offense. My father by and by
Condemning me, did banish me his Realm without cause why.
And at my going like a fo did ban me bitterly.
Too Pitthey Troyzen outlawelike my chariot straight took I
My way lay hard upon the shore of Corinth. Soodeinly
The sea did rise, and like a mount the wave did swell on hye,
And seemed howger for too grow in drawing ever nigh,
And roaring clyued in the top. Up starts immediately
A horned bullock from amid the broken wave, and by
The breast did raise him in the air, And at his nosethrills and
His platter mouth did puff out part of sea upon the land.
My servants hearts were sore afraid. But my heart musing ay
Upon my wrongful banishment, did naught at all dismay.
My horses setting up their ears and snorting wexed shye,
And being greatly flayghted with the monster in their eye,
Turned down too sea: and on the rocks my wagon drew. In vain
I stryuing for too hold them back, laid hand upon the reyne
All whyght with ^ome, and haling back lay almost bolt vpryght.
And sure the feercenesse of the steedes had yielded too my might,
But that the whaele that ronneth ay about the Extree round,
Did break by dashing on a stub, and overthrew too ground.
Then from the Charyot I was snatched the brydles being cast
About my limbs. Ye might have seen my sinews sticking fast
Upon the stub: my gu^ts drawn out alive: my members, part
Still left upon the stump, and part forth harryed with the cart:
The crasshing of my broken bones: and with what passing pain
I breathed out my weery ghost. There did not whole remain
One piece of all my corce by which ye might discern as tho
What lump or part it was. For all was wound from top too to.
Now can thou nymph, or dare thou compare thy harms with mine?
Moreover I the lightlesse Realm behild with these same eyne,
And bathde my tattred body in the river Phlegeton.
And had not bright Apollos son his cunning shewde upon.
My body by his surgery, my life had quyght be gone.
Which after I by force of herbs and lee^hecraft had ageine
Receyud by Aes^ulapius means, though Pluto did disdeine,
Then Cynthia (least this gift of hers might work me greater spyght)
Thick clouds did round about me cast. And too thentent I might
Be saufe myself, and harmelessely appear too others sight:
She made me old. And for my face, she left it in such plyght,
That none can know me by my look. And long she dowted whither
Too give me Dele or Crete. At length refusing both toogither,
She plaast me here. And therwithall she bade me give up quyght
The name that of my horses in remembrance put me might.
For whereas erst Horses^aine.
Hippolytus has been thy name (quod she)
I will that Twyce man.
Virbie afterward thy name for ever be.
From that time forth within this wood I keep my residence,
As of the meaner Gods, a God of small magnificence
And here I hyde me underneath my souereine Ladies wing
Obeying humbly too her hest in every kind of thing.
But yet the harms of other folk could nothing help nor boot
Aegerias sorrows too assuage. Down at a mountains foot
She lying melted intoo tears, till Phoebus sister sheene
For pity of her great distress in which she had her seen,
Did turn her too a fountain clear, and melted quyght away
Her members intoo water thin that never should decay.
The strangeness of the thing did make the nymphs astonyed: and
The Lady of Amazons son amaazd thereat did stand,
As when the Tyrrhene Tilman saw in earing of his land
The fatal clod first stir alone without the help of hand,
And by and by forgoing quyght the earthly shape of clod,
Too take the seemly shape of man, and shortly like a God
Too tell of things as then too comme. The Tyrrhenes did him call
By name of Tages. He did teach the Tuskanes first of all
Too guess by searching bulks of beasts what after should befall.
Or like as did king Romulus when soodeinly he found
His lawnce on mountain Palatine fast rooted in the ground,
And bearing leaves, no longer now a weapon but a tree,
Which shadowed such as woondringly came thither for too see.
Or else as Cippus when he in the ronning brook had seen
His horns. For why he saw them, and supposing there had been
No credit too be given unto the glauncing image, he
Put often his fingers too his head, and felt it so too be.
And blaming now no more his eyes, incomming from the chase
With conquest of his foes, he stayed. And lifting up his face
And with his face, his horns too heaven, he said: what ever thing
Is by this wonder meant O Gods, If joyful news it bring
I pray ye let it joyful too my folk and country be:
But if it threaten evil, let the evil light on me.
In saying so, an altar green of clowwers he did frame,
And offered fuming frankincence in fire upon the same,
And powered boawles of wine thereon, and searched therwithall
The quiuering inwards of a sheep too know what should befall.
A Tyrrhene wizard having sought the bowelles, saw therein
Great chaunges and attempts of things then ready too begin,
Which were not plainly manifest. But when that he at last
His eyes from inwards of the beast on Cippus horns had cast,
Hayle king (he said.) For unto thee O Cippus, unto thee,
And too thy horns shall this same place and Room obedyent be.
Abridge delay: and make thou haste too enter at the gates
Which tarrye open for thee. So command the soothfast fates.
Thou shall be king as soon as thou have entered once the town,
And thou and thine for evermore shall wear the royal crown.
With that he stepping back his foot, did turn his frowning face
From Room ward, saying. Far, O far, ye Gods such handsel chase.
More right it were I all my life a bannisht man should be,
Than that the holy Capitol me reigning there should see
Thus much he said: and by and by toogither he did call
The people and the Senators, But yet he first of all
Did hyde his horns with Laurel leaves: and then without the wall*
He standing on a mount the which his men had made of soddes,
And having after ancient guyse made prayer too the Gods
Said: here is one that shall (unless ye bannish him your town
Immediately) be king of Room and wear a royal crown.
What man it is, I will by sign, but not by name bewray.
He has upon his brow two horns. The wizard here does say,
That if he enter Room, you shall like servants him obey.
He might have entered at your gates which open for him lay,
But I did stay him thence. And yet there is not unto me
A nearer friend in all the world. Howbeet forbid him ye
O Romans that he comme not once within your walls. Or if
He have deserved, bynd him fast in fetters like a thief.
Or in this fatal Tyrants death, of fear dispatch your mind.
Such noise as Pynetrees make what time the heady eastern wind
Does whiz amongst them, or as from the sea does far rebound:
Even such among the folk of Room that present was the sound.
Howbeet in that confused roar of fearful folk, did fall
Out one voice asking, whoo is he? And staring therewithal
Upon their foreheads, they did seek the foresaid horns. Again
(quod Cippus) lo, ye have the man for whom ye seek. And then
He pulld (against his peoples will) his garlond from his head,
And showed them the two fair horns that on his brows were spread.
At that the people dassheth down their looks and syghing, is
Right sorye (whoo would think it true?) too see that head of his
Most famous for his good deserts. Yet did they not forget
The honor of his personage, but willingly did set
The Laurel garlond on his head ageine. And by and by
The Senate said. Well Cippus, since until the time thou dye
Thou may not come within these walls, we give thee as much ground
In honor of thee, as a teeme of steeres can plough thee round,
Between the dawning of the day, and shetting in of night.
Moreover on the brazen gate at which this Cippus might
Have entered Room, a pair of horns were graude too represent
His woondrous shape, as of his deed an endless monument.
Ye Muses whoo too Poets are the present springs of grace,
Now show (for you know, neither are you dulld by time or space)
How Aesculapius in the I Will that is in Tyber deep
Among the sacred sayncts of Room had fortune for too creep.
A cruel plage did heertoofore infect the Latian air,
And peoples bodies pyning pale the murreine did appayre.
When tired with the burial of their friends, they did perceive
Themselves no help at man's hand nor by Physic too receive.
Then seeking help from heaven, they sent too Delphos (which does stand
Amid the world) for counsel too be had at Phoebus hand.
Beseeching him with helthfull aid too succor their distress,
And of the mighty City Room the mischeef too redress.
The quiuers which Apollo bright himself was wont too bear,
The Baytrees, and the place itself toogither shaken were.
And by and by the table from the furthest part of all
The Chau^cell spoke these words, which did their hearts with fear appal.
The thing ye Romans seek for here, ye should have sought more ny
Your country. Yea and nearer home go seek it now. Not I
Apollo, but Apollos son is he that must redress
Your sorrows. Take your journey with good handsell of success,
And fetch my son among you. When Apollos hest was told
Among the prudent Senators, they sercht what town did hold
His son, and unto Epidavvre a Gallye for him sent.
As Soon as that th'Ambassadour arrived there they went
Untoo the counsel and the Lords of Greekland: whom they pray
Too have the God the present plages of Romans for too stay,
And for themselves the Oracle of Phoebus forth they lay.
The Counsel were of sundry mynds and could not well agree.
Sum thought that succor in such need denied should not be.
And diverse did persuade too keep their help, and not too send
Their Gods away since they themselves might need them in the end.
While dowtfully they of and on debate this curious case,
The evening twylyght utterly the day away did chase,
And on the world the shadow of the earth had darkness brought.
That night the Lord Ambassador as sleep upon him wrought,
Did dream he saw before him stand the God whose help he sought,
In shape as in his chapel he was woonted for too stand,
With right hand stroking down his berd, and staff in toother hand,
And meekely saying: fear not, I will comme and leave my shryne.
This serpent which does wreath with knottes about this staff of mine
Mark well, and take good heed thereof: that when thou shall it see,
Thou may it know. For intoo it transformed will I be.
But bigger I will be. for I will seem of such a syse,
As may celestial bodies well too turn intoo suffice.
Straight with the voice, the God: and with the voice and God, away
Went sleep: and after sleep was gone ensewed cheerful day.
Next morning having clearly put the fyrye stars too flight,
The Lords not knowing what too do, assembled all foorthryght
Within the sumptuous temple of the God that was required,
And of his mind by heavenly sign sum knowledge they desired.
They scarce had done their prayers, when the God in shape of snake
With loftye crest of gold, began a hissing for too make,
Which was a warning given. And with his presence he did shake
The Altar, shryne, door, marble floor, and roof all laid with gold,
And vauncing up his breast he stayed right stately too behold
Amid the Church, and round about his fyrye eyes he rold.
The sight did fray the people. But the wyuelesse priest (whoose hear
Was trussed in a fair whyght Call) did know the God was there.
And said, behold tiz God, tiz God. As many as be here
Pray both with mouth and mind. O thou our glorious God, appear
Too our beehoofe, and help thy folk that keep thy hallowes right.
The people present woorshipped his Godhead there in sight,
Repeating dowble that the priest did say. the Romans eeke
Devoutly did with Godly voice and heart his ^auour seek.
The God by nodding did consent, and gave assured sign
By shaking of his golden crest that on his head did shine,
And hissed twyce with spirting tongue. Then trayld he down the fine
And glistring greeces of his church. And turning back his eyes,
He looked too his altarward and too his former shryne
And temple, as too take his leave and bid them all fare well
From thence right howge upon the ground (which sweet of flowers did smell
That people strewed in his way,) he passed stately down,
And bending intoo bowghts went through the heart of all the town,
Until that he the bowwing wharf beside the haven took.
Where s^aying, when he had (as seemd) dismissed with gentle look
His train of Chapleynes and the folk that waited on him thither,
He laid him in the Roman ship too sail away toogither.
The ship did feel the burden of his Godhed too the full,
And for the heavy weight of him did after pass more dull.
The Romans being glad of him, and having killd a steer
Upon the shore, vntyde their ropes and cables from the peer.
The lyghtsum wynd did drive the ship. The God auauncing hye,
And leaning with his neck upon the Gallyes side, did lie
And look upon the greenish waves, and cutting easly through
The Ionian sea with little gales of western wynd not rough,
The sixth day morning came upon the coast of Italy.
And passing forth by Iunos Church that mustreth too the eye
Upon the head of Lacine he was carried also by
