The rock of Scylley. then he left the land of Calabrye
And rowing softly by the rock Zephyrion, he did draw
Too Celen cliffs the which upon the ryghtsyde have a flawe.
By Romeche and by Cavvlon, and by Narice thence he past,
And from the streyghtes of Sicily gate quyght and clear at last.
Then ran he by the Aeolian Isles and by the metal mine
Of Tempsa, and by Levvcosye, and temprate Pest where fine
And pleasant Roses flourish ay. From thence by Capreas
And Atheney the headlond of Minerua he did pass
Too Surrent, where with gentle vynes the hills be ouerclad,
And by the town of Hercules and Stabye ill bestad
And Naples borne too Idleness, and Cumes where Sybell had
Her temples, and the scalding bathes, and Linterne where grows store
Of masticke trees, and Vulturne which bears sand apace from shore,
And Sinuesse where as Adders are as whyght as any snow,
And Minturne of infected air because it stands so low,
And Caiete where Aeneas did his nurce in tumbe bestow,
And Formy where Antiphates the Lestrigon did keep,
And Trache enuyrond with a fen, and Circes mountain steep:
Too Ancon with the boystous shore. As Soon as that the ship
Arrived here, (for now the sea was rough,) the God let slip
His circles, and in bending bowghts and wallowing waves did glyde
Intoo his fathers temple which was builded there beside
Upon the shore. and when the sea was calm and pacifyde,
The foresaid God of Epidavvre, his fathers Church forsook,
(The lodging of his nearest friend which for a time he took,)
And with his crackling scales did in the sand a furrowe cut,
And taking hold upon the stern did in the Galy put
his head, and rested till he came past Camp and Lauine sands,
And entered Tybers mouth at which the City Ostia stands.
The folk of Room came hither all by heaps both men and wives
And eeke the Nuns that keep the fire of Vesta as their lives,
Too meet the God, and welcomd him with joyful noise. And as
The Gally rowed up the stream, great store of incence was
On altars burnt on both the banks, so that on either side
The fuming of the frankincence the very air did hyde,
And also slain in sacrifyse full many cattle did.
Anon he came too Room the head of all the world: and there
The serpent lifting up himself, began his head too bear
Right up along the maast, upon the top whereof on hye
He looked round about, a meet abiding place too spy
The Tyber does deuyde itself in twain, and does embrace
A little pretye Island (so the people term the place)
From either side whereof the banks are distant equal space.
Apollos Snake descending from the maast conveyed him thither,
And taking eft his heavenly shape, as one repayring hither
Too bring our City healthfulnesse, did end our sorrows quyght.
Although too be a God with us admitted were this wight.
Yet was he borne a forreiner. But Caesar has obteynd.
His Godhead in his native soil and City where he reignd.
Whom peerless both in peace and war, not more his wars up knit
With triumph, nor his great exployts atcheeued by his wit,
Nor yet the great renown that he obteynd so speedely,
Have turned too a blazing star, than did his progeny.
For of the acts of Caesar, none is greater than that he
Left such a son behynd him as Augustus is, too be
His heir. For are they things more hard too ouercomme thy Realm
Of Britain standing in the sea? or up the seuenfold stream
Of Nyle that bears Paperreede victorious ships too row?
Or too rebelliouse Numidye too give an overthrow?
Or Iuba king of Moors, and Pons (which proudely did it bear
Upon the name of Mythridate) too force by sword and spear
Too yield them subjects unto Room? or by his just desert
Too merit many triumphs, and of sum too have his part?
Than such an heir too leave beehynd, in whom the Gods do show
Exceeding favor unto men for that they do bestow
So great a prince upon the world? Now too thentent that he
Should not be borne of mortal seed, the oother was too be
Canonyzed for a God. Which thing when golden Venus see,
(She also saw how dreadful death was for the bishop then
Prepaard, and how conspiracye was wrought by wicked men)
She looked pale. And as the Gods came any in her way,
She said unto them one by one. Behold and see I pray,
With how exceeding eagerness they seek me too betray,
And with what woondrous craft they strive too take my life away,
I meene the thing that only now remains unto me
Of Iule the Trojans race. Must I then only ever be
Thus vexed with undeserved cares? How seems now the pain
Of Diomeds spear of Calydon too wound my hand again?
How seems it me that Troy ageine is lost through ill defense?
How seems my son Aenaeas like a bannisht man, from thence
Too wander far ageine, and on the sea too tossed be,
And war with Turnus for too make? or rather (truth too say)
With Juno? what meene I about harms passed many a day
Against mine offspring, thus too stand? This present fear and woe
Permit me not too think on things now past so long ago.
Ye see how wicked swoordes against my head are whetted. I
Beseech ye keep them from my throat, and set the traitors by
Their purpose. neither suffer you dame Vestaas fire too dye
By murdering of her bishop. Thus went Venus woefully
Complaining over all the heaven, and moovde the Gods thereby.
And for they could not break the strong decrees of destinye,
They showed signs most manifest of sorrow too ensew.
For battles feyghting in the clouds with crasshing armor flew.
And dreadful trumpets sownded in the air, and horns eeke blew,
As warning men before hand of the mischeef that did brew.
And Phoebus also looking dim did cast a drowzy light.
Upon the earth, which seemd likewise too be in sorye plyght.
From underneath amid the stars brands often seemd burning bright
It often rayned drops of blood. The morning star looked blew,
And was bespotted here and there with specks of rusty hew.
The moon had also spots of blood. The Screeche owl sent from hell
Did with her tune unfortunate in every corner yell.
Salt tears from Iuory images in sundry places fell.
And in the Chappells of the Gods was singing heard, and words
Of threatening. Not a sacrifyse one sign of good auoordes.
But great turmoyle too be at hand their har^strings do declare.
And when the beast is ripped up the inwards headless are.
About the Court, and every house, and Churches in the nyhgts
The dogs did howl, and every where appeered ghastly spryghts.
And with an earthquake shaken was the town. Yet could not all
These warnings of the Gods dispoynt the treason that should fall,
Nor ouercomme the destinies. The naked swoordes were brought
Intoo the temple. For no place in all the town was thought
So meet too woork the mischeef in, or for them too commit
The heinous murder, as the Court in which they used too sit
In counsel. Venus then with both her hands her stomach smit,
And was about too hyde him with the clowd in which she hid
Aenaeas, when she from the sword of Diomed did him rid.
Or Paris, when from Menelay she did him saufe convey.
But Jove her father staying her did thus unto her say.
Why daughter mine, will thou alone be stryuing too prevent
Unuanquishable destiny? In faith and if thou went
Thy self intoo the house in which the fatal susters three
Do dwell, thou should there of brass and steel substantial see
The registers of things so strong and massye made too be,
That ^au^ and everlasting, they do neither stand in fear
Of thunder, nor of lyghtning, nor of any ruin there.
The destnyes of thine offspring thou shall there find graven deep
In Adamant. I red them: and in mind I do them keep.
And forbycause thou shall not beiquyght ignorant of all,
I will declare what things I marked herafter too befall.
The man for whom thou make suit, has lived full his time
And having run his race on earth must now too heaven up clyme.
Where thou shall make a God of him ay honored for too be
With temples and with Altars on the earth. Moreover he
That is his heir and bears his name, shall allalone sustain
The burden laid upon his back, and shall our help obtain
His fathers murder too revenge. The town of Mutinye
Beseedged by his power, shall yield. The feelds of Pharsaly
Shall feel him, and Philippos in the Realm of Macedonne
Shall once ageine be staynd with blood. The great Pompeius son
Shall vanquished be by him upon the sea of Sicilye.
The Roman Capteynes wife the Queen of Egypt through her hye
Presumption trusting too her match too much, shall threate in vain
Too make her Canop over our high Capitol too reign.
What should I tell thee of the wyld and barbrous nations that
At both the Oceans dwelling be? The universal plat
Of all the earth inhabited, shall all be his. The sea
Shall unto him obedient be likewise. And when that he
Has stablisht peace in all the world, then shall he set his mind
Too civil matters, vpryght laws by justice for too find,
And by example of himself all others he shall bynd.
Then having care of time too comme, and of posteritye,
A holy wife shall bear too him a son that may supply
His careful charge and bear his name. And lastly in the end
He shall too heaven among the stars his auncetors ascend,
But not before his life by length too drooping age do tend.
and therefore from the murthred corce of Julius Caesar take
^is soul with speed, and of the same a burning cressed make,
That from our heavenly palace he may evermore look down
Upon our royal Capitol and Court within Room town.
He scarcely ended had these words, but Venus out of hand
Amid the Senate house of Room invisible did stand,
And from her Caesar's body took his new expulsed spryght
The which she not permitting too resolve too air quyght,
Did place it in the sky among the stars that glister bright
And as she bare it, she did feel it gather heavenly might,
And for too wexen fyrye. She no sooner let it fly,
But that a goodly shyning star it up a lost did stye
And drew a great way after it bright beams like burning hear.
Whoo looking on his sons good deeds confessed that they were
Far greater than his own, and glad he was too see that he
Excelled him. Although his son in no wise would agree
Too have his deeds preferred before his fathers: yet does fame,
(Whoo ay is free, and bound too no command) withstand the same
And stryuing in that one behalf against his hest and will,
Proceeds too prefer his deeds before his fathers still.
Even so too Agamemnons great renown gives Atreus place
Even so Achilles deeds, the deeds of Peleus do abace.
Even so beyond Aegaeus far does Theseyes prowess go.
And (that I may examples use full matching these) even so
Is Saturn less in fame than Jove. Jove rules the heavenly spheres,
And all the tr^ple shaped world. And our Augustus bears
Dominion over all the earth. They both are fathers: They
Are rulers both. Ye Gods too whom both fire and sword gave way,
What time ye with Aenaeas came from Troy: ye Gods that were
Of mortal men canonyzed: Thou Qui^in whoo did reere
The walls of Room: and Mars whoo wart the valcant Quirins sire
And Vesta of the household Gods of Caesar with thy fire
Most holy: and thou Phoebus whoo with Vesta also art
Of household: and thou Jupiter whoo in the highest part
Of mountain Tarpey haste thy Church: and all ye Gods that may
With conscience sauf by Poets be appealed too: I pray
Let that same day be slow too comme and after I am dead,
In which Augustus (whoo as now of all the world is head)
Quyght giving up the care thereof ascend too heaven for ay
There (absent hence,) to favor such as unto him shall pray.
Now have I brought a woork too end which neither Jove's feerce wrath,
Nor sword, nor fire, nor freating age with all the force it has
Are able too abolish quyght. Let comme that fatal hour
Which (saving of this brittle flesh) has over me no power,
And at his pleasure make an end of mine uncertain time.
Yet shall the better part of me assured be too clyme
Aloft above the starry sky. And all the world shall never
Be able for too quench my name. For look how far so ever
The Roman Empire by the right of conquest shall extend,
So far shall all folk read this woork. And time without all end
(If Poets as by prophecy about the truth may ame)
My life shall everlastingly be lengthened still by fame.
Finis Libri decimi quinti. Laus and honor soli Deo.
IMPRINTED AT LONdon by Willyam Seres dwelling at the west end of Paul's
church, at the sign of the Hedgehogge.
